## Method used to arrive at a recommendation

The recommendaTons for the first draV of this guideline resulted from a collecTve decision reached by informal discussion by the authors and, whenever necessary, with input from the Chair of the Clinical PracTce Guidelines CommiXee. If no agreement had been reached on the appropriate grading of a recommendaTon, a vote would have been held and the majority opinion carried. However, this was not necessary for this guideline.

## Conflicts of Interest Statement

All authors made declaraTons of interest in line with the policy in the UK Kidney AssociaTon Clinical PracTce Guidelines Development Manual. Further details can be obtained on request from The UK Kidney AssociaTon.

## Grading the evidence

The evidence for these recommendaTons has been assessed using the modified GRADE system. (5) The modified GRADE system defines both the strength of the recommendaTons of the guideline authors and the level of evidence upon which each of the recommendaTons is based. This grading system classifies expert recommendaTons as 'strong' (Grade 1) or 'weak' (Grade 2) based upon the balance between the benefits and risks, burden and cost. The quality or level of evidence is designated as high (Grade A), moderate (Grade B), low (Grade C) or very low (D) depending on factors such as study design, directness of evidence and consistency of results. Grades of recommendaTon and quality of evidence may range from 1A to 2D.

Authors reviewed the evidence and came to a collecTve decision on the guidance, with input from the chair of the guidelines commiXee as needed.

## Acknowledgements

This document has been externally reviewed by key stake holders according to the process described in the Clinical PracTce Guidelines Development Policy Manual.

## Executive Summary

Anaemia is common in chronic kidney disease (CKD) encompassing non-dialysis dependent CKD (NDD-CKD) and dialysis dependent CKD (DD-CKD); people on peritoneal dialysis (PD) and haemodialysis (HD); and kidney transplant recipients (KTR).  Iron deficiency and erythropoieTn deficiency (a consequence of impaired oxygen sensing by the failing kidneys) are perhaps the most common causes of anaemia in people with CKD, especially those requiring kidney replacement therapy (KRT/dialysis).  The Renal NaTonal Service Framework and NaTonal InsTtute for Health and Clinical Excellence in the UK, and Kidney Disease Improving Global Outcomes (KDIGO), all advocate treatment of anaemia in people with CKD.  Blood transfusions are infrequently required, and newer therapies such as Hypoxia-Inducible Factor (HIF-PHI) stabilisers are now in current use. Anaemia is a major clinical problem in people suffering from CKD. This guideline adds to the previous 2017 and updated 2020 anaemia guidelines. Specifically, we aim to:

1. Provide guidance on the use of iron in people with NDD-CKD and DD-CKD with added informaTon from several RCTs.
2. Comment on iron deficiency without anaemia in people with CKD.
3. Provide further informaTon on anaemia management in people with a transplant.
4. Provide guidance in the use of the new HIF-PHI drugs in people with NDD-CKD and comment on use in DD-CKD.
5. Comment on the potenTal new therapies to be aware of for future.
6. Support safe implementaTon of therapies.
7. Update audit and research recommendaTons.

We offer evidence based graded pracTce guidance covering appropriate use of HIF-PHI in people with CKD, accompanied by recommendaTons for implementaTon, clinical research and audits.

I am enormously grateful to all the members of the guideline working group for volunteering so generously their Tme and experTse in revising and further developing this guideline. I would like to parTcularly thank my NIHR fellow Dr SebasTan Spencer for his efforts as a NIHR trainee.

## Prof Sunil Bhandari

Chair of Guideline Working Group

## Introduction

This 2024 clinical pracTce guideline provides updated recommendaTons on the management of Anaemia of Chronic Kidney Disease (ACKD) including people receiving kidney replacement therapy and serves to supersede all previously published UK guidelines. RecommendaTons have been graded using the modified GRADE system to indicate both the strength of each recommendaTon (strong or weak) and level of evidence for the recommendaTon (A-D) (1, 2) . The UK Kidney AssociaTon (UKKA) endorses the NICE Guideline for anaemia management in Chronic Kidney Disease (CKD) 2021 (3) .

This 2024 guideline update covers the management of anaemia in adults, children and young people with anaemia associated with CKD. While there is no universally accepted classificaTon for categorising the populaTon with anaemia of CKD by age, this guideline adopts the classificaTon set out in NICE Guideline (4) defined as follows:

- children: 0-13 years

- young people: 14-17 years

- adults: 18 years and over

This guideline has reviewed the addiTonal evidence from July 2016 using systemaTc literature searches to idenTfy all published clinical evidence relevant to the review quesTons. Databases were searched for all published papers between July 2016 and May 2023, using relevant medical subject headings, free-text terms and study-type filters where appropriate. All searches were conducted in MEDLINE, PUBMED, Embase, and The Cochrane Library. Data search used the following search terms:

- Anaemia and CKD and the US equivalent terms for 'anemia'
- Anaemia and dialysis
- Anaemia and transplantaTon
- Anaemia and end stage kidney disease and equivalents
- Blood transfusion and dialysis
- ErythropoieTn, EPO, ESA
- ESA Resistance
- FerroporTn
- Hepcidin
- Hypoxia inducible factor prolyl hydroxylase
- Hypoxia inducible factor inducers
- hypoxia inducible factor inhibitors, HIF-PHI
- Immunosuppression and anaemia
- Immunosuppression and EPO
- Immunosuppression and blood transfusion
- Iron anaphylaxis
- Iron deficiency
- Iron hypophosphataemia
- Iron therapy
- Iron sensiTvity
- Iron toxicity
- SGLT2 inhibitors and anaemia
- SGLT2 inhibitors and ErythropoieTn
- Pure red cell aplasia

<!-- image -->

- Renal anaemia
- Renal transplant and anaemia
- Renal transplant and blood transfusion
- Renal transplant and EPO

Previous guidelines covered the periods prior to the above dates. ArTcles not wriXen in English were not assessed. ArTcles available in abstract forms, leXers, case reports, editorials or review arTcles were also excluded. ArTcles were assessed for relevance to the guideline topic, eligibility for inclusion in the evidence base for that guideline and methodological quality.

ArTcles were considered of parTcular relevance if they were describing:

- ProspecTve randomised or quasi-randomised trials
- Controlled trials
- Meta-analysis of several trials
- Cochrane systemaTc reviews

Where evidence was available from the above sources, recommendaTons were based on these publicaTons. Where there was a lack of evidence from high-quality studies, recommendaTons were based on current consensus from the guideline commiXee's experience and opinion of what consTtutes good pracTce and that was made clear in the document:

We also reviewed all related guidelines including those listed below:

- European Renal Best PracTce (ERBP) for Anaemia in CKD (5,6)
- Kidney Disease Outcomes Quality IniTaTve (KDOQI) Guidelines for Management of anaemia in CKD (7)
- Kidney Disease Improving Global Outcomes (KDIGO) Clinical PracTce Guidelines for Anaemia in Chronic Kidney Disease (8)
- The NaTonal InsTtute for Health and Care Excellence (NICE) guidelines (ng203) (3)

## Background

Anaemia is a common complicaTon of CKD. It is associated with leV ventricular dysfuncTon and heart failure, in addiTon to a reducTon in exercise capacity and quality of life.

The recent 25 th UK Renal Registry report (2021 data) shows that 53.8% of people receiving KRT had a Hb &lt;100 g/L. The median haemoglobin (Hb) for kidney transplant recipients, people on peritoneal dialysis (PD) and haemodialysis was 107, 106 and 94 g/L respecTvely (9).

Indeed, over 20% of people with DD-CKD have a Hb &lt;90 g/L. In the UK, including Scotland, 20.3% of people receiving in-centre HD had a Hb &lt;100 g/L and 20.9% had a Hb &gt;120 g/L; 13.3% had a ferritin &lt;200 µg/L. The 25 th UK Renal Registry report (2021 data) also shows that 22.1% of people receiving PD in the UK had a Hb &lt;100 g/L and 24.2% had a Hb &gt;120 g/L. 18.8% had a ferriTn &lt; 100 µg/L.

Iron therapies, Erythropoiesis STmulaTng Agents (ESAs) and more recently Hypoxia Inducible Factor inhibitors (HIF-PHI) have allowed improved anaemia management in people with CKD (including those on kidney replacement therapy).

<!-- image -->

<!-- image -->

Hepcidin modulators and ferroporTn acTvator data remains preliminary and none of these agents have received a UK markeTng authorisaTon at the Tme of publicaTon of this guideline.

## Summary of Clinical Practice Guidelines on Anaemia of Chronic Kidney Disease

## 1. Assessment for Anaemia of CKD (Guidelines 1.1 - 1.5)

## Guideline 1.1 - Evalua9on of Chronic Anaemia - Screening for Anaemia:

We suggest that haemoglobin (Hb) levels should be rouTnely measured to screen for anaemia:

- At least annually in people with CKD G3
- At least twice a year in people with CKD G4-5 not on kidney replacement therapy (2B)

## Guideline 1.2 - Evalua9on of Anaemia - Haemoglobin Level:

We recommend that all people with anaemia associated with chronic kidney disease should be invesTgated for the cause and possible treatment, irrespecTve of the grade of kidney disease or requirement for kidney replacement therapy if:

- Their haemoglobin (Hb) levels are less than 110 g/L (less than 105 g/L if younger than 2 years), or they develop symptoms aXributable to anaemia. This is to ensure the correct diagnosis and management of anaemia. (1A)

## Guideline 1.3 - Evalua9on of Anaemia - Kidney Func9on:

We suggest that CKD should be considered as a possible cause of anaemia when the glomerular filtraTon rate (GFR) is &lt; 60 ml/min/1.73m 2 . It is more likely to be the cause if the GFR is &lt;30ml/min/1.73m 2 (&lt; 45 ml/min/ 1.73m 2 in people with diabetes) and no other cause, e.g., blood loss, folic acid or vitamin B12 deficiency, is idenTfied . (2B)

## Guideline 1.4 - Evalua9on of Anaemia - Erythropoie9n Measurement:

We recommend that measurement of erythropoieTn levels should NOT rouTnely be considered for the diagnosis or management of anaemia for people with CKD. (1A)

## Guideline 1.5 - Evalua9on of Anaemia - Baseline Inves9ga9ons:

We recommend that iniTal clinical and laboratory evaluaTon of anaemia should be performed prior to iniTaTon of treatment for anaemia in people with CKD. (1A)

## Guideline 1.5.1

We recommend that laboratory evaluaTon should include the following tests (1B):

- Full blood count (FBC) in addiTon to the Hb concentraTon
- Red blood cell indices:
- Mean corpuscular haemoglobin [MCH]
- Mean corpuscular volume [MCV]
- Mean corpuscular haemoglobin concentraTon [MCHC])
- White blood cell count and differenTal count
- Platelet count
- Absolute reTculocyte count to assess bone marrow responsiveness (if indicated)

## Tests to Determine Iron Status:

- Percentage of hypochromic red blood cells (% HRC), but only if processing of blood sample is possible within 6 hours or
- ReTculocyte Hb count (CHr) or equivalent tests e.g., reTculocyte Hb equivalent (RET-He) or
- CombinaTon of transferrin saturaTon (TSAT) and serum ferriTn if the above tests are not available or the person has thalassemia or thalassemia trait

- Serum ferriTn to assess iron stores
- Plasma/serum C-reacTve protein (CRP) to assess possible inflammaTon

## Guidelines 1.5.2

Based on iniTal assessment, we recommend the following tests to diagnose the cause of anaemia in selected cases (1B):

- Serum B12 and serum folate concentraTons
- Tests for haemolysis (plasma/serum levels of haptoglobin, lactate dehydrogenase, bilirubin, Coombs' test)
- Plasma/serum and/or urine protein electrophoresis
- Hb electrophoresis
- Free light chains and bone marrow examinaTon

## 2. Treatment of Anaemia of CKD with Iron (Guidelines 2.1 - 2.7)

## Guideline 2.1 - Treatment of Anaemia with Iron therapy - Iron Reple9on:

We recommend that people should be iron replete to achieve and aim to maintain target Hb range, whether receiving ESA or HIF-PHI or not. (1B)

## Iron repleTon is usually defined as:

- %HRC &lt;6% / CHr or RET-He &gt;31 pg / ferriTn and TSAT (&gt;100 microgram/L and &gt;20%) in people with non-dialysis dependent CKD (NDD-CKD) or on PD and &gt;200 microgram/L in people on HD. (2B)
- A target ferriTn level greater than 100 microgram/L for children with CKD on dialysis as well as children with CKD not on ESA or HIF-PHI therapy. (not graded)
- In ensuring iron repleTon, iron should be considered when ferriTn is &lt;500 mcg/L and/or the TSAT &lt;30%.

## Guideline 2.2 - Treatment of Anaemia with Iron Therapy - Ini9a9on of ESA/HIF-PHI and Iron Status:

We recommend that people on ESA or HIF-PHI therapy should remain iron replete before and during therapy either with oral iron or intravenous iron. (1B)

## Guideline 2.2.1

We recommend that ESA or HIF-PHI therapy should not be iniTated in the presence of absolute iron deficiency (ferriTn &lt;100 mcg/L in NDD-CKD and &lt;200 mcg/L in DD-CKD) or funcTonal Iron deficiency (TSAT &lt;20% will normal or elevated ferriTn levels) unTl this is corrected and anaemia persists. Iron supplements should be given prior to or when iniTaTng ESA or HIF-PHI therapy. (1B)

## Guideline 2.2.2

We suggest that to define funcTonal iron deficiency (FID) ('iron restricted erythropoiesis'), a TSAT &lt;20% and a normal or elevated ferriTn in people with NDD-CKD or maintained on PD and in those receiving HD be used. Normal or high serum ferriTn values do not exclude iron deficiency, as it could be due to other causes such as infecTon or inflammaTon. (2B)

## Guideline 2.3 - Treatment of Anaemia with Iron Therapy - Route of Administra9on:

We suggest that for people with CKD not requiring HD, or people currently receiving PD who are being considered for ESA therapy, intravenous iron should be considered to reduce ESA dose requirements. (2B)

## Guideline 2.3.1

We suggest for people with NDD-CKD or maintained on PD (i.e. not requiring HD), the choice between oral vs. parenteral iron depends on a shared decision and should include the impact of the

<!-- image -->

<!-- image -->

severity of iron deficiency, the previous response and side effects, the availability of venous access and the need to iniTate ESA or HIF-PHI therapy. (2A)

## Guideline 2.3.2

We recommend that most people receiving haemodialysis will require IV iron. (1A)

## Guideline 2.3.3

We suggest when offering IV iron therapy to people not receiving in-centre HD, consider high dose, low frequency (HiD/LF) IV iron as the treatment of choice for adults and young people when trying to achieve iron repleTon, considering all of the following (2B):

- Availability of venous access
- Preferences of the person with anaemia of CKD or, where appropriate, their family or carers
- Nursing and administraTon costs
- Cost of local drug supply
- Provision of resuscitaTon faciliTes

## Guideline 2.3.4

We suggest the frequency of administraTon of HiD/LF IV iron for people with CKD not requiring HD and for people receiving PD needs to be tailored for each person as there are no clear data. (not graded)

## Guideline 2.4 - Treatment of Anaemia with Iron Therapy - Upper Limit for Iron Therapy:

For people not on HD, we recommend that serum ferriTn should not exceed 600 mcg/L in those treated with iron. To achieve this, iron management should be reviewed when ferriTn is &gt;500 mcg/L, recognising that a level of &gt;800 mcg/L may reflect iron toxicity. (1B)

## Guideline 2.4.1

For people receiving HD, we recommend that proacTve high-dose IV iron, iniTally 600 mg in divided doses in the first month and then 400 mg every month (or equivalent), should be given unless ferriTn &gt;700 mcg/L or TSAT &gt;40%. (1A)

## Guideline 2.4.2

We suggest a tailored approach by clinicians to assess the risk and benefit of starTng a proacTve IV iron protocol in people who have been established on HD for &gt;12 months. (2B)

## Guideline 2.5 - Treatment of Iron Deficiency without Anaemia:

We recommend for people with CKD with iron deficiency without anaemia and concomitant heart failure, administraTon of IV iron to improve well-being, physical funcTon and to reduce heart failure admissions. (1A)

## Guideline 2.5.1

We suggest for people with CKD with iron deficiency but without anaemia and no concomitant heart failure, a trial of oral or IV iron for improvement in clinical symptoms such as restless legs. (2B)

## Guideline 2.6 - Administra9on of IV Iron - Safety Recommenda9ons:

We recommend that resuscitaTve medicaTon and personnel trained to evaluate and resuscitate be present in the event of anaphylaxis at each administraTon of intravenous iron. (1A)

## Guideline 2.6.1

We recommend avoiding parenteral iron therapy in people with acTve infecTon. (1C)

Guideline 2.6.2 We suggest avoiding iron therapy in people with the following condiTons (not graded):

- HepaTTs C virus infecTon (PosiTve PCR test)
- Haemochromatosis

Guideline 2.6.3 We suggest cauTon in prescribing parenteral iron in people with (not graded):

- Chronic liver disease
- Heterozygous for any of the haemochromatosis genes (C282Y (c.845G&gt;A), H63D (c.187C&gt;G) and S65C (c.193A&gt;T).

## Guideline 2.6.4

We suggest choice of parenteral iron be tailored, considering the benefits and risks associated with available iron preparaTons. (2C)

## Guideline 2.7 - Monitoring of Treatment - Iron Therapy:

We recommend regular monitoring of iron status (every 1-3 months) in people receiving intravenous iron to avoid toxicity (defined as a serum ferriTn of &gt; 800 mcg/L or TSAT &gt;40%). (1B)

## Guideline 2.7.1

We recommend that a serum ferriTn consistently &gt;800 mcg/L with no evidence of inflammaTon (normal CRP) is suggesTve of iron overload or potenTal toxicty. (1B)

## 3. Treatment of Anaemia with Erythropoiesis Stimulating Agents (Guidelines 3.13.17)

## Guideline 3.1- Inves9ga9ons Before Ini9a9ng ESA Therapy:

We recommend that all correctable causes of anaemia should be ruled out before considering treatment with ESAs . (1B)

## Guideline 3.2 - Treatment of Anaemia with Iron Therapy - Ini9a9on of ESA and Iron Status:

We recommend that ESA therapy should NOT be iniTated in the presence of absolute iron deficiency, (ferriTn &lt;100 mcg/L in people with non-dialysis dependent CKD (NDD-CKD) and &lt;200 mcg/L in people who are dialysis dependent) unTl this is corrected and it is determined that anaemia persists, in conjuncTon with a shared decision of the advantages and risks of ESA therapy. In people with funcTonal iron deficiency, iron supplements should be given prior to or when iniTaTng ESA therapy. (1B)

## Guideline 3.3 - Treatment of Anaemia - Erythropoiesis S9mula9ng Agents:

We recommend that treatment with ESAs should be offered to people with anaemia of CKD, in conjuncTon to a shared decision of the advantages and risks, who are likely to benefit in terms of quality of life and physical funcTon and to avoid blood transfusion; especially in people considered suitable for transplantaTon. (1B)

## Guideline 3.4 - Treatment of Anaemia with ESA therapy - Target Haemoglobin Range:

We recommend that people with non-dialysis dependent CKD (NDD-CKD) or those receiving dialysis, who are on ESA therapy, should achieve Hb between:

- 100 and 120 g/L in adults, young people and children aged 2 years and older . (1B)
- 95 and 115 g/L in children younger than 2 years of age (reflecTng the lower normal range in that age). (2B)

<!-- image -->

<!-- image -->

## Guideline 3.5 - Treatment of Anaemia without ESA Therapy - Target Haemoglobin Range:

We suggest that this Hb target range applies exclusively to people with CKD receiving ESAs and is not intended to apply to the treatment of iron deficiency in people receiving iron therapy without the use of ESAs. (2B)

## Guideline 3.6 - Treatment of Anaemia - Choice of ESA:

We recommend that the choice of ESA is based on local availability and cost of ESAs. (1B)

## Guideline 3.7 - Treatment of Anaemia - Ini9al ESA Dose:

We suggest that the iniTal ESA dose should be determined by the individual's Hb level, the desired target Hb range, the observed rate of increase in Hb level and clinical circumstances. (2B)

## Guideline 3.8 - Treatment of Anaemia with ESA Therapy - Route of Administra9on:

We suggest that the route of ESA administraTon should be determined by the CKD grade, individual recipient preference, treatment seLng, efficacy, safety and class of ESA used. Subcutaneous (SC) use is preferable in people who are not receiving HD to avoid puncture of peripheral veins. Subcutaneous administraTon of short-acTng ESA preparaTons may be preferred in people receiving haemodialysis due to improved ESA efficacy at the EPO receptor, allowing for smaller doses to be used. However, convenience and choice may favour intravenous (IV) administraTon in people on HD. (2B)

## Guideline 3.9 - Treatment of Anaemia with ESA Therapy - Frequency of Administra9on:

We suggest that the frequency of administraTon should be determined by the CKD grade, person preference, treatment seLng and class of ESA. Less frequent administraTon using long-acTng ESAs may be the treatment of choice in people with CKD not on haemodialysis. (2B)

## Guideline 3.10 - Treatment of Anaemia with ESA Therapy - ESA Dose Adjustments:

We recommend that adjustments to ESA doses should be considered when Hb is &lt;105 or &gt;115 g/L in adults, young people and children aged 2 years and older (1A)

## Guideline 3.10.1

We suggest these thresholds for intervenTon should achieve a populaTon distribuTon centered on a mean of 110 g/L with a range of 100-120 g/L. (2B)

## Guideline 3.10.2

We suggest in children younger than 2 years to keep the Hb level within the aspiraTonal range, do not wait unTl Hb levels are outside the aspiraTonal range before adjusTng treatment (for example, take acTon when Hb levels are within 5 g/L of the range's limits). (not graded)

## Guideline 3.10.3

We suggest that ESA doses should ideally be decreased rather than withheld when a downward adjustment of Hb level is desirable. (2B)

## Guideline 3.11 - Treatment of Anaemia with ESA Therapy - Specific Situa9ons:

We suggest that ESA administraTon in ESA-dependent people should conTnue during acute illness, surgical procedure or any other cause of hospitalisaTon, unless there is a clear contra-indicaTon (for example, acute stroke or vascular access thrombosis). (2B)

## Guideline 3.12 - Cau9on in Prescribing ESA in Certain People with CKD:

We suggest exerTng cauTon while prescribing ESA therapy in people with CKD with a history of stroke, or malignancy, parTcularly in those with acTve malignancy when cure is the anTcipated outcome. (2C)

<!-- image -->

## Guideline 3.13 - Monitoring of ESA Treatment - Haemoglobin during ESA Therapy:

We suggest that Hb concentraTon should be monitored every 2-4 weeks in the correcTon phase or aVer a dose adjustment and every 1-3 months for stable individuals in the maintenance phase of ESA treatment. More frequent monitoring will depend on clinical circumstances. (2B)

## Guideline 3.14 - Monitoring of ESA Treatment - Resistance to ESA Therapy:

We recommend that inadequate response ('resistance') to ESA therapy is defined as failure to reach the target Hb range despite SC epoeTn dose &gt;300 IU/kg/week (450 IU/kg/week IV epoeTn), or darbepoeTn dose &gt;1.5 mcg/kg/week, or equivalent dose of methoxy ethylene glycol epoeTn beta following invesTgaTon and treatment of other causes. (1A)

## Guideline 3.14.1

We suggest that clinicians consider accepTng lower aspiraTonal haemoglobin target ranges in those on high, escalaTng ESA doses with inadequate response, or consider alternaTve therapy such as a trial use of HIF-PHI. (2C)

## Guideline 3.15 - Monitoring of ESA Treatment - Evalua9on for ESA Induced Pure Red Cell Aplasia:

We recommend that a diagnosis of ESA induced pure red cell aplasia (PRCA) should be considered whenever a person receiving long term ESA therapy (&gt;8 weeks) develops all the following (1A):

- A sudden decrease in Hb concentraTon at the rate of 5 to 10 g/L per week or requirement of transfusions at the rate of approximately 1 to 2 per week
- Normal platelet and white cell counts
- Absolute reTculocyte count less than 10,000/µl
- High serum ferriTn level

## Guideline 3.15.1

We recommend that all ESA therapy should be stopped in people who develop ESA-induced PRCA. (1A)

## Guideline 3.15.2

We recommend that individuals who remain transfusion dependent aVer withdrawing ESA therapy should be treated with immunosuppressant medicaTons guided by the level of anT EPO anTbodies. (1B)

## Guideline 3.15.3

We do not recommend rouTne screening for anT-erythropoieTn anTbodies among people with CKD regularly treated with ESAs. (1B)

## Guideline 3.16 - Monitoring of ESA treatment - Hypertension during ESA therapy:

We recommend that blood pressure should be monitored in all people receiving ESAs and, if present, hypertension be treated by volume removal and/or anThypertensive drugs. (1A)

## 4. Treatment of Anaemia with HIF-PHI Agents (Guidelines 4.1-4.9)

## Guideline 4.1 - Treatment of Anaemia - HIF-PHI Agents

We recommend that treatment with HIF-PHI agents should be offered  aVer iron repleTon, to people with symptomaTc anaemia (Hb &lt;105 g/L) of CKD (stages 3-5 (eGFR &lt;60 ml/min/1.73m 2 )) who are not receiving dialysis at the start of therapy and who are likely to benefit in terms of quality of life and

<!-- image -->

physical funcTon and to avoid blood transfusion; especially in people considered suitable for transplantaTon. (1B)

## Guideline 4.1.1 - Treatment of Anaemia - DD-CKD and HIF-PHI Agents

We suggest that that treatment with HIF-PHI agents should be considered aVer iron repleTon, to people with DD-CKD and symptomaTc anaemia (Hb &lt;105 g/L) who are likely to benefit in terms of quality of life and physical funcTon and to avoid blood transfusion; especially in people considered suitable for transplantaTon. (2B)

## Guideline 4.1.2 - Treatment of Anaemia - People intolerant to ESA

We suggest that that treatment with HIF-PHI agents should be considered, aVer iron repleTon, to people who are intolerant to ESA therapy. (2C)

## Guideline 4.1.3 - Choosing between ESA and HIF-PHI therapy for people with non-dialysis dependent CKD and DD-CKD

We suggest, when deciding between ESA and HIF-PHI therapy for people with non-dialysis dependent CKD or DD-CKD, consideraTons include the preference of the person with anaemia of CKD, or, where appropriate, their family or carers, the cost of local drug supply, nursing and administraTon costs and previous treatment with ESA or HIF-PHI. (2B)

## Guideline 4.2 - Treatment of Anaemia

We suggest that HIF-PHI administraTon in HIF-PHI-dependent people should conTnue during acute illness, surgical procedures or any other cause of hospitalisaTon, unless there is a clear contraindicaTon such as accelerated hypertension or thrombosis. (2C)

## Guideline 4.3 - Treatment of Anaemia with HIF- PHI therapy - Target Haemoglobin:

We suggest that people over the age of 18 years with NDD-CKD on HIF-PHI therapy should achieve a Hb between:

- 100 and 120 g/L in adults and young people, similar to ESA therapy (2B)
- In children and those younger than 2 years of age no data is currently available (not graded)

## Guideline 4.3.1

We suggest that people over the age of 18 years with DD-CKD on HIF-PHI therapy should achieve a Hb between:

- 100 and 120 g/L in adults and young people similar to ESA therapy but this may depend on the choice of HIF-PHI where a Hb of 100-110 g/|L is recommended. (2B)
- In children and those younger than 2 years of age no data is currently available

## Guideline 4.4 - Treatment of Anaemia - Ini9al HIF-PHI dose

We recommend that the iniTal HIF-PHI dose should be based on the person's weight, Hb level and the observed rate of increase in Hb level, and clinical circumstances. The appropriate dose should follow label recommendaTons unTl further data is available. (1B)

## Guideline 4.4.1

We suggest that the starTng HIF PHI dose should be lower for those who are ESA-naïve versus those who are not. (2B)

<!-- image -->

## Guideline 4.5 - Treatment of Anaemia with HIF-PHI therapy - Frequency of Administra9on:

We suggest that the frequency of administraTon should be determined by the response to therapy to maintain the desired Hb target range of 100-120 g/L. (2B)

## Guideline 4.6 - Treatment of Anaemia with HIF-PHI Therapy - Dose Adjustments:

We suggest that adjustments to HIF-PHI doses should be considered when Hb is &lt;105 or &gt;115 g/L in adults to balance the benefit and safety to people given the current evidence base. (2B)

## Guideline 4.7 - Prescribing HIF-PHI in Sub-Groups of People with CKD:

We recommend that HIF-PHI should be avoided or used cauTously in people with acTve malignancy. (1B)

## Guideline 4.7.1

We recommend that HIF-PHI should be avoided or used cauTously in people with autosomal dominant polycysTc kidney disease unTl further data are available. (1D)

## Guideline 4.7.2

We recommend that HIF-PHI should be avoided or used cauTously in people with a history of seizures. (1B)

## Guideline 4.7.3

We recommend cauTon in using HIF-PHI in people with uncontrolled hypertension . (1B)

## Guideline 4.7.4

We recommend cauTon in using HIF-PHI therapy in people with reTnopathy. (1D)

## Guideline 4.7.5

We recommend cauTon in people with a history of thromboTc events following the SmPC for contraindicaTons. (1A)

## Guideline 4.7.6

We suggest that HIF-PHI may be considered in people with hyporesponsiveness to ESA therapy or underlying inflammaTon, but further high-quality randomised trials are needed to confirm its effecTveness. (2C)

## Guideline 4.8 - Safety of HIF-PHI:

We suggest the cauTous use of HIF-PHI in people with CKD and either known CVD or thromboTc events and consideraTon of lower dose regimes to reduce rapid rises in Hb. (2C)

## Guideline 4.9 - Monitoring Response to HIF-PHI:

We recommend that Hb levels should be monitored every two weeks unTl the desired Hb target range of 100 to 120 g/L is achieved and stabilised, and every 4 weeks thereaVer, or as clinically indicated. (1B)

<!-- image -->

## 5. Treatment of Anaemia of CKD with Blood Transfusions (Guidelines 5.1 - 5.1.3)

## Guideline 5.1 Blood Transfusion in People with Anaemia of CKD:

We recommend that in people with anaemia of CKD, especially those in whom kidney transplantaTon is an opTon, red blood cell transfusion should be avoided if possible, to minimise the risk of allosensiTsaTon. (1A)

## Guideline 5.1.1

We recommend that if red blood cell transfusion becomes essenTal (usually in the seLng of acute blood loss, acute haemolysis or severe sepsis), transfusion should be based on policies set by local transfusion guidelines rather than Hb target range for ESA therapy in anaemia of CKD. (1B)

## Guideline 5.1.2

We suggest using single unit transfusion, where possible, for stable non-bleeding people with CKD who clinically require transfusion. (2B)

## Guideline 5.1.3

We suggest that kidney transplant recipients, those on the transplant waiTng list or people on immunosuppressive therapy should receive only hepaTTs E negaTve blood components (all UK blood components are tested), but neither CMV negaTve nor irradiated blood is required. (2B)

## 6. Management of Peri-Transplant and Post-Transplant Anaemia (Guidelines 6.16.3.3)

## Guideline 6.1 - Peri-Transplant Anaemia:

We suggest that anaemia management should be opTmal pre-transplant in all people with CKD on the transplant wait-list, to minimise the risk of a post-transplant transfusion. (2B)

## Guideline 6.1.1

We suggest that for people with CKD undergoing kidney transplantaTon, ESA therapy may be conTnued, aVer a shared decision with the paTent taking account of risks and benefits, unTl endogenous EPO producTon is sufficient to maintain Hb concentraTons. For HIF-PHI therapy we make no recommendaTon unTl further data are available. (2C)

## Guideline 6.2 - Blood Transfusion:

We suggest for stable non-bleeding individuals who clinically require a red cell transfusion, use single unit transfusion, where possible. (2B)

## Guideline 6.3 - Post-Transplant Anaemia:

We suggest that consideraTon is given to idenTfy (and correct) reversible transplant-specific causes of anaemia. (2B)

## Guideline 6.3.1

We recommend that the treatment guidelines for anaemia in kidney transplant recipients should be similar to those for people with CKD not on dialysis (NDD-CKD). (1B)

## Guideline 6.3.2

We suggest for stable non-bleeding individuals who clinically require a red cell transfusion, use single unit transfusion, where possible. (2B)

## Guideline 6.3.3

We suggest the use of HIF-PHI therapy in transplant recipients should be as in people with NDD-CKD. (2B)

## 7.  Special Populations (Guidelines 7.1-7.3)

## Guideline 7.1 - Malignancy:

We suggest treaTng symptomaTc anaemia in people with CKD and cancer when Hb &lt;100 g/L. (2B)

## Guideline 7.2 - Haematological Disease:

We suggest ESA therapy be considered in people with myelodysplasTc syndrome (MDS), myeloma and others with CKD and haematological disease. We make no recommendaTons on HIF-PHI therapy. (not graded)

## Guideline 7.3 - Pregnancy:

We recommend that pregnant and post-partum women with CKD are given parenteral iron, if indicated, from the second trimester onwards, if the benefit is judged to outweigh the potenTal risk for the mother and fetus and according to SmPC guidance. (1C)

## Guideline 7.3.1

We recommend that ESA therapy during pregnancy be conTnued unless there is a major contraindicaTon (e.g., hypertension or thrombosis risk). (2C)

## Summary of Audit Measures on Anaemia of Chronic Kidney Disease

1. ProporTon of people starTng an ESA without prior measurement of serum ferriTn and TSAT (or % HRC or CHr or Ret-He).
2. ProporTon of people with non-dialysis dependent CKD with an eGFR &lt; 30 ml/min/1.73m 2 (CKDEPI) with an annual haemoglobin level.
3. ProporTon of people with non-dialysis dependent CKD stage 4-5 with Hb 100-120 g/L.
4. ProporTon of people with dialysis dependent CKD on Peritoneal Dialysis receiving iron therapy; type: oral vs. parenteral.
5. ProporTon of people with non-dialysis dependent CKD and people receiving PD who are iron replete.
6. ProporTon of people on HD who are iron replete.
7. ProporTon of people with non-dialysis dependent CKD with serum ferriTn &gt;600 mcg/L.
8. ProporTon of people on HD with a serum ferriTn &gt;700 mcg/L or TSAT &gt;40 %.
9. ProporTon of people with non-dialysis dependent CKD and heart failure and non-dialysis dependent CKD without anaemia who have iron parameters (ferriTn and TSATs) checked in the last 4 months.
10. ProporTon of people with non-dialysis dependent CKD and heart failure with iron deficiency but preserved haemoglobin treated with iron.
11. ProporTon of people on kidney replacement therapy (haemodialysis or peritoneal dialysis for more than 3 months) with Hb &lt; 100 g/L who are not prescribed an ESA.
12. ProporTon of people with non-dialysis dependent CKD with serum ferriTn levels &lt; 100 microgram/L, %HRC &gt;6% or a Chr/Ret-He &lt;31 pg at start of treatment with ESA or HIF-PHI.

<!-- image -->

<!-- image -->

13. ProporTon of people with dialysis dependent CKD with serum ferriTn levels &lt; 200 micrograms/L, %HRC &gt;6% or a Chr/Ret-He &lt;31 pg at start of treatment with ESA or HIF-PHI.
14. The proporTon of people with CKD treated with an ESA or HIF-PHI with Hb &gt; 120 g/L.
15. Mean (median) ESA dose in people with CKD maintained on ESA therapy.
16. Mean (median) HIF-PHI dose in people with CKD maintained on HIF-PHI therapy.
17. Prevalence of resistance or hyporesponsiveness to ESA among people receiving kidney replacement therapy.
18. The proporTon of people with anaemia associated with CKD treated with HIF-PHI requiring IV iron infusions.
19. ProporTon of people receiving HD who received a blood transfusion within the previous year.
20. ProporTon of people with advanced non-dialysis dependent CKD (eGFR &lt;30 ml/min/1.73m 2 ) who received a blood transfusion in the previous year.

## Summary of Research Recomendations

We recommend the following further research including, wherever possible, randomised trials:

1. RCT to evaluate the impact of IV versus oral iron in people on peritoneal dialysis.
2. RCT of IV iron on hard clinical outcomes in people with non-dialysis dependent CKD but without anaemia.
3. RCT of oral iron dosing in people with non-dialysis dependent CKD.
4. DeterminaTon of opTmal iron dosing and regimes for HIF-PHI based therapy.
5. Use of HIF-PHI in certain sub-groups such as DM, ADPKD, diabeTc reTnopathy, ESA hyporesponsiveness and allergy to IV iron.
6. Impact of HIF-PHI on quality of life and funcTonal capacity in people with CKD.
7. The safety and haemoglobin correcTon benefits of HIF-PH inhibitors in people with adult polycysTc kidney disease.
8. The safety and efficacy of HIF-PH inhibitors in people with a funcToning kidney transplant.
9. Whether HIF-PHI therapy is safe and beneficial in people with advanced heart failure.
10. Whether HIF-PHI therapy is safe and beneficial in people with systemic chronic inflammatory diseases affecTng the kidneys.
11. The safety and efficacy of HIF-PHI- therapy in young people and children under 18 years of age.
12. The factors that influence people with kidney disease and health professionals to choose between HIF-PHI and ESA therapies.
13. CombinaTon therapy of HIF-PHI with ESA to lower doses and improve outcomes.
14. Comparison of different HIF-PHI agents head to head.
15. Whether SGLT2-inhibitor therapy can safely prevent and correct anaemia in people with nondialysis dependent CKD.
16. The safety and efficacy of prospecTve therapies to treat anaemia in CKD; including AnTcalin proteins directed against hepcidin, and anT-IL-6 ligand anTbody, hepcidin monoclonal anTbody, Bone Morphogenic Protein 6 (BMP6) monoclonal anTbody and ferroporTn anTbody therapy.
17. Safety of HIF-PHI use in people with an underlying malignancy (acTve or recent).
18. Whether HIF-PHI use reduces the frequency of IV iron infusions in people with CKD.

## Rationale for Clinical Practice Guidelines on Anaemia of Chronic Kidney Disease

## 1. Assessment for Anaemia of CKD (Guidelines 1.1 - 1.5)

## Guideline 1.1 - Evalua9on of Chronic Anaemia - Screening for Anaemia:

We suggest that haemoglobin (Hb) levels should be rouTnely measured to screen for anaemia:

- At least annually in people with CKD G3
- At least twice a year in people with CKD G4-5 not on kidney replacement therapy (2B)

## Rationale

There is insufficient literature to suggest the ideal frequency of Hb tesTng in people with CKD who are not on ESA therapy. AlternaTvely, data from clinical trials have shown that the rate of Hb decline in these people is gradual (1, 2) . In a Canadian study to assess the effect of ESA therapy on leV ventricular mass in people with CKD (2) 172 people were assigned either to receive therapy with erythropoieTn α subcutaneously to maintain or achieve Hb level targets of 120 to 140 g/L, or to the control/delayed treatment group with mean Hb levels of 90 ± 5 g/L. During 2 years' follow up a significant proporTon of paTents eventually required ESA therapy. However, among those who did not require ESA therapy, mean Hb values remained relaTvely stable throughout the study period. Hb level should be measured at least monthly in CKD G5 haemodialysis paTents and every three months in CKD G5 peritoneal dialysis paTents.

KDIGO 2012 guidelines suggest measurement of Hb at least annually in paTents with NDD-CKD G3, at least twice per year in paTents with NDD-CKD G4-5 and at least every 3 months in paTents with CKD G5HD and CKD G5 PD. For those treated with an ESA, they recommend measuring Hb concentraTon when clinically indicated and at least every 3 months in paTents with NDD-CKD G3-5 and CKD G5 PD and at least monthly in paTents with CKD G5 HD (3) .

## References

1. Roger SD, McMahon LP, Clarkson A, et al. Effects of early and late intervenTon with epoeTn α on leV ventricular mass among paTents with chronic kidney disease (stage 3 or 4): Results of a randomized clinical trial. J Am Soc Nephrol 2004; 15:148-156.
2. Levin A, Djurdjev O, Thompson C, et al. Canadian randomized trial of haemoglobin maintenance to prevent or delay leV ventricular mass growth in paTents with CKD. Am J Kidney Dis 2005; 46:799-811.
3. KDIGO Clinical PracTce Guideline for Anaemia in Chronic Kidney Disease. Kidney Int Suppl 2012;2:279-33

## Guideline 1.2 - Evalua9on of Anaemia - Haemoglobin Level:

We recommend that all people with chronic anaemia associated with chronic kidney disease should be invesTgated for the cause and possible treatment, irrespecTve of the grade of kidney disease or requirement for kidney replacement therapy if:

<!-- image -->

- Their haemoglobin (Hb) levels are less than 110 g/L (less than 105 g/L if younger than 2 years), or they develop symptoms aXributable to anaemia. This is to ensure the correct diagnosis and management of anaemia. (1A)

## Rationale

The UK Kidney AssociaTon (UKKA) and Royal College of Physicians endorse the NICE Guidelines for Chronic Kidney Disease: Managing Anaemia (1) . The reader is referred to these guidelines as well as the European Renal Best PracTce (ERBP) for Anaemia in CKD (2,3) and the KDOQI (4) Guidelines for management of anaemia in CKD. The KDIGO website (www.kdigo.org) (5) is a useful site of reference for comparison of evidence-based guidelines internaTonally.

Anaemia is defined as having a Hb value below the established cut off defined by the World Health OrganisaTon (4) . Different defined groups have different cut offs. For adults:

- Men and postmenopausal women Hb &lt;130 g/L
- Premenopausal women Hb &lt;120 g/L
- Pregnant women Hb &lt;110 g/L

In 2006, KDOQI modified this definiTon by giving a single criterion for diagnosing anaemia in adult males (Hb &lt; 135 g/L, regardless of age) because the decrease in Hb among males aged &gt; 60 years is oVen aXributable to associated co-morbidiTes (4), while KDIGO suggest a diagnosis of anaemia in adults with CKD when the Hb concentraTon is &lt;130 g/L in males and &lt;120 g/L in females (5) .

Anaemia is defined as a haemoglobin concentraTon less than the 5th percenTle for age. Hb levels vary by age, and many laboratories use adult norms as references; therefore, the paTent's Hb level must be compared with age-based norms to diagnose anaemia (6) .

In addiTon to sex, age and pregnancy other factors influence Hb level including smoking, alTtude, race and geneTc disorders (thalassemia and sickle cell disease). In CKD, a person's anaemia should be defined using these same criteria. The degree of kidney impairment affects the likelihood of any person developing anaemia. Although current treatment with ESAs is not recommended unless Hb falls consistently below 110 g/L, other causes of anaemia should be excluded in persons with Hb below normal range. The current definiTon for anaemia applies to adult subjects older than 18 years, of all races and ethnic groups, and living at relaTvely low alTtude (&lt;1,000 m or 3,000 V.) (7) . With increasing alTtude, endogenous erythropoieTn producTon is increased; as a result, Hb concentraTon can be expected to increase by about 6 g/L in women and 9 g/L in men for each 1,000 m of alTtude above sea level (8) .

## References

1. NaTonal CollaboraTng Centre for Chronic CondiTons, Royal College of Physicians. Guideline on anaemia management in chronic kidney disease. 2015. NaTonal InsTtute for Clinical Health and Excellence. Available on hXp://www.nice.org.uk/guidance/NG8/evidence (accessed 15/06/2017).

<!-- image -->

2. Locatelli F, Aljama P, Barany P et al. Revised European Best PracTce Guidelines for the management of anaemia in paTents with chronic renal failure. Nephrol. Dial. Transplant. 2004; 19 Suppl 2: ii1-47.3.
3. Locatelli F, Bárány P, Covic A, et al. Kidney Disease: Improving Global Outcomes guidelines on anaemia management in chronic kidney disease: a European Renal Best PracTce posiTon statement. Nephrol Dial Transplant. 2013 Jun;28(6):1346-59.
4. KDOQI; NaTonal Kidney FoundaTon Clinical pracTce guidelines and clinical pracTce recommendaTons for anaemia in chronic kidney disease in adults. Am J Kidney Dis. 2006 May; 47(5 Suppl 3):S16-85.
5. KDIGO Clinical PracTce Guideline for Anaemia in Chronic Kidney Disease. Kidney Int Suppl 2012;2:279-335.
6. Oski FA, Brugnara C, Nathan DG. A diagnosTc approach to the anaemic paTent. In: Nathan and Oski's Haematology of Infancy and Childhood. 6th ed. Philadelphia, Pa.: Saunders; 2003:409-418

## Guideline 1.3 - Evalua9on of Anaemia - Kidney Func9on:

We suggest that CKD should be considered as a possible cause of anaemia when the glomerular filtraTon rate (GFR) is &lt; 60 ml/min/1.73m 2 . It is more likely to be the cause if the GFR is &lt;30ml/min/1.73m 2 (&lt; 45 ml/min/ 1.73m 2 in people with diabetes) and no other cause, e.g., blood loss, folic acid or vitamin B12 deficiency, is idenTfied. (2B)

## Rationale

The prevalence of anaemia in people with NDD-CKD increases as the GFR progressively falls (1) . NHANES III data demonstrate a prevalence of anaemia of 1%, 9% and 33% in people with NDD-CKD with an eGFR of 60, 30 and 15 ml/min/1.73m 2 respecTvely (2) . UK data of &gt; 112,000 unselected paTents in the general populaTon showed a populaTon prevalence of NDD-CKD G3-G5 of 4.9% (3) . In these people the prevalence of sex specific anaemia (&lt;120 g/L men: &lt; 110 g/L women) was 12%.

Anaemia is more prevalent among people with diabetes. In addiTon, anaemia of CKD develops earlier in people with diabetes compared with people who do not have diabetes (4-8) . In a crosssecTonal study involving over 800 people with diabetes, anaemia has been found to be two to three Tmes more prevalent in paTents with diabetes compared with the general populaTon at all levels of GFR (9) .

3. de Lusignan S, Chan T, Stevens P et al. IdenTfying paTents with chronic kidney disease from general pracTce computer records. Fam Pract 2005; 22: 234-241.

## Guideline 1.4 - Evalua9on of Anaemia - Erythropoie9n Measurement:

We recommend that measurement of erythropoieTn levels should NOT rouTnely be considered for the diagnosis or management of anaemia for people with CKD. (1A)

## Rationale

In kidney anaemia, serum erythropoieTn (EPO) levels are lower than appropriate for the degree of anaemia. In people with CKD and anaemia, erythropoieTn Ttres are not lower but may be equal to or even higher than in normal non-anaemic individuals (1-3) . Measurement of erythropoieTn level is very rarely helpful.

## References

1. Erslev AJ, Besarab A. The rate and control of baseline red cell producTon in haematologically stable paTents with uraemia. J Lab Clin Med 1995; 126(3):283-6.
2. Naets JP, Garcia JF, Tousaaint C, et al. Radioimmunoassay of erythropoieTn in chronic uraemia or nephric paTents. Scand J Haematol 1986;37:390-394.
3. Ross RP, McCrea JB, Besarab A. ErythropoieTn response to blood loss in haemodialysis paTents in blunted but preserved. ASAIO J 1994; 40:M880-M885.

## Guideline 1.5 - Evalua9on of Anaemia - Baseline Inves9ga9ons:

We recommend that iniTal clinical and laboratory evaluaTon of anaemia should be performed prior to iniTaTon of treatment for anaemia in people with CKD. (1A)

## Guideline 1.5.1

We recommend that laboratory evaluaTon should include the following tests (1B):

- Full blood count (FBC) including-in addiTon to the Hb concentraTon:
- Red blood cell indices:
- Mean corpuscular haemoglobin [MCH]
- Mean corpuscular volume [MCV]
- Mean corpuscular haemoglobin concentraTon [MCHC])
- White blood cell count and differenTal count

- Platelet count
- Absolute reTculocyte count to assess bone marrow responsiveness (if indicated)

## Tests to Determine Iron Status:

- Percentage of hypochromic red blood cells (% HRC), but only if processing of blood sample is possible within 6 hours or
- ReTculocyte Hb count (CHr) or equivalent tests e.g., reTculocyte Hb equivalent (RET-He) or
- CombinaTon of transferrin saturaTon (TSAT) and serum ferriTn if the above tests are not available or the person has thalassemia or thalassemia trait
- Serum ferriTn to assess iron stores
- Plasma/serum C-reacTve protein (CRP) to assess possible inflammaTon

## Guidelines 1.5.2

Based on the iniTal assessment we recommend in selected cases; the following tests may be useful to diagnose the cause of anaemia (1B):

- Serum B12 and serum folate concentraTons
- Tests for haemolysis (plasma/serum levels of haptoglobin, lactate dehydrogenase, bilirubin, Coombs' test)
- Plasma/serum and/or urine protein electrophoresis
- Hb electrophoresis
- Free light chains and bone marrow examinaTon

## Rationale

Although relaTve erythropoieTn deficiency is common among people with anaemia and CKD, other potenTal causes should be idenTfied or excluded. A clinical and laboratory evaluaTon of the cause of anaemia should precede iniTaTon of ESA therapy. The recommended laboratory evaluaTon aims at assessing:

- The degree and cause of anaemia,
- Bone marrow responsiveness, and
- Iron stores and iron availability for erythropoiesis.

Anaemia due to causes other than erythropoieTn deficiency should be suspected when:

- The severity of the anaemia is disproporTonate to the deficit in kidney funcTon,
- There is evidence of iron deficiency,
- There is evidence of haemolysis, or
- There is evidence of bone marrow disorder as manifest by leucopoenia and/or thrombocytopenia.

## a) Assessment of anaemia severity

In people with CKD not yet requiring dialysis and in those on peritoneal dialysis (PD), the Tming of the blood sample draw is not criTcal because plasma volume in these people remains relaTvely constant. In people receiving haemodialysis (HD) one issue remains to be clarified. Haemoglobin concentraTons are rouTnely measured in people receiving dialysis before dialysis. This potenTally leads to lower haematocrit values as a result of diluTon from fluid overload prior to ultrafiltraTon and an underesTmate to actual haemoglobin value.

InterdialyTc weight gain contributes to a decrease in Hb level, whereas intradialyTc ultrafiltraTon

<!-- image -->

<!-- image -->

leads to an increase in Hb level. Thus, a pre-dialysis sample underesTmates the euvolaemic Hb level, whereas a post dialysis sample over-esTmates the euvolaemic Hb. Indeed, changes on haematocrit can vary from the start to the end of dialysis by up to 6% depending on the volume of ultrafiltraTon. In a study of 68 stable people receiving HD receiving erythropoieTn subcutaneously, average mean pre-dialysis Hb was 10 g/L lower than average post dialysis Hb (1) . There was a strong linear inverse correlaTon between percentage of change in Hb and haematocrit (Hct) values and percentage of change in body weight. In another study of 49 stable HD paTents, among all pre-HD and post-HD Hb values, levels measured at the end of short dialysis intervals were closest to the mean Hb value of the week, derived from calculaTon of the area under the curve for the readings of the week (2) . In unit-based haemodialysis persons receiving thrice weekly dialysis, Hb monitoring performed prior to a mid-week haemodialysis session would minimise Hb variability due to the longer inter-dialyTc interval between the last treatment of one week and the first of the next.

## References

1. Movilli E, PerTca N, Camerini C et al. Pre dialysis versus post dialysis haematocrit evaluaTon during erythropoieTn therapy. Am J Kidney Dis 2002; 39:850-853.
2. Bellizzi V, Minutolo R, Terracciano V et al. Influence of the cyclic variaTon of hydraTon status on haemoglobin levels in haemodialysis paTents. Am J Kidney Dis 2002; 40:549-555.

## b) Assessment of Bone Marrow Responsiveness

In general, anaemia of CKD (anaemia of chronic disease) is normochromic and normocyTc and is morphologically indisTnguishable from the anaemia of chronic illness. IniTal assessment of anaemia in people with CKD should aim at idenTfying other factors that may influence the response to treatment.

- In addiTon to Hb, other indices of the full blood count (FBC) report may provide important clinical informaTon: Macrocytosis could be due to folate or vitamin B12 deficiency.
- In addiTon to anaemia of CKD, microcytosis could be due to iron deficiency or haemoglobinopathies.
- Macrocytosis with leucopenia or thrombocytopenia could be due to several factors such as alcohol intake, nutriTonal deficit (vitamin B12 or folate deficiency), or myelodysplasia.
- Serum folate is more prone to variaTon and can be affected by the person's diet immediately prior to blood being taken, alcohol, trauma and other factors therefore occasionally red cell folate may need to be measured where serum folate is equivocal.
- Haemolysis is suggested by the presence of macrocytosis, high lactate dehydrogenase and posiTve Coombs test.
- The normal absolute reTculocyte count ranges from 40,000 to 50,000 cells/μL. Although it has a significant inter-paTent variability, this test may be useful as a semi-quanTtaTve marker of erythropoieTc acTvity.

## c) Evalua9ng Iron Status in Anaemic People with CKD

The aim of evaluaTng iron status is to assess:

1. Iron level in Tssue stores and
2. The adequacy of iron uTlisaTon for erythropoiesis.

<!-- image -->

Serum ferriTn level is the only available blood marker of storage iron. There are several tests to assess adequacy of iron for erythropoiesis: TSAT, MCV, MCH, percentage of hypochromic red blood cells (HRC) and reTculocyte Hb content (CHr) or RET-He. Serum iron has not rouTnely been studied in people with CKD.

## Tests limita9ons

- HRC esTmaTon is a useful test for assessment of iron availability but is limited by the effect of sample storage Tme and need for special analysers. Long sample storage Tme (&gt; 6 hours) may spuriously increase HRC. Because a fresh sample is needed, this measure may not be pracTcal in rouTne clinical pracTce, especially in satellite dialysis paTents.
- If using percentage of hypochromic red blood cells from a fresh sample is not possible, reTculocyte Hb content (CHr) or RET-He could be a suitable alternaTve.
- If tesTng for CHr (or RET-He) is not feasible, it is preferable to test ferriTn and TSAT together because the combinaTon provides an important insight into erythropoiesis, iron storage and iron availability to bone marrow.
- Low serum ferriTn is diagnosTc of iron deficiency. High serum ferriTn, in addiTon to expressing the adequacy of iron stores, could be due to inflammatory condiTons. TSAT is influenced by nutriTonal status, Tming and inflammaTon. TSAT is also limited by high day to day variaTons.

Historically in people with NDD-CKD, it was proposed that serum ferriTn levels less than 25 ng/mL in males and less than 12 ng/mL in females were suggesTve of depleTon of iron stores as a cause of anaemia; but serum ferriTn level is less reliable in the evaluaTon of iron stores in people with NDDCKD and in those receiving HD, because ferriTn level is affected by other factors in addiTon to iron storage status. In relaTvely healthy people receiving HD, before widespread use of IV iron therapy, the finding of a ferriTn level less than 50 ng/mL was not uncommon (1) and was associated with absent bone marrow iron in approximately 80% of people (2) . However, in people receiving HD with several co-morbidiTes, absent iron stores may sTll be found at ferriTn levels approaching or even exceeding 200 ng/mL (3) .

Iron-deficiency is most likely to contribute to anaemia when TSAT results are less than 20%. However, the clinical uTlity of TSAT is impaired by the absence of a diagnosTc threshold above which deficient iron uTlisaTon can be excluded as a cause of anaemia (4) .

There is liXle informaTon in literature to guide the approach to people with CKD who show laboratory evidence of iron deficiency. Nevertheless, given the high prevalence of GI blood loss due to variety of causes in this populaTon, deciding on a subsequent management plan, including endoscopy, depends on the clinical presentaTon. This supports the recommendaTon that people with CKD who present with anaemia and iron deficiency should undergo careful clinical assessment prior to the iniTaTon of anaemia therapy (5-7) .

Reduced iron availability for erythropoiesis can manifest as low mean corpuscular volume (MCV) and mean corpuscular Hb (MCH), but given the relaTvely long lifespan of circulaTng erythrocyte, this test

<!-- image -->

will not reflect the exisTng availability of iron at the Tme of tesTng. TesTng the reTculocytes for their Hb content (CHr or RET-He) may allow more accurate esTmaTon of iron availability, because reTculocytes are present in the circulaTon for 4-5 days, so give a discrete populaTon to study. Reduced red cell Hb can be reflecTve of reduced haem availability or globin. Therefore, the red cell analyte values (%HRC, CHr, Ret-He) may be affected by the presence of haemoglobinopathies (4) .

## 2. Treatment of Anaemia of CKD with Iron (Guidelines 2.1 - 2.7)

## Guideline 2.1 - Treatment of Anaemia with Iron therapy - Iron Reple9on:

We recommend that people should be iron replete to achieve and aim to maintain target Hb range whether receiving ESA or HIF PHI or not. (1B)

## Iron repleTon is usually defined as:

- %HRC &lt;6% / CHr or RET-He &gt;31 pg / ferriTn and TSAT (&gt;100 microgram/L and &gt;20%) in people with non-dialysis dependent CKD (NDD-CKD) or on PD and &gt;200 microgram/L in people receiving HD. (2B)
- For children, aim for a target ferriTn level greater than 100 microgram/L for children with CKD receiving dialysis as well as children with CKD not on ESA or HIF-PHI therapy. (not graded)
- In ensuring iron repleTon iron should be considered when ferriTn is &lt;500 mcg/L and/or the TSAT &lt;30%.

## Rationale

The definiTon of 'adequate' iron status (1) is:

- Serum ferriTn
- o 200-700 microgram/L in people receiving HD
- o 100-600 microgram/L in people with NDD-CKD and

- HRC &lt;6%, or CHr/RET-He &gt;31 pg
- TSAT 20%-40%

It must be noted that the above ranges are not the opTmum values to target with treatment as observed in some trials, if the treatment aims to minimise ESA dosage to maintain target Hb and maximising Hb levels and minimising the need to iniTate ESA therapy. However, the aim of iron treatment targets is to opTmise anaemia therapy while minimising potenTal toxicity. Therapy targets aim at:

1. Minimising the ESA or HIF PHI dose required to maintain target Hb levels in those on ESA or HIF PHI therapy and;
2. Maximising the Hb level and minimising the need to iniTate ESA or HIF PHI therapy to achieve target-range Hb levels in those not on ESA or HIF PHI therapy.

Increasing the Hb in anaemic people with CKD places the greatest demand for iron in the erythropoieTc Tssues. During ESA or HIF-PHI inducTon therapy iron requirements will depend on the rate of erythropoiesis, the Hb deficit, and ongoing iron losses. Once the target Hb has been reached and Hb stabilised, the iron requirements will be dependent on ongoing iron losses. When adequate iron status is achieved, those on ESA or HIF-PHI therapy should be given maintenance iron treatment as required.

Several studies have reported that the dose of ESA required to achieve and maintain a given Hb level is inversely related to iron stores (2-7) . Iron deficiency (absolute or funcTonal) was the main cause of ESA resistance in the UK, but this has now been solved by parenteral iron replacement strategies (8) . The evidence behind the statement that TSAT generally should be maintained at greater than 20% stems from a single RCT comparing higher to lower TSAT targets; parTcipants randomised to a target TSAT of 30% to 50% demonstrated a 40% reducTon in ESA dose compared with those assigned to a target of 20% to 30% (9) .

In a randomised controlled study involving 157 haemodialysis parTcipants comparing iron management based on serum ferriTn and transferrin saturaTon versus CHr, CHr was a markedly more stable analyte than serum ferriTn or transferrin saturaTon. Iron management based on CHr resulted in similar haematocrit and epoeTn dosing while significantly reducing IV iron exposure (10) . In another study involving 164 people with CKD on HD, low CHr (&lt;26 pg) was suggesTve of funcTonal iron deficiency. When a subgroup of parTcipants were randomly assigned to receive a single dose of IV iron dextran (1000 mg), a CHr &lt;26 pg at baseline predicted iron deficiency with a sensiTvity of 100% and specificity of 80%. The serum ferriTn, transferrin saturaTon and percentage of hypochromic red blood cells were all less accurate. The Tme to correcTon of iron deficiency at the level of the reTculocyte was found to be within 48 hours as measured by correcTon of the mean CHr to &gt;26 pg, and by the shiV of the majority of the reTculocyte populaTon to CHr &gt;26 pg within this Tme span (11) .

In a study comparing TSAT versus CHr as a guide of parenteral iron therapy in 197 Japanese people with CKD-PD, although CHr reflected the iron status more sensiTvely, TSAT was a beXer clinical marker for iron supplementaTon therapy (12) . A cross-secTonal study of 72 DD-CKD (HD) was performed in which the mean haemoglobin was 9.6 ± 0.16 g/dL. Mean haemoglobin content of

<!-- image -->

<!-- image -->

reTculocytes (CHr) was normally distributed and correlated with MCV, MCH and red cell ferriTn. A low CHr idenTfied iron deficiency with normal serum ferriTn or transferrin saturaTon (13) . Tessitore et al (14) compared the diagnosTc efficiency of different iron markers in HD. Although percentage hypochromia &gt;6% was the best marker to idenTfy responsiveness to intravenous iron; CHr was 78% efficient at cut-off ≤ 29 pg.

TSAT and serum ferriTn were evaluated in 47 people with HD with baseline serum ferriTn levels &lt;600 mcg/L. ParTcipants were treated with IV dextran (1000 mg over ten haemodialysis treatments). ParTcipants were classified as having iron deficiency if haematocrit value increased by 5% or if their erythropoieTn dose decreased by 10% by 2 months. Receiver operator curves (ROC) demonstrated that none of the iron indices had a high level of uTlity (both sensiTvity and specificity &gt;80%). As such it was concluded that both tests should be interpreted in the context of the parTcipant's underlying EPO responsiveness. In parTcipants who are responsive to EPO, a TSAT &lt;18% or serum ferriTn level &lt;100 mcg/L should be used to indicate inadequate iron. When EPO resistance is present, TSAT&lt;27% or ferriTn &lt;300 mcg/L should be used to guide iron management (15) .

A study of 209 parTcipants with solid tumours or haematological malignancy using a threshold of CHr 32 pg ruled out iron deficiency with a negaTve predicTve value of 98.5% (16) . An American study of 556 parTcipants who were referred for anaemia management yielded a receiver operaTng characterisTc analysis CHr cut-off of 30.7 pg to idenTfy IDA with 68.2% sensiTvity and 69.7% specificity (17) . A further study of 40 people with CKD-HD in the maintenance phase of erythropoieTn therapy compared for each person: baseline aVer an iron-free period and a second sample aVer 4 weeks of IV iron. For a CHr cut-off of 30.8 pg the area under curve (AUC) was 0.84 (95% CI 0.640.93), sensiTvity 78.7%, and specificity 87.2%. %HRC AUC was 0.78 (95% CI 0.64-0.91), at cutoff 2.4%, sensiTvity 72.2%, and specificity 88.1% (18) .

A cross-secTonal study by Karunarathne et al of 100 people with CKD in Sri Lanka demonstrated for the already defined cut-off value of CHr 29 pg showed a sensiTvity and specificity of 54% and 73% respecTvely, but from the ROC coordinates they suggest an increase to 31 pg will increase the sensiTvity and specificity to 63% and 61% respecTvely (19) .

NICE guidelines on the evaluaTon of iron therapy in people with CKD suggests that for people on haemodialysis, %HRC &gt;6% dominated all other iron evaluaTon strategies (it led both to more QALYs and lower cost) (1) . For the other people, TSAT&lt;20% alone or serum ferriTn &lt;100 mcg/L alone were the least cost-effecTve strategy, but %HRC was the most cost-effecTve (1) .

NICE guidelines on anaemia management in people with CKD suggest to:

- Use percentage of hypochromic red blood cells (% HRC; &gt; 6%), but only if processing of blood sample is possible within 6 hours. Since a fresh blood sample is needed, this test may be difficult to use rouTnely in clinical pracTce.
- If using percentage of hypochromic red blood cells is not possible, use reTculocyte Hb content (CHr &lt;31 pg- revised in these guidelines but NICE remains currently at 29 pg) or equivalent tests - for example, reTculocyte Hb equivalent.

<!-- image -->

- If these tests are not available or the person has thalassaemia or thalassaemia trait, use a combinaTon of transferrin saturaTon (less than 20%) and serum ferriTn measurement (less than 100 microgram/L).

We believe that CHr (&lt;31 pg) is more sensiTve in determining iron depleTon than %HRC (17-19) . This is because CHr reflects haemoglobin content of young reTculocytes, and therefore reflects iron availability in the preceding few days; while %HRC reflects haemoglobin contents of whole erythrocyte pool, and since senescent erythrocyte tend to get smaller in volume, the test may be affected by the overall rate of erythropoiesis.

- If neither test is available, we recommend tesTng both serum ferriTn and transferrin saturaTon rather than relying on either test separately unless the serum ferriTn is &lt;100 mcg/L and suggests absolute iron deficiency (1,15) .
- For children, a target ferriTn &gt;100 mcg/L for dialysis and NDD-CKD, not on ESA therapy is appropriate (20) . There is no evidence that a higher ferriTn target of 200 mcg/L is beneficial or safe in paediatric HD.

Guideline 2.2 - Treatment of Anaemia with Iron Therapy - Ini9a9on of ESA/HIF-PHI and Iron Status: We recommend that people on ESA or HIF-PHI therapy should remain iron replete before and during therapy either with oral iron or intravenous iron. (1B)

## Guideline 2.2.1

We recommend that ESA or HIF-PHI therapy should not be iniTated in the presence of absolute iron deficiency (ferriTn &lt;100 mcg/L in NDD-CKD and &lt;200 mcg/L in DD-CKD) or funcTonal Iron deficiency (TSAT &lt;20% will normal or elevated ferriTn levels) unTl this is corrected and anaemia persists. Iron supplements should be given prior to or when iniTaTng ESA or HIF-PHI therapy. (1B)

## Guideline 2.2.2

We suggest that to define funcTonal iron deficiency (FID) ('iron restricted erythropoiesis'), a TSAT &lt;20% in people with NDD-CKD or maintained on PD, and in those receiving HD be used. Normal or high serum ferriTn values do not exclude iron deficiency, as it could be due to other causes such as infecTon or inflammaTon. (2B)

## Rationale

Iron is required for producTon of new red cells. Iron must be supplied to the erythropoieTc Tssue at an adequate rate, parTcularly if sTmulated by ESA or HIF-PHI therapy.

- For people with CKD on dialysis (DD-CKD), percentage of HRC &gt;6%, or CHr/RET-He &lt;31 pg are ideal test to assess iron status.
- If these tests are not available or the person has thalassaemia or thalassaemia trait, a combinaTon of transferrin saturaTon (less than 20%) and serum ferriTn measurement (less than 100 mcg/L) could be a suitable alternaTve (1)

A consistent finding in the series of trials using HIF-PHI (Roxadustat) was the effect on iron metabolism. In the non-dialysis trials against placebo (ANDES, ALPS and OLYMPUS), there was a 50% reducTon in the requirement for IV iron use, such that the monthly dose of iron required was significantly reduced (2-4) . This led to reducTons in serum ferriTn but no change in TSAT or serum iron. However, the trials did not assess the quanTty of oral iron use. This suggests that HIF-PHI therapy may simultaneously correct anaemia and iron depleTon. In the DOLOMITES trial, the use of either IV or oral iron was also reduced in the Roxadustat vs darbepoeTn alfa groups. While iron administraTon was leV to the invesTgator's discreTon and/or were based on local clinical pracTce paXerns in the darbepoeTn alfa group, oral iron was requested by trial protocol in the Roxadustat group (5) .

Another challenge in assessing the impact of HIF-PHI on iron use was that in some trials, iron protocols differed between treatment and comparator groups within a trial while other trials tested similar iron protocols (6, 7, 8) . Other potenTal trial design limitaTons included differences in Hb targets and the actual achieved Hb between treatment arms, differences in the proporTon of parTcipants with baseline iron deficiency and baseline imbalances in iron and hepcidin status and potenTally relevant co-morbidiTes. Therefore, further data from randomised controlled trials is needed to confirm these secondary findings on iron uTlisaTon before definiTve guidance can be given.

Iron parameters should be monitored during treatment with HIF-PHI therapy and iron deficiency should be avoided because it is associated with thromboembolic events, impaired red blood cell producTon (9) , lower health related quality of life (HRQoL), higher rates of cardiovascular events and higher mortality (10) .
