## Guideline 2.3 - Treatment of Anaemia with Iron Therapy - Route of Administra9on:

We suggest that for people with CKD not requiring HD, or people currently receiving PD who are being considered for ESA therapy, intravenous iron should be considered to reduce ESA dose requirements. (2B)

## Guideline 2.3.1

We suggest for people with NDD-CKD or maintained on PD (i.e. not requiring HD), the choice between oral vs. parenteral iron depends on a shared decision and should include the impact of the severity of iron deficiency, the previous response and side effects, the availability of venous access and the need to iniTate ESA or HIF-PHI therapy. (2A)

## Guideline 2.3.2

We recommend that most people on haemodialysis will require IV iron. (1A)

## Guideline 2.3.3

We suggest when offering IV iron therapy to people not receiving in-centre HD, to consider high dose, low frequency (HiD/LF) IV iron as the treatment of choice for adults and young people when trying to achieve iron repleTon, considering all of the following (2B):

- Availability of venous access
- Preferences of the person with anaemia of CKD or, where appropriate, their family or carers
- Nursing and administraTon costs

- Cost of local drug supply
- Provision of resuscitaTon faciliTes

## Guideline 2.3.4

We suggest the frequency of administraTon of HiD/LF IV iron for people with CKD not requiring HD and for people receiving PD needs to be tailored for each person as there is no clear data. (not graded)

## Rationale

The evidence base for IV iron over oral iron in people with CKD who are pre-dialysis or maintained on PD is limited. Oral iron, if tolerated, appears to be adequate in most people parTcularly in combinaTon with ESA therapy. In people who appear resistant to ESA therapy on oral iron, or are intolerant of oral iron, a therapeuTc trial of IV iron trial seems reasonable.

- One randomised study in 188 parTcipants with IV iron (1000 mg iron sucrose in divided doses over 14 days) versus oral iron (ferrous sulphate 325 mg TDS) in pre-dialysis parTcipants demonstrated a greater improvement in Hb outcome in those on IV iron (more parTcipants achieved a Hb increased of &gt;10 g/L) but no difference in the proporTon of parTcipants who had to commence ESA aVer the start of the study (1).
- Two studies in pre-dialysis populaTons not on ESA (one without oral iron and the other aVer oral iron therapy) demonstrated improvements in Hb outcome aVer IV iron (2, 3) .

Oral iron is easy and cheap to prescribe. However, it seems reasonable to treat people with CKD who have not responded to, or have been intolerant of, oral iron with IV iron.

Two randomised controlled trials of oral versus IV iron supplementaTon in parTcipants with advanced NDD-CKD receiving concomitant ESAs agree. In the first study of 45 parTcipants with Hb &lt;110 g/L given either ferrous sulphate 200 mg TDS versus 300 mg iron sucrose IV monthly, there was no difference in Hb or ESA dose between the oral and IV group receiving ESA over a mean 5.2 months follow-up (4) . Iron stores were greater in the IV than oral group. Five parTcipants (55%) in the oral iron group had diabetes, compared to none on the IV iron group and this may have confounded the results on iron stores. In addiTon, more parTcipants in the oral iron group were exposed to ACEi/ARBs.

Similar findings were reported in another study of 96 people with NDD-CKD comparing 5 weeks of IV iron sucrose (200 mg every 7 days for a total of 5 doses) versus 29 days of thrice daily oral iron (ferrous sulphate 325 mg TDS). There was no difference in Hb or ESA dose but greater increase in ferriTn in the IV group (5) . In this study the frequency of gastrointesTnal symptoms was greater in the oral iron group than the IV iron group (consTpaTon 34.5% vs. 12.5%; nausea 10.4% vs. 4.2%). High dose, low frequency (HiD/LF) is considered to be a maximum of 2 infusions. For adults this is considered to be a minimum of 500 mg of iron in each infusion. Low dose, high frequency (LD/HF) is considered to be more than 2 infusions. For adults, there would typically be 100-200 mg of iron in each infusion.

At the Tme of publicaTon intravenous iron products available in the UK did not have a UK markeTng authorisaTon for all ages of children and young people for this indicaTon.

Refer to the Summary of Product CharacterisTcs for the prescripTon of individual iron preparaTons.

<!-- image -->

<!-- image -->

There is increasing evidence that using IV iron in CKD-PD is safe, improves Hb levels and reduces ESA dose requirements (6) :

- A cross-over study of oral and IV iron demonstrated higher Hb and lower ESA doses with IV iron aVer 4 months oral (7) .
- A retrospecTve study evaluaTng ferric carboxymaltose (FCM) in a populaTon of 91 people with CKD-PD over 12 months found no SAEs including hypersensiTvity. More than 60% of parTcipants achieved ferriTn 200-800 mcg/L and TSAT &gt;20% and a reduced ESA dose was necessary to maintain Hb levels (8) .
- A study demonstrated that abnormal iron status, is associated with an increased risk of mortality in people treated with PD. FuncTonal Iron Deficiency (FID) had the greatest all-cause and cardiovascular mortality (9) .

The relaTve safety of parenteral iron compared with oral iron was assessed in a study involving parTcipants with stage 3 and 4 NDD-CKD and iron deficiency anaemia. Study parTcipants were randomly assigned to either oral ferrous sulphate (n=69 to 325 mg three Tmes daily for 8 weeks) or IV iron sucrose (n=67 to 200 mg every 2 weeks, total 1 g). The trial was terminated early based of a higher risk of serious adverse events in the IV iron treatment group. There were 36 serious cardiovascular events among 19 parTcipants assigned to the oral iron treatment group and 55 events among 17 parTcipants of the intravenous iron group (adjusted incidence rate raTo 2.51 (1.56-4.04)). InfecTons resulTng in hospitalisaTon had a significantly increased adjusted incidence rate raTo of 2.12 (1.24-3.64). The authors concluded that among people with NDD-CKD and anaemia, IV iron therapy could be associated with an increased risk of serious adverse events, including those from cardiovascular causes and infecTous diseases (10) .

Conversely, the above finding was not reproduced in another larger trial that involved 626 people with NDD-CKD with anaemia and iron deficiency not on ESAs (11) . In this trial, parTcipants were randomised (1:1:2) to intravenous (IV) ferric carboxymaltose (FCM), targeTng a higher (400-600 microgram/L) or lower (100-200 microgram/L) ferriTn range or oral iron therapy. The primary end point was Tme to iniTaTon of other anaemia management (ESA, other iron therapy or blood transfusion) or Hb trigger of two consecuTve values &lt;100 g/L during Weeks 8-52. Achieving a higher ferriTn target range led to a delay in the need to iniTate ESA therapy and also a reducTon in ESA dosage. The increase in Hb was greater with high-ferriTn FCM versus oral iron (P = 0.014) and a greater proporTon of parTcipants achieved an Hb increase ≥10 g/L with high-ferriTn FCM versus oral iron (HR: 2.04; 95% CI: 1.52-2.72; P &lt; 0.001). Rates of adverse events and serious adverse events were similar in all groups (11) .

Similarly, no safety signal could be detected in another trial comparing intravenous iron isomaltoside versus oral iron in stage G5 NDD-CKD. In this trial 351 iron-deficient parTcipants were randomised 2:1 to either iron isomaltoside 1000 or iron sulphate administered as 100 mg elemental oral iron twice daily (200 mg daily) for 8 weeks. Hb response, serum-ferriTn and TSAT were significantly increased with IV iron compared with those treated with oral iron. Incidence of adverse drug reacTons was not different between both groups. More parTcipants treated with oral iron sulphate withdrew from the study due to adverse events (4.3 versus 0.9%, P = 0.2) (12) .

<!-- image -->

At present oral iron should remain first line treatment among people with NDD-CKD and IV iron used if recipients are intolerant of oral iron or remain with absolute or FID despite oral iron therapy. The further interpretaTon of these results is limited by several factors including the relaTve short duraTon of follow-up and limited data on potenTal long term adverse effects such as the impact of oxidaTve stress. However, as indicated by NICE, therapy should be individualised, or perhaps tailored and a high dose low frequency intravenous regime should be employed.

People treated with HD have addiTonal iron losses from GI bleeding, blood tests and losses in the dialysis circuit that result in iron supplementaTon requirements that outstrip the capacity of the gut to absorb iron effecTvely. Maintenance IV iron in HD greatly reduces ESA requirements and costs (1,4,13-18) . Maintaining iron stores at steady state in a HD populaTon requires 50-60 mg/week of intravenous iron (14-16) . How this is repleted remains a subject under study. A recent open label, randomised, mulTcentre, non-inferiority trial conducted in 351 people on HD randomised 2 : 1 to either iron isomaltoside 1000 (Group A) or iron sucrose (Group B) (19) . Subjects in Group A were equally divided into A1 (500 mg single bolus injecTon) and A2 (500 mg split dose). Group B were also treated with 500 mg split dose. All treatments showed similar efficacy and safety (19) .

The FERWON Trial of 1538 people with NDD-CKD and iron deficiency anaemia (Hb &lt;110 g/L, SF &lt;100 mcg/L or &lt;300 mcg/L and TSAT&lt;30% and stable dose of ESA, was a randomised, open label study. 1027 parTcipants were randomised to 1g iron isomaltoside and 511 to iron sucrose (200 mg given in in total up to five Tme) (20) . The co-primary endpoints were serious or severe hypersensiTvity reacTons and change in Hb from baseline to week 8. There were addiTonal key safety endpoints:  the incidence of composite cardiovascular adverse events and frequency of hypophosphatemia (&lt;0.65 mmol/L]). The results showed a faster and greater early response with iron isomaltoside for Hb, SF and TSAT compared to iron sucrose. There was no staTsTcally significant difference in the frequency of serious or severe hypersensiTvity reacTons between the treatment groups. The incidence of composite cardiovascular AEs was staTsTcally significantly lower in the isomaltoside group compared to the sucrose group (4.1% vs 6.9%; p=0.25).

A prospecTve case control study from rural India comparing oral (ferrous sulfate) vs IV iron (iron sucrose found that serum iron, ferriTn and TSAT increased in the IV iron compared to the oral group (21) . There were significant increases in Hb in all stages of kidney disease with IV iron in contrast to not being achieved with oral iron (21) .

A trial of 60 people with pre-dialysis CKD (NDD-CKD) oral ferric citrate (2 g three Tmes daily) versus oral ferrous sulfate (325 mg TDS) showed greater increases in TSAT and ferriTn but no difference in Hb or in incidence of adverse events at 12 weeks (22) .

## Guideline 2.4 - Treatment of Anaemia with Iron Therapy - Upper Limit for Iron Therapy:

For people not on HD, we recommend that serum ferriTn should not exceed 600 mcg/L in those treated with iron. To achieve this, iron management should be reviewed when ferriTn is &gt;500 mcg/L, recognising that a level of &gt;800 mcg/L may reflect iron toxicity. (1B)

## Guideline 2.4.1

For people receiving HD, we recommend that proacTve high-dose IV iron, iniTally 600 mg in divided doses in the first month and then 400 mg every month (or equivalent) should be given unless ferriTn &gt;700 mcg/L or TSAT &gt;40%. (1A)

## Guideline 2.4.2

We suggest a tailored approach by clinicians to assess the risk and benefit of starTng a proacTve IV iron protocol in people who have been established on HD for &gt;12 months. (2B)

## Rationale

Iron overload is defined as increased total body iron content with the possible risk of organ dysfuncTon (1) . There is no clinically available method that accurately determines total body iron content. An elevated serum ferriTn does not always correlate with elevaTons in liver iron content (2,

3) . MagneTc resonance imaging provides a reliable assessment of Tssue iron content in people on HD regularly treated with parenteral iron (4) . However, the clinical relevance of increased liver iron remains unclear.

Elevated serum ferriTn together with elevated serum transferrin saturaTon remain the most clinically accurate parameter of iron overload in people with CKD.

DisconTnuaTon of adequate maintenance IV iron when an individual's ferriTn is &gt;500 mcg/L produces a populaTon mean that straddles the 600 mcg/L ceiling (5) . Ongoing iron therapy with ferriTn &gt;500 mcg/L results in a higher median ferriTn outcome (6) .

InterpretaTon of iron status results and deciding on the need for further iron therapy should include a concomitant assessment of changes in Hb level and ESA or HIF-PHI dose over Tme. Examples:

- A dropping ferriTn as well as decreasing Hb levels signifies blood loss e.g., on HD or bowel related anaemia: iron therapy is indicated; further invesTgaTon may be required depending on the clinical scenario.

<!-- image -->

- A decreasing ferriTn level aVer iniTaTon of ESA therapy, with a concomitant rise in Hb level indicates a response to ESA with a shiV of iron from stores to bone marrow: further iron therapy is guided by target ferriTn level.
- An increasing ferriTn level aVer reducTon of ESA dose to bring Hb level down to target range indicates ferriTn level is rising as Hb synthesis is dropping further iron therapy may be postponed.
- A rising ferriTn level and a drop in TSAT suggest an inflammatory condiTon: a source of inflammaTon may be sought such as sepsis, vascular access, surgery, recent hospitalisaTon: further iron therapy depends on target ferriTn level and clinical scenario.
- Ongoing high requirements for IV iron to maintain a given ferriTn level also point to ongoing blood loss which may require further invesTgaTon.

The finding of a TSAT &lt;20% coupled with a ferriTn &gt;500 mcg/L poses a parTcularly difficult problem for clinicians. This situaTon may be caused by iron test variability (7) , inflammaTon, or reTculoendothelial iron blockade. Evidence on the risks and benefits of IV iron therapy in this populaTon is not well established. The effect of iron therapy in this group was assessed in the DRIVE trial (8) , which evaluated the efficacy of IV ferric gluconate in 134 parTcipants with high ferriTn (500-1200 mcg/L) and low TSAT levels (≤ 25%) who were anaemic despite a high rHuEPO dose (≥225 IU/kg/week or ≥22500 IU/week). AVer 6 weeks the parTcipants receiving ferric gluconate (125 mg IV at eight consecuTve HD sessions) showed a significant increase in Hb in comparison with controls. However, the study has a number of limitaTons because, given the small sample size and short follow-up, it provides no informaTon about safety and iron overload.

The PIVOTAL trial compared a high-dose, proacTve IV iron sucrose regimen to a low-dose, reacTve IV iron sucrose regimen in 2141 adult people in their first year of HD receiving an ESA (9) . The high-dose group received 600 mg of IV iron iniTally in the first month and then 400 mg IV iron sucrose proacTvely every month unless serum ferriTn was &gt;700 μg/L or transferrin saturaTon (TSAT) ≥40%. The low-dose group received 0 to 400 mg monthly, with a serum ferriTn of &lt;200 μg/L or a TSAT of &lt;20% being a trigger for iron administraTon. The primary end point was the composite of nonfatal myocardial infarcTon, nonfatal stroke, hospitalisaTon for heart failure, or death, assessed in a Tmeto-first-event analysis. Secondary end points included death, hospitalisaTon for any cause, infecTon rate and ESA dose. The median follow-up period was 2.1 years. The high-dose group received a median monthly iron dose of 264 mg, compared with 145 mg in the low-dose group. The median monthly dose of an ESA was 29,757 IU in the high-dose group and 38,805 IU in the low-dose group. Hence the trial found that paTents in the proacTve arm who achieved ferriTn levels ~600 mcg/L required lower ESA doses overall than those paTents who were in the reacTve arm where ferriTn levels were maintained ~ 200 mcg/L.  A total of 320 (29.3%) in the high-dose group had a primary end-point event, compared with 338 (32.3%) in the low-dose group (HR, 0.85; 95% CI, 0.73 to 1.00; P&lt;0.001 for noninferiority; P=0.04 for superiority). There was no significant difference in infecTon rates or hospitalisaTon for any cause between the two groups. The authors concluded that a highdose IV iron regimen administered proacTvely was superior to a low-dose regimen administered reacTvely; resulTng in a lower risk of death or major adverse cardiovascular events and requiring lower doses of ESA and a lower incidence of blood transfusions (9) .

Therefore, intravenous iron administered at a low dose and high frequency may be more appropriate for adults who are receiving in-centre haemodialysis. It should be noted that the PIVOTAL trial

<!-- image -->

invesTgated incident HD parTcipants only, those who were already established on HD for &gt;12 months were excluded from recruitment. We recommend an individualised approach by clinicians to assess the risk and benefit of starTng a proacTve iv iron protocol in people who have been established on HD for &gt;12 months but it seems likely to have similar benefits.

The FIND-CKD study assessed the 12-month efficacy and safety of IV ferric carboxymaltose (FCM) compared with oral iron in ND-CKD parTcipants with anaemia and iron deficiency. Two different target ferriTn levels were studied: higher (400-600 mcg/L) or lower (100-200 mcg/L). This RCT found that IV FCM targeTng a higher ferriTn is more effecTve than oral iron in delaying or reducing the ESA requirement and the occurrence of 2 consecuTve Hb levels &lt;10 g/dL (10) .

## Guideline 2.5 - Treatment of Iron Deficiency without Anaemia:

We recommend for people with chronic kidney disease with iron deficiency without anaemia and concomitant heart failure administraTon of IV iron to improve well-being, physical funcTon, and to reduce heart failure admissions. (1A)

## Guideline 2.5.1

We suggest for people with CKD with iron deficiency but without anaemia and no concomitant heart failure, a trial of oral or IV iron for improvement in clinical symptoms such as restless legs. (2B)

## Rationale

## Iron Deficiency and no Anaemia

It is not known whether treatment of people with CKD and Hb values &gt;120 g/L in the presence of iron deficiency is beneficial. The Iron and Heart mulTcentre double-blinded randomised controlled clinical trial examined the effects of a single dose of intravenous iron vs. placebo on funcTonal capacity using the 6-minute walk test, in non-anaemic iron deficient people (serum ferriTn &lt;100 μg/L and/or TSAT&lt;20%) with NDD-CKD stage 3b-5. 54 individuals were randomised to receive ferric derisomaltose (n = 26) or placebo (n = 28) (1-3) . Secondary analysis showed no impact on markers of oxidaTve stress, inflammaTon, and endothelial funcTon. There was a modest but staTsTcally significant rise in E-selecTn in the IV iron group at 1-month and 3-month follow-up (p = 0.030 and p = 0.002 respecTvely). These results suggest ferric derisomaltose administraTon in people with NDDCKD iron deficient does not induce prolonged oxidaTve stress or inflammaTon. These preliminary secondary analyses suggest larger trials are required to quanTfy the benefit of IV iron administraTon in this group.

The FAIR-HF trial enrolled 459 parTcipants with NYHA class II or III, iron deficiency (ferriTn &lt;100 µg/l or between 100 and 299 µ g/l if TSATs &lt;20%), Hb 95 - 135 g/L (4) . ParTcipants were randomised to receive 200 mg of IV iron (ferric carboxymaltose) or saline. The primary end points were NYHA funcTonal class and self-reported PaTent Global Assessment at 24 weeks. The distance walked in 6 minutes was a secondary end point. ParTcipants receiving ferric carboxymaltose, 50% reported being much or moderately improved on the PaTent Global Assessment as compared to 28% of parTcipants receiving placebo (OR, 2.51; 95% CI: 1.75 to 3.61). In the ferric carboxymaltose group, 47% had a NYHA funcTonal class I or II at week 24, as compared with 30% of parTcipants assigned placebo (OR for improvement by one class, 2.40; 95% CI: 1.55 to 3.71). Significant improvements were seen in distance on 6-minute walk test. In a sub-group analysis, the benefits of ferric carboxymaltose were seen in people with eGFR both above and less than 60 ml/min/1.73m 2 .

The CONFIRM-HF trial enrolled 304 symptomaTc parTcipants with heart failure with leV ventricular ejecTon fracTon &lt; or = 45%, elevated natriureTc pepTdes, and iron deficiency (ferriTn &lt;100 ng/mL or 100-300 ng/mL if TSATS &lt;20%) (5) . ParTcipants were randomised to either IV iron (ferric carboxymaltose, given as 500 or 1000 mg) or placebo. The primary end point was 6-min-walk-test (6MWT) distance from baseline to week 24. Treatment with FCM significantly prolonged 6MWT at week 24 (difference FCM vs. placebo: 33 ± 11 m, p = 0.002). The finding was consistent across all subgroups including those with eGFR &lt; 60 mL/min/1.73m 2 .

The IRONMAN trial was a randomised controlled trial in the UK of people with heart failure (LV ejecTon fracTon &lt; or equal to 45%) and TSATs &lt; 20% or serum ferriTn &lt; 100 µg/L were eligible (6) . 1137 were enrolled and randomly assigned to receive ferric derisomaltose or usual care. The primary outcome was recurrent hospital admissions for heart failure and cardiovascular death. 336 primary endpoints (22.4 per 100 paTent years) occurred in the ferric derisomaltose group and 4111 (27.5 per 100 paTent years) in the placebo group (RR 0.82 (0.66 - 1.02) p = 0.070. In the COVID 19 analysis (censoring f/u on Sept 30 th 2020) 210 primary endpoints (22·3 per 100 paTent-years) occurred in the

<!-- image -->

<!-- image -->

ferric derisomaltose group compared with 280 (29·3 per 100 paTent-years) in the usual care group (RR 0·76 [95% CI: 0·58 to 1·00]; p=0·047). In a subgroup analysis there was no difference between those with eGFR above or below 60 mL/min/1.73m 2 . For &lt; 60 mL/min/1.73m 2 group there were 248 events (26.86 per 100 paTent-years) in the ferric derisomaltose group compared to 318 events (35.36 per 100 paTent years) in the placebo group (RR 0.77 [0.60-0.98].

The Iron and Heart trial and the Iron and Muscle trial both randomised parTcipants to intravenous iron (ferric derisomaltose and ferric carboxymaltose in the two studies respecTvely (2, 7) . Both recruited parTcipants with CKD (eGFR &lt; 60 mL/min/1.73m 2 ) and defined iron deficiency as a ferriTn &lt; 100µg/L and/or TSATs &lt; 20%. Both used 6MWT distance one month as the primary outcome. Iron and Heart recruited 54 parTcipants and the Iron and Muscle trial recruited 75. AdjusTng for baseline 6MWT there was no staTsTcally significant difference in 6MWT distance at 1 month in either study ( p = 0.26 and 0.74 in the two studies respecTvely). A subgroup in the Iron and Muscle trial had mitochondrial funcTon assessed by two methodologies: respirometry and by 31-phosphorus magneTc resonance spectroscopy. There was no difference in mitochondrial funcTon between placebo and iron groups at 1 month ( p = 0.84 and 0.84 for the two methodologies respecTvely).

In a systemaTc review of iron-deficient but not anaemic adults, iron supplementaTon was associated with and improvement in subjecTve measures of faTgue but not with objecTve improvements in physical capacity, as determined by aerobic tests. It should be noted that people with CKD were not specifically included or analysed in the studies examined (8) .

Two randomised controlled trials explored the use of IV iron in the treatment of restless leg syndrome (RLS) in HD paTents. They both idenTfied a significant but transient improvement in symptoms. However, since IV iron is now an established treatment in haemodialysis they have limited impact (9, 10) .

They were both included in a systemaTc review which found that treatment of RLS with iron was associated with an increased rate of adverse events RR 2.04 (95% CI: 1.46-2.85), however these were not severe and not associated with treatment disconTnuaTon. Overall, iron supplementaTon was associated with improvement of RLS (11) .

Guedes and colleagues (12, 13) have shown a direct associaTon with biomarkers of iron stores with worse physical health-related quality of life in NDD-CKD paTents with or without anaemia and also an associaTon with increased risk of all-cause mortality and cardiovascular events in people with NDD-CKD, with or without anaemia. Some have suggested consideraTon of treatment or people with 'profound iron deficiency defined as a serum ferriTn of &lt;30 mcg/L and TSAT &lt;16% but data is lacking to confirm this.

## Guideline 2.6 - Administra9on of IV Iron - Safety Recommenda9ons:

We recommend that resuscitaTve medicaTon and personnel trained to evaluate and resuscitate be present in the event of anaphylaxis at each administraTon of intravenous iron. (1A)

## Guideline 2.6.1

We recommend avoiding parenteral iron therapy in people with acTve infecTon. (1C)

## Guideline 2.6.2

We suggest avoiding iron therapy in people with the following condiTons (not graded):

- HepaTTs C virus infecTon (PosiTve PCR test)
- Haemochromatosis

## Guideline 2.6.3

We suggest cauTon in prescribing parenteral iron in people with (not graded):

- Chronic liver disease
- Heterozygous for any of the haemochromatosis genes (C282Y (c.845G&gt;A), H63D (c.187C&gt;G) and S65C (c.193A&gt;T).

## Guideline 2.6.4

We suggest choice of parenteral iron be tailored, considering the benefits and risks associated with available iron preparaTons. (2C)

## Rationale

All forms of IV iron may be associated with acute adverse events (AEs). Immune mechanisms (including IgE-mediated responses or complement acTvaTon-related pseudo-allergy) may have a role in some cases (1) .  Anaphylactoid reacTons appear to occur more frequently with high molecular weight iron dextran (2) . The rate of life-threatening reacTons to iron dextran administraTon is 0.6% to 0.7% (3,4) . Labile or free iron reacTons occur more frequently with non-dextran forms of iron (4) . These reacTons, also known as Fishbane reacTons, are infusion rate related and are characterised by facial flushing, back pain and chest Tghtness someTmes accompanied with dyspnoea. They can oVen be mistaken for anaphylaxis, however symptoms oVen disappear following cessaTon of the infusion. PaTents should have their vital signs monitored following cessaTon of the infusion and if symptoms have disappeared, can conTnue the therapy at a lower infusion rate. This does not exclude the paTent form receiving that form of IV iron in the future, however a slower rate of infusion would be required for subsequent treatment. (1)

In one study, a total of 2534 people receiving haemodialysis were directly observed aVer doubleblind exposure to intravenous sodium ferric gluconate (SFGC) or placebo. One parTcipant in each of the SFGC and placebo groups experienced anaphylactoid reacTons. AddiTonal cases with characterisTcs possibly consistent with anaphylaxis occurred in 0.4% of intravenous SFGC-treated parTcipants and 0.1% of placebo-treated parTcipants. The results suggest that there is a relaTvely low rate of anaphylaxis with non-dextran irons and that the reacTons are generally easily managed (5, 6) .

A retrospecTve cohort study compared anaphylaxis rates between five IV iron preparaTons: iron dextran, ferumoxytol, ferric gluconate, iron sucrose and ferric carboxymaltose. The adjusted IRs for anaphylaxis per 10 000 first administraTons were 9.8 cases (95% CI: 6.2 to 15.3 cases) for iron dextran, 4.0 cases (CI: 2.5 to 6.6 cases) for ferumoxytol, 1.5 cases (CI: 0.3 to 6.6 cases) for ferric gluconate, 1.2 cases (CI: 0.6 to 2.5 cases) for iron sucrose, and 0.8 cases (CI: 0.3 to 2.6 cases) for ferric carboxymaltose (7) .

<!-- image -->

<!-- image -->

In the IRONMAN trial, 1127 people with heart failure with HFrEF and iron deficiency were recruited to either receive IV ferric derisomaltose (n=559) or usual care (n=568) (8) . There were no differences between the two groups and the rate of serious adverse events. Only one person in the study had an infusion related reacTon, reported to have experienced vomiTng, back pain and dizziness aVer commencing infusion, but recovered fully aVer overnight observaTon (8) .

The MHRA has issued an updated guidance on the use of parenteral iron. This was in response to concerns raised as a result of serious and rarely fatal hypersensiTvity reacTon, parTcularly in pregnant women. These reacTons can occur even when a previous administraTon has been tolerated (including a negaTve test dose). The risk of hypersensiTvity is increased in people with: known allergies (including drug allergies); immune or inflammatory condiTons (e.g., systemic lupus erythematous, rheumatoid arthriTs); or those with a history of severe asthma, eczema, or other atopic allergy. As a result, the MHRA updated guidelines recommend that (9) :

- IV iron should be administered in strict accordance with the posology and method of administraTon described in the product informaTon for each individual product (note that advice varies between products).
- CauTon is needed with every dose of intravenous iron that is given, even if previous administraTons have been well tolerated.
- IV iron products should only be administered when staff trained to evaluate and manage anaphylacTc or anaphylactoid reacTons-as well as resuscitaTon faciliTes-are immediately available.
- Recipients should be closely monitored for signs of hypersensiTvity during and for at least 30 minutes aVer every administraTon of an IV iron product.
- In people with increased risk of hypersensiTvity, treatment with IV iron products should only be considered if the benefits are clearly judged to outweigh the potenTal risks.

## Rationale

Parenteral iron administraTon to people receiving haemodialysis has been shown to result in a reducTon of circulaTng TNFα levels (1) . In addiTon, chronic iron loading has been associated with an impaired immune response of circulaTng monocytes to ex vivo sTmulaTon with lipo-poly saccharide (LPS) (2) . Excess iron inhibits anT-microbial effector pathways of macrophages (3, 4) . This is exerted via blockade of LPS and interferon-gamma (IFNJ) inducible immune pathways, while producTon of macrophage de-acTvaTng cytokines such as interleukin10 (IL10) is increased (5, 6) . The effect of iron on immune funcTon could be dependent on the iron preparaTon; one study has shown that iron sucrose had more prominent effects on monocyte differenTaTon than other clinically available compounds (7) .

Ishida and Johansen criTcally reviewed available literature regarding the associaTon between iron and infecTon in people receiving HD (8) . The authors idenTfied studies that evaluated the associaTon between the risk of infecTon, serum ferriTn levels (13 studies) and iron usage (24 studies). Thirteen studies with sample sizes ranging from 61 to 2,662 have examined the link between serum ferriTn and infecTon in haemodialysis paTents. Among the 13 studies, nine studies reported an associaTon, and four studies did not find an associaTon between serum ferriTn and infecTon. Among the studies that idenTfied an associaTon, high serum ferriTn (typically defined as &gt;500 or 1,000 microgram/L) was associated with higher incidence of bacterial infecTon or infecTon-related mortality. The incidence of bacterial infecTon ranged from 0.34 to 0.59 infecTons per paTent-year (in studies evaluaTng the rate of infecTon) and 0.93% to 61.9% (in studies evaluaTng the proporTon with infecTon) in the higher serum ferriTn groups and 0.09 to 0.18 infecTons per paTent-year and 0% to 37% in the lower serum ferriTn groups. The authors concluded that these studies suggest an excess of 16 to 50 infecTons per 100 paTent-years in the higher compared with the lower serum ferriTn groups. In studies that expressed the associaTon between serum ferriTn and bacterial infecTon as raTos, higher serum ferriTn was independently associated with a 1.5 to 3.1-fold higher incidence of bacterial infecTon or infecTon-related mortality. Among the 24 studies that evaluated the relaTonship between iron therapy and infecTon, twenty-two studies were observaTonal with sample sizes ranging from 21 to 309,219 parTcipants. Twelve of these studies found an associaTon between any iron usage, higher dose or frequency of iron usage and infecTon or infecTon-related mortality.

One study compared mortality with different dosing paXerns of IV iron (9) . Based on data from 117,050 people receiving HD, the study evaluated the effect of bolus versus maintenance IV iron dosing during repeated 1- month exposure periods on risks of mortality and infecTon-related hospitalisaTon during the subsequent 3 months. In mulTvariable addiTve risk models, compared to maintenance dosing (median monthly dose 200 mg), bolus dosing (median 700 mg) was associated with an increased risk of infecTon-related hospitalisaTon (risk difference, 25 addiTonal events/1000 paTent-years; 95% CI: 16 to 33), with the risk being largest among paTents with a catheter or history

<!-- image -->

of recent infecTon. An associaTon between bolus dosing and infecTon-related mortality was also observed. In contrast, maintenance and low-dose iron (125 mg) dosing were not associated with increased risks of infecTon-related hospitalisaTon or mortality outcomes when compared with no iron.

A mulTcentre study prospecTvely evaluated the associaTon between serum ferriTn levels and IV iron usage with adverse outcomes and mortality among 1086 Japanese people receiving chronic HD. By using Cox proporTonal hazard models and Tme-dependent variables, there was a significantly higher risk of infecTon with higher (above 100 microgram/L) compared to lower (below 100 ng/dl) serum ferriTn levels, and with high (≥50 mg/week) and even low (&lt;50 mg/week) doses of IV iron compared with no IV iron; they also reported significantly higher risk of death among people with highamplitude ferriTn fluctuaTons (serum ferriTn level consistently above 100 microgram/L or upward trend from below to above 100 microgram/L) compared with those with low ferriTn level (10) .

In a study involving 626 people with pre-dialysis CKD. People were treated with intravenous ferric carboxymaltose (with a high and low ferriTn target) or oral iron for 52 weeks. The percentage of deaths, myocardial infarcTons, and infecTons was not significantly different between oral irontreated and IVI-treated parTcipants. However, the study was not powered to evaluate safety of parenteral iron (11) .

Post-hoc analysis studied adverse events between the three arms. The incidence of one or more adverse events was 91.0, 100.0 and 105.0 per 100 paTent-years in the high ferriTn, low ferriTn and oral iron groups, respecTvely. The incidence of adverse events with a suspected relaTon to study drug was 15.9, 17.8 and 36.7 per 100 paTent-years in the three groups; for serious adverse events, the incidence was 28.2, 27.9 and 24.3 per 100 paTent-years. The incidence of cardiac disorders and infecTons was similar between groups (12) .

In a study evaluaTng the safety of parenteral iron therapy in 10,169 people receiving haemodialysis in the United States; aVer adjusTng for 23 demographic and comorbidity characterisTcs among 5833 people included in the mulTvariable analysis; bills for ≤10 vials of iron over 6 months showed no adverse effect on survival when compared with none, but bills for &gt;10 vials showed a staTsTcally significant elevated rate of death (13) . Bills for ≤10 vials of iron over 6 months also showed no significant associaTon with hospitalisaTon (adjusted = 0.92; 95% CI: 0.83 to 1.03; P = 0.15), but bills for &gt;10 vials showed staTsTcally significant elevated risk. More intensive dosing was associated with diminished survival and higher rates of hospitalisaTon, even aVer extensive adjustment for baseline comorbidity (13) .

A subsequent analysis of 32,566 Fresenius Inc. people receiving haemodialysis by the same authors did not find an associaTon between IVI iron dose and risk of death aVer adjusTng for Tme-varying measures of iron treatment and fixed and Tme-varying measures of morbidity (14) .

Kalantar-Zadeh et al. studied 58,058 DaVita Inc. people receiving dialysis (15) . For people who received 400 mg of IVI per month, the risk for death was found to be lower compared with people with no IV iron administered. By contrast, doses &gt;400 mg per month were associated with an

increased risk of death (15) .

Kshirsagar et al. studied 117,050 people receiving haemodialysis. No associaTon was found between dose of IVI iron and short-term risk of myocardial infarcTon, stroke, or death (16) .

A prospecTve observaTonal study by Hoen et al. followed 988 people receiving HD from 19 French centres for 6 months. There were 51 episodes of bacteraemia, but no associaTon with either IV iron dosing or serum ferriTn concentraTon was detected (17) . A more recent study from the same group in 985 people receiving dialysis paTents, demonstrated no significant increase in infecTon rates (18) .

In the IRONMAN trial, 1127 people with heart failure and reduced ejecTon fracTon (HFrEF) and iron deficiency were recruited to either receive IV ferric derisomaltose (n=559) or usual care (n=568). It was found that between the two groups, there were a similar rates of infecTons (25% vs 29%. HR 3.12; 95% CI: -8.30 to 2.06. p=0.24). Deaths due to infecTons were also similar in the two groups (6% vs 5%. HR 1.22, 95% CI: 0.74 to 2.02. p=0.43) as were hospitalisaTons due to infecTon (n= 175 vs 213. HR 0.82, 95% CI: 0.62 to 1.08. p=0.16) (19) .

The PIVOTAL trial randomised 2141 people receiving HD who were within the first year of receiving haemodialysis to a high dose proacTve iron regimen (400 mg monthly) or a low dose reacTve regimen (0-400 mg monthly) based of TSAT and ferriTn, showing a high dose iron regimen was superior in reducing rates of non-fatal MI, non-fatal stroke, hospitalisaTon due to heart failure or death (20) . Secondary analysis sought to address concerns around increasing infecTon rates in paTents receiving IV iron. Secondary endpoints included infecTon, hospitalisaTon for infecTon and death from infecTon. It was found that there was no significant difference in secondary endpoints in the high dose vs low dose iron groups.

Results for 'all infecTon episodes,' there were 508 first events (46.5%) in the proacTve high-dose group versus 477 first events (45.5%) in the low-dose group (HR, 0.98; 95% CI: 0.87 to 1.11; P=0.80). Results for 'hospitalisaTons for infecTons' were 323 first events (29.6%) in the proacTve high-dose arm versus 307 first events (29.3%) in the reacTve low-dose arm (HR, 0.99; 95% CI: 0.82 to 1.16; P=0.92). Results for 'death from infecTons,' showed 46 events (4.21%) in the proacTve high-dose arm versus 41 first events (3.91%) in the reacTve low-dose arm (HR, 1.04; 95% CI: 0.69 to 1.59; P=0.84). ParTcipants with central venous catheter were more likely to experience an infecTon, however no differences in all three infecTon endpoints (in relaTon to the treatment arms) were seen in those people dialysing via central venous access vs arteriovenous fistula (20, 21) .

Meta-analysis from Hougen et al. also supported PIVOTAL findings by comparing infecTon rates from four RCT studies using high dose IV iron regimens (&gt;400 mg a month) vs low dose IV iron or no iron regimens in people receiving haemodialysis (22) . This study concluded that there were no differences in infecTon rates in the high dose iron group (n=743; relaTve risk, 1.02; 95% confidence interval, 0.74 to 1.41). Eight observaTonal studies using high dose IV iron regiments (&gt;200 mg a month) showed no increase in infecTon risk (n=135,532; pooled hazard raTo, 1.13; 95% confidence interval, 0.99 to 1.28) (22) .

<!-- image -->

<!-- image -->

Meta-analysis from Shah A et al. reviewed 154 RCTs looking at risk of infecTon with intravenous iron vs oral iron or no iron across various disease states. This study found that intravenous iron was associated with an increased risk of infecTon when compared with oral iron or no iron (RR, 1.17; 95% CI: 1.04-1.31; I2 = 37%; moderate certainty of evidence) most significantly in those with inflammatory bowel disease (IBD). Across the trials, there were significant variaTon in in how infecTon was defined or reported, leading to inconsistent reporTng across the studies. There were no differences observed in mortality between the groups (23) .

## Rationale

The most commonly used oral treatments for iron deficiency anaemia oVen cause gastrointesTnal side effects, leading to disconTnuaTon of treatment. GastrointesTnal side effects most oVen occur due to the conversion of bivalent iron into trivalent iron, along with the formaTon of reacTve oxygen species (ROS). However, due to the nature of vascular access, intravenous iron administraTon is not well-accepted by many people who are not receiving haemodialysis and furthermore can lead to high non bound (labile) iron levels in the circulaTon, resulTng in ROS generaTon.

To address these concerns, nanosized iron preparaTons have been developed. Reducing the parTcle size of iron compounds increases their surface area, improving solubility in gastric juice and enhancing absorpTon. Novel iron preparaTons like sucrosomial iron, ferric citrate, and ferric maltol offer high bioavailability and good tolerance, making them suitable opTons for anaemia in chronic kidney disease.

In summary, the available evidence suggests that the following alternaTve oral iron preparaTons may have several benefits in the treatment of anaemia in people with CKD, including increasing

<!-- image -->

haemoglobin levels, improving iron parameters, and reducing the need for intravenous iron and erythropoieTn-sTmulaTng agents. However, the specific effects can vary among studies, and further research is needed to clarify opTmal use and long-term safety. Physicians should consider individual paTent factors and preferences when deciding on the use of newer iron preparaTons as part of an anaemia management strategy in people with CKD.

## Alterna9ve Prepara9ons:

## Ferric Citrate

Ferric citrate is used in people with CKD undergoing dialysis primarily for phosphate binding but is also employed off-label as an iron supplement.

There have been two meta-analyses of ferric citrate and treatment of anaemia. One analysis of nine studies found that ferric citrate significantly increased haemoglobin levels compared to acTve control drugs (mean difference 0.43 g/dL) or placebo (mean difference 0.39 g/dL) (1) . A second meta-analysis of five studies showed that ferric citrate substanTally increased haemoglobin levels compared to the comparator group (mean difference 0.65 g/dL) along with improvements in other laboratory parameters such as iron, ferriTn, transferrin saturaTon, and bicarbonate levels (2) .

In a sequenTal, randomised trial, subjects on ferric citrate achieved higher mean iron parameters (ferriTn = 899±488 ng/ml; transferrin saturaTon = 39%±17%) compared to subjects on acTve control (ferriTn = 628±367 ng/ml; transferrin saturaTon = 30%±12%). Haemoglobin levels were also staTsTcally higher on ferric citrate (3) .

A 12-week, double-blind, placebo-controlled trial of ferric citrate of 149 parTcipants with eGFR &lt;60 mL/min/1.73m 2 , Hb, 90-120 g/L; TSAT ≤30%, ferriTn ≤300 ng/mL). Ferric citrate treatment increased mean transferrin saturaTon (TSAT) from 22% ± 7% to 32% ± 14% and increased haemoglobin levels (from 105 ± 8 to 110 ± 10 g/L) compared to placebo (4) .

The ASTRIO trial randomised 93 people who were undergoing HD and being treated with non-ironbased phosphate binders and ESA to receive 24 weeks of ferric citrate or to conTnue their non-ironbased phosphate binders. While Hb and Hct were not significantly changed, the ferric citrate group experienced a significant decrease ESA dose requirement compared to the control group (5) .

In a prospecTve study of 60 parTcipants on HD treated with lanthanum carbonate, parTcipants were randomly assigned to 2 groups: lanthanum carbonate or switch to ferric citrate. The ferric citrate group had significantly increased serum iron, serum ferriTn, transferrin saturaTon, and significantly decreased ESA doses compared to the control group (6) .

A randomised double-blind clinical trial in adults with NDD-CKD and iron deficiency anaemia to compare the safety and efficacy of oral ferric citrate (n=117) versus placebo (n=115) was performed (7) . Ferric citrate-treated paTents had a significantly higher proporTon achieving a ≥10 g/L increase in haemoglobin, with a safety profile similar to the placebo group (7). A further analysis of this study found that ferric citrate showed a greater treatment effect on paTents with lower baseline ferriTn levels, resulTng in a greater rate of haemoglobin rise (8) .

A small RCT of 60 adults with moderate to severe NDD-CKD (eGFR 15-45 ml/min/1.73m 2 ) comparing ferric citrate and ferrous sulphate led to greater increases in transferrin saturaTon and ferriTn

<!-- image -->

compared to ferrous sulphate, but there was no significant difference in haemoglobin levels and the incidence of adverse events did not differ between treatment arms (9) .

In 441 subjects with end-stage kidney failure, ferric citrate (FC) increased ferriTn and transferrin saturaTon levels, reduced intravenous iron use, and lowered ESA requirements (10) . Subjects were randomised to ferric citrate, sevelamer carbonate and/or calcium acetate and followed for 52 weeks. Subjects on FC had increased ferriTn and TSAT levels by week 12 and needed less IV iron and less cumulaTve ESA over 52 weeks (10) .

## Ferric Maltol

Ferric maltol is a compound composed of ferric iron and maltol, a naturally occurring sugar derivaTve commonly found in various food products. This complex remains Tghtly bound in the intesTnal tract unTl it reaches the point of absorpTon. At this stage, iron exhibits a stronger aXracTon to iron transport receptors on the surface of intesTnal cells, leading to its separaTon from maltol. This unique mechanism ensures that there is no unbound or free iron in the gut, reducing the risk of generaTng harmful hydroxyl radicals and minimizing the potenTal for gastrointesTnal side effects. Iron uptake from ferric maltol is subject to saturaTon and relies on the presence of iron transport receptors, ensuring controlled and efficient absorpTon.

AEGIS-CKD is a phase 3, double-blind, randomised, placebo-controlled trial, where parTcipants were given oral ferric maltol at 30 mg or placebo twice daily for 16 weeks (2:1 randomisaTon), followed by ferric maltol at 30 mg twice daily for up to 36 weeks (all paTents) (11) . 167 parTcipants were randomised, with 111 receiving ferric maltol and 56 receiving a placebo. At week 16, haemoglobin significantly increased with ferric maltol compared to placebo. FerriTn, transferrin saturaTon, and serum iron levels also increased with ferric maltol but declined with placebo. Haemoglobin levels remained stable up to week 52 in paTents conTnuing ferric maltol, and they increased in paTents switching from placebo to ferric maltol. The most common adverse events were gastrointesTnal, and adverse events led to treatment withdrawal in a small percentage of paTents, with slightly more in the placebo group (11) .

## Ferric Pyrophosphate (FPC)

FPC is a complex iron salt which is administered along with dialysate. Two phase 3 trials, CRUISE 1 and 2, involving 599 iron-replete parTcipants receiving chronic haemodialysis having been performed (12) . ParTcipants received dialysate containing FPC-iron or a standard dialysate (placebo) for up to 48 weeks. Results showed that Hb concentraTon was maintained in the FPC group but decreased in the placebo group (0.4 g/dL decrease, p &lt; 0.001). Placebo treatment also resulted in larger decreases in CHr and serum ferriTn compared to FPC treatment. The proporTons of paTents with adverse events were similar in both treatment groups (12) .

A Chinese study invesTgated the pharmacokineTcs and safety of FPC in subjects with and without HD. In healthy subjects, the peak serum concentraTon was reached at the end of the infusion, with a Cmax of 33.46 μmol/L at a Tmax of 4.09 hours. In people receiving HD, there were differences in Cmax and Tmax between two doses of FPC. Adverse events were reported in both healthy subjects and people receiving HD, with a limited number of incidences (13) .

## Sucrosomial Iron

Sucrosomial iron comprises ferric pyrophosphate enclosed within a membrane made of phospholipids and sucrose esters of faXy acids.

<!-- image -->

In a randomised trial of 99 parTcipants with CKD and iron deficiency anaemia (Hb ≤120 g/L, ferriTn ≤100 ng/mL, TSAT ≤25%) were divided into two groups: one received oral liposomal iron (30 mg/day) and the other intravenous (IV) iron gluconate (total dose of 1000 mg, infused weekly) for 3 months. The IV iron group showed a faster increase in haemoglobin levels compared to the liposomal iron group during the first month of treatment, but both groups had similar haemoglobin levels by the end of the 3-month treatment period. AVer disconTnuaTon of iron treatment, haemoglobin levels remained stable in the IV group but returned to baseline in the liposomal iron group. The incidence of adverse events was significantly lower in the oral liposomal iron group (14) .

## Hypophosphataemia

Hypophosphataemia is a known side effect of intravenous iron preparaTons as listed in the SmPCs of IV iron products. It is a common side effect with ferric carboxymaltose (FCM) (15) . As detailed in their SmPC, symptomaTc hypophosphataemia leading to osteomalacia and fractures requiring clinical intervenTon including surgery has been reported in the post markeTng seLng. PaTents should be asked to seek medical advice if they experience worsening faTgue with myalgias or bone pain. Serum phosphate should be monitored in paTents who receive mulTple administraTons at higher doses or long-term treatment, and those with exisTng risk factors for hypophosphataemia. In case of persisTng hypophosphataemia, treatment with ferric carboxymaltose should be re-evaluated.

Acute features can include muscle pain and weakness as well as faTgue, although oVen can be asymptomaTc (16) . There is evidence that hypophosphataemia, secondary to IV iron therapy can lead to long term issues with bone metabolism, presenTng as osteomalacia and fractures (16) .

Wolf et al. made the first link between FCM causing a rise in Fibroblast growth factor 23 (FGF23), s hormone which regulates phosphate and vitamin D, causing a decrease in phosphate levels, calcitriol and calcium, with an increase in PTH levels. This study compared FCM to iron dextran treatment, measuring the difference in FGF23 and phosphate levels in people with iron deficiency, secondary to heavy uterine bleeding. Authors reported a significant rise in iFGF23 following treatment with FCM, but not iron dextran (r = 0.59; p &lt; 0.001), with corresponding decrease in phosphate (r = -0.50; p = 0.002). Differences in vitamin D, calcium and PTH were not significant. Hypophosphataemia was reported to conTnue for up to 80 days (17) .

Following on from this study, sub-analysis of the FIRM trial compared FGF23 and phosphate levels in a trial of FCM vs ferumoxytol in which hypophosphataemia was significantly decreased in the FCM group vs ferumoxytol group (&lt;2.0 mg/dl, 50.8% vs. 0.9%; &lt;1.3 mg/dl, 10.0% vs. 0.0%; P &lt; 0.001), conTnuing for up to 5 weeks aVer FCM administraTon in 29.1% of parTcipants. FCM only increased the FGF23 concentraTons, which lead to a significant decrease in calcium (mean difference between iron treatment groups in change from baseline to week 2, 0.37 mg/dl; 95%CI 0.25-0.50; P &lt; 0.001) and vitamin D (mean within-paTent percentage change from baseline to week 2, -60.4 ± 25.9% vs. 2.5 ± 28.0%; P &lt; 0.001), with a rise in PTH levels (18) .

PHOSPHARE-IDA trials compared phosphate levels in people receiving ferric derisomaltose (FDI) or FCM, finding that incidence of hypophosphataemia was significantly higher in the FCM group vs the FDI group (74.4% versus 8.0%, respecTvely; p &lt; 0.001), showing a significant rise in FGF23, a decrease in calcium and increase in PTH levels in the FCM group, as previously reported (19) . Sub-

<!-- image -->

analysis showed 57% of paTents in the FCM group (vs 0% in FDI group) experienced persistent hypophosphataemia, with associated changes in bone metabolism biomarkers (20) .

PHOSPHARE-IBD similarly showed higher rates of hypophosphataemia in the FCM group vs FDI group in the IBD populaTon- 8.3% FDI vs 51.0% FCM (adjusted risk difference: -42.8% (95% CI: -57.1% to 24.6%) p&lt;0.0001) (21) .

Literature review by Schafer B et al. looked at 42 prospecTve clinical trials, which found that people, who have received FCM, are more likely to experience hypophosphataemia than those who have received FDI, with an incidence of 42% vs 4% across 42 clinical trials (95% CI: 36-58% vs. 95% CI: 25%). Significantly decreased levels in serum phosphate were also seen when comparing the two treatments, a decrease in 0.4mmol/L with FCM vs a decrease in 0.06mmol/L with FDI, with hypophosphataemia reportedly persisTng aVer 3 months of treatment with FCM (7) . The only significant predictor of experiencing hypophosphataemia being the severity of anaemia at Tme of treatment, with no significant link seen between IV iron treatment in CKD (18,22) . However, FGF23 has been reported to increase aVer IV iron therapy in people with CKD, with a decrease in acTve vitamin D levels (23) , despite higher baseline levels of FGF23 in paTents with CKD, affecTng bone metabolism long term (24) .

Glaspy J et al. published a literature review that included 40 trials reviewing all of the IV iron products on the US market at the Tme. This included iron dextran, ferric gluconate, FCM, ferumoxytol and iron sucrose. This review found that people without CKD and who receive higher doses of iron are more likely to experience hypophosphataemia (25) . As with previous findings, FCM treatment was associated with the highest rate of reported hypophosphataemia (25) .

Kidney transplant recipients can experience hypophosphataemia within the first 3 months post transplantaTon (secondary to phosphaturia), which is associated with high levels of FGF23 and PTH. This normalises in the recovery period and increasing again with declining graV funcTon, leading to adverse effects on the cardiovascular system, graV funcTon and bone metabolism (26) . 23 kidney transplant recipients received FCM, showing an average decrease in phosphate levels of 0.27 mmol/l (p = 0.003). 56.5% of parTcipants experienced hypophosphataemia, with severe Hypophosphataemia (&lt;0.5 mmol/L) seen in 34.8% of people. Hypophosphataemia persisted in 38.5% of paTents with median normalisaTon of levels at 41 days (27) . This shows that kidney transplant recipients should receive FCM with cauTon and close monitoring (28) .

## Guideline 2.7 - Monitoring of Treatment - Iron Therapy:

We recommend regular monitoring of iron status (every 1-3 months) in people receiving intravenous iron to avoid toxicity. (defined as a serum ferriTn &gt;800 mcg/L or TSAT &gt;40%) (1B)

## Guideline 2.7.1

We recommend that a serum ferriTn consistently &gt;800 mcg/L with no evidence of inflammaTon (normal CRP) is suggesTve of iron overload or potenTal iron toxicity. (1B)

## Rationale

Intravenous iron therapy has potenTal risks as well as benefits. Toxicity associated with high ferriTn outcomes was originally reported in the context of mulTple transfusions in the pre-ESA era. The risk persists that intravenous iron may reproduce similar toxicity and thus regular monitoring during

<!-- image -->

therapy is required. Similarly with ongoing iron losses on HD regular monitoring to avoid worsening iron deficiency is required (1, 3) . The safety of persistently very high ferriTn levels remains unknown. In a cohort of 58058 prevalent people receiving haemodialysis in the USA, both all-cause and cardiovascular mortality had increasing rates across increasing ferriTn levels, whereas the opposite (inverse) associaTon was observed for TSAT increments. Serum ferriTn levels between 200 and 1200 microgram/L and iron saturaTon raTo between 30 and 50% were associated with the lowest allcause and cardiovascular death risks. However, associaTon studies are biased by the fact that serum ferriTn is also a marker of inflammaTon. In unadjusted, Tme-varying model, serum ferriTn &gt;800 microgram/L during each quarter was associated with increased death rate (4) . Significant iron overload in the liver and spleen (assessed through T 2 magneTc resonance) has been described in 19 of 21 people receiving HD with serum ferriTn &gt;1000 microgram/L and severe comorbidiTes who were treated with IV iron (5) . Similarly, Rostoker et al (6) . prospecTvely studied a cohort of 119 fit people receiving HD who were receiving iron and ESA therapy and measured their liver iron content by means of T 1 and T 2 magneTc resonance. Mild to severe hepaTc iron overload was observed in 84% of the people, 36% of whom had severe iron overload approaching that found in haemocromatosis (7) .

Clinical seLngs in which more frequent iron tesTng may be necessary include the following:

1. IniTaTon of ESA therapy
2. Achieving less-than-target Hb level during ongoing ESA therapy
3. Recent bleeding
4. AVer surgery
5. AVer hospitalisaTon
6. Monitoring response aVer a course of IV iron
7. EvaluaTon for ESA hypo-responsiveness

## 3. Treatment of Anaemia with Erythropoiesis Stimulating Agents (Guidelines 3.13.17)

## Guideline 3.1- Inves9ga9ons Before Ini9a9ng ESA Therapy:

We recommend that all correctable causes of anaemia should be ruled out before considering treatment with ESAs. (1B)

## Rationale

InvesTgaTons for iron deficiency, folate and B12 deficiency, hyperparathyroidism, inflammatory states as well as gastro-intesTnal blood losses should be invesTgated and addressed before iniTaTng ESA therapy. By addressing any underlying causes and correcTng any deficiencies, ESA use may be avoided. (1) CorrecTng any deficiencies, including iron deficiency, before starTng ESA treatment will allow for opTmal ESA responses, minimising the dose required to maintain target haemoglobin levels. (2)

## References

1. NaTonal CollaboraTng Centre for Chronic CondiTons, Royal College of Physicians. Chronic kidney disease: assessment and management. 2021. NaTonal InsTtute for Clinical Excellence.
2. Roger SD, Tio M, Park HC, et al. Intravenous iron and erythropoiesis-sTmulaTng agents in haemodialysis: A systemaTc review and meta-analysis. Nephrology (Carlton) . 2017;22 (12):969976.

## Guideline 3.2 - Treatment of Anaemia with Iron Therapy - Ini9a9on of ESA and Iron Status:

We recommend that ESA therapy should NOT be iniTated in the presence of absolute iron deficiency, (ferriTn &lt;100 mcg/L in people with non-dialysis dependent CKD (NDD-CKD) and &lt;200 mcg/L in people who are dialysis dependent) unTl this is corrected and it is determined that anaemia persists in conjuncTon to a shared decision of the advantages and risks of ESA therapy. In people with funcTonal iron deficiency iron supplements should be given prior to or when iniTaTng ESA therapy. (1B)

## Rationale

Iron is required for producTon of new red cells. Iron must be supplied to the erythropoieTc Tssue at an adequate rate, parTcularly if sTmulated by ESA therapy. If iron stores are low ESAs can sTll be used if anaemia of CKD is a likely contributor, as long as iron is made directly available to the erythropoieTc Tssues coincident with the iniTaTon of ESA therapy.

- For people with CKD, percentage of hypochromic red blood cells &gt;6% or reTculocyte Hb content &lt; 31 pg are good tests to assess iron status.
- If these tests are not available or the person has thalassaemia or thalassaemia trait, a combinaTon of transferrin saturaTon (less than 20%) and serum ferriTn measurement (less than 100 microgram/L) are a suitable alternaTve (1) .

<!-- image -->

The PIVOTAL trial has shown that in people receiving haemodialysis on a high-dose proacTve iron regimen (400 mg a month aVer an iniTal dose of 600 mg in the first month) require smaller ESA doses to maintain desirable Hb level between 100-120 g/L, when compared to those paTents on low dose iron regimen (0-400 mg monthly when required to maintain ferriTn &gt;200 μg/L and a transferrin saturaTon &gt;20%). The median monthly dose of erythropoiesis-sTmulaTng agent was 19.4% lower in people receiving the high-dose regimen (29,757 IU per month; interquarTle range, 18,673 to 48,833) than in paTents receiving the low-dose regimen (38,805 IU per month; interquarTle range, 24,377 to 60,620) (median difference, -7539 IU per month; 95% CI, -9485 to -5582). The people in the high dose arm experienced less cardiovascular events, which may be contributed to lower ESA doses use due to pro-acTve iron therapy (2) .

Several smaller prospecTve trials have also been carried out to confirm what was found in the PIVOTAL trial. One study compared the ESA requirements in Iranian people receiving haemodialysis receiving regular low dose iron (100 mg/month, n=30) and high dose iron (400 mg/month, n=30) regimens. It was found that the ESA dosage to maintain hemoglobin level between 100 and 120 g/L was significantly decreased in the high dose iron arm (52,129.03 ± 23,810 vs. 45,760 ± 20,978.71, P ≤ 0.02) (3) .

Another study also compared ESA requirements in Thai people receiving haemodialysis prescribed regular low dose iron (100 mg/month, n=40) and regular high dose iron (200 mg/month, n=39). Similarly, this showed reduced ESA requirements, to maintain a desirable Hb level of 100-120 g/L, in those paTents on the high dose iron regiment. The mean monthly ESA dose at month 12, was 35,706 ± 21,637 IU in the 100-mg IV iron group compared with 26,382 ± 14,983 IU in the 200-mg IV iron group (P = 0.03). No significance was seen between the two groups when looking at the death rate, cardiovascular events, blood transfusions or EQ-5D quality of life measurements (4) .

## Guideline 3.3 - Treatment of Anaemia - Erythropoiesis S9mula9ng Agents:

We recommend that treatment with Erythropoiesis STmulaTng Agents (ESAs) should be offered to people with anaemia of CKD, who are likely to benefit in terms of quality of life and physical funcTon and to avoid blood transfusion; especially in people considered suitable for transplantaTon. (1B)

## Rationale

Treatment of anaemia in CKD with ESA can be expensive (1) , takes Tme to work and carries a small but significant risk to the paTent. It is therefore reasonable, as with any therapy, to treat only those who are expected to benefit in the Tme frame that therapy is being considered. For example, people with severe sepsis/inflammaTon/acute bleeding are unlikely to respond.

People with a very short life expectancy (days or weeks) are not likely to survive long enough for therapy to provide benefit in terms of an increase in Hb. The clinician and paTent should agree on a therapeuTc plan and, at an appropriate Tme, review whether therapy is providing enough benefit to conTnue treatment.

## References

1. NaTonal CollaboraTng Centre for Chronic CondiTons, Royal College of Physicians. Chronic kidney disease: assessment and management. 2021. NaTonal InsTtute for Clinical Excellence.

## Guideline 3.4 - Treatment of Anaemia with ESA therapy - Target Haemoglobin Range:

We recommend that people with non-dialysis dependent CKD (NDD-CKD) or those receiving dialysis, who are on ESA therapy, should achieve Hb between:

- 100 and 120 g/L in adults, young people and children aged 2 years and older. (1B)
- 95 and 115 g/L in children younger than 2 years of age (reflecTng the lower normal range in that age). (2B)

## Guideline 3.5 - Treatment of Anaemia without ESA Therapy - Target Haemoglobin Range:

We suggest that this Hb target range applies exclusively to people with CKD receiving ESAs and is not intended to apply to the treatment of iron deficiency in people receiving iron therapy without the use of ESAs. (2B)

## Rationale for Guidelines 3.4 and 3.5

In determining target Hb guidelines it is important to assess potenTal benefits (in terms of possible improved survival, improvement in health-related quality of life (HRQoL) and avoidance of transfusion requirement and hospitalisaTon) vs. potenTal harms (increased mortality, increased risk of vascular events).

Although several studies have shown that higher Hb targets could be associated with improvements in both physical and mental health domains (1) , the HRQoL benefits of higher Hb targets diminish over Tme (1) . In addiTon, there is no apparent Hb threshold above which there is definiTvely a quality-oflife improvement in the higher Hb treatment arms.

Besarab et al (2) reported a study of normalisaTon of haemoglobin in 1233 prevalent people with receiving HD with high cardiovascular risk on haemodialysis. NormalisaTon of haemoglobin showed no benefit in risk reducTon but did show an improvement in quality of life. The treatment arm showed a trend towards increased risk of death or cardiac event (183 deaths and 19 myocardial infarcts, producing 202 primary events, compared to 164 events (150 deaths, 14 myocardial infarcts) and vascular access (39% versus 29%) and the trial was terminated before compleTon on the grounds that the study was unlikely to show benefit from normalisaTon.

<!-- image -->

<!-- image -->

Two trials evaluated the effect of ESA on people not yet receiving dialysis - CHOIR (3) and CREATE (4) . outcome of the CHOIR trial showed no benefit of higher Hb outcome in people with NDD-CKD (eGFR 15-50ml/min/1.73m 2 ) randomised to Hb of 113 g/L vs. 135 g/L. Higher outcome target Hb had an increased risk (using composite endpoints of death, myocardial infarcTon, or hospitalisaTon for congesTve cardiac failure) and no incremental improvement in quality of life (3) . The limitaTon of this trial is that, compared with the group assigned to the lower Hb treatment target, the higher Hb target group showed at baseline a staTsTcally greater proporTon of paTents with a history of hypertension and coronary artery bypass graV. A report posted by the trial sponsor (5) indicates that paTents assigned to the higher Hb treatment arm also had a significantly greater severity of congesTve heart failure (CHF) at baseline. The results of a mulTvariate analysis, included in this report, indicate that aVer adjustment for baseline condiTons (CHF by NaTonal Health and NutriTon ExaminaTon Survey CHF score, atrial fibrillaTon/fluXer, serum albumin level, reTculocyte count, and age), the relaTonship between treatment assignment and primary composite outcome events is no longer staTsTcally significant (HR, 1.24; 95% confidence interval [CI], 0.95 to 1.62; p = 0.11 compared with the unadjusted HR of 1.34; 95% CI: 1.03 to 1.74; p = 0.03) (6). A secondary analysis of the CHOIR trial suggested that higher doses of epoeTn α, rather than target Hb per se, were associated with an increased risk of death, myocardial infarcTon, congesTve heart failure or stroke compared with lower epoeTn doses, and with poorer outcomes (6) . Another secondary analysis of the CHOIR trial found that, among paTents with diabetes mellitus, the percentage of people reaching the primary end point of death, myocardial infarcTon, congesTve heart failure or stroke within 3 years was similar in the high and low haemoglobin arms of the trial (24.8% versus 24.7%, respecTvely; p = 0.249). By contrast, among paTents without diabetes mellitus at baseline, 36.4% of parTcipants randomised to the higher Hb target had reached the primary end point aVer 3 years compared with 24% of those randomised to the lower haemoglobin target (HR 1.70; 95% CI: 1.03-2.81; p= 0.04). Individuals without diabetes mellitus randomised to the higher haemoglobin target had a significantly greater risk of reaching the primary end point aVer 3 years than individuals with diabetes mellitus randomised to the lower haemoglobin target (7) .

The CREATE (4) trial reported that early correcTon of anaemia to normal Hb (130-150 g/L vs. 105-115 g/L) did not reduce risk of cardiovascular events. Indeed, the hazards raTo for primary endpoints of death from any cause or death from cardiovascular disease consistently (but not significantly) favoured the lower haemoglobin target group. The trend to increase in events appeared to occur aVer iniTaTon of dialysis but there was no difference in endpoints aVer censoring of data from paTents who started dialysis. Quality of life was significantly beXer in the higher Hb outcome group. Although GFR was not significantly different between the two groups, more people started kidney replacement therapy earlier in the higher Hb outcome group (p =0.03) with the difference apparent from 18 months. An important limitaTon of this trial is that the event rate was much lower than predicted; thus, the power to detect a difference in event rates was decreased (4) .

Other important limitaTon (s) of these trials is that important subgroups of parTcipants enrolled in large trials, such as young adults, people returning to dialysis aVer failed kidney transplant, or people with chronic lung disease were not idenTfied or assessed in any of these trials.

Further analysis of outcome of high target Hb was performed by the KDOQI team (8) . An Evidence Review Team analysed all data from randomised controlled trials of anaemia management in NDD-

<!-- image -->

CKD, including, CHOIR, CREATE and other studies. Combining mortality outcomes from eight studies involving 3038 subjects with CKD (the CHOIR and CREATE trials contributed most of the weight to the analysis) revealed no difference between the higher and lower Hb target (7) , but combining adverse cardiovascular events from six studies involving 2850 subjects showed an increased risk among the paTents assigned to the higher Hb targets (a RR of 1.24, 95% CI: 1.02-1.51) (8) , although it is worth noTng that the CHOIR and CREATE trials also contributed most of the weight to the analysis. Among dialysis paTents, combining mortality (four studies, 2391 subjects) or cardiovascular outcomes (three studies, 1975 subjects) showed no staTsTcally significant difference between the higher and lower Hb level with The US Normal Haematocrit Trial (2) contribuTng most of the weight to the analysis.

In the TREAT trial (9) , 4038 people with diabetes, chronic kidney disease not on dialysis, and anaemia, were randomly assigned in a 1:1 raTo to darbepoeTn α, to achieve Hb level of approximately 130 g/L or to placebo, with rescue darbepoeTn α when the haemoglobin level was less than 90 g/L. The primary end points were the composite outcomes of death or a cardiovascular event (nonfatal myocardial infarcTon, congesTve heart failure, stroke, or hospitalisaTon for myocardial ischaemia) and of death or end-stage kidney disease. AVer a median follow up of 29 months, there was no difference between the two arms in the primary outcome of death, cardiovascular event or end stage kidney disease. Fatal or nonfatal stroke occurred in 101 parTcipants assigned to darbepoeTn α and 53 parTcipants assigned to placebo (HR, 1.92; 95% CI: 1.38 to 2.68; p &lt;0.001). The invesTgators concluded that for many involved in clinical decision making this risk of prescribing an ESA in this populaTon will outweigh the potenTal benefits (9) .

Data from observaTonal studies have, however, not shown increased hazard risk among people who achieved higher Hb. In one study, data from people receiving haemodialysis in the UK Renal Registry from 1999 to 2005 were analysed for the relaTve risk of death at different Hb concentraTons. Hb concentraTons above the reference range (100-110 g/L) consistently showed a 35% lower relaTve risk of death, while people with haemoglobin below 100 g/L had a 28% higher mortality. The greatest mortality was seen in people with haemoglobin &lt;90 g/L (73% increased risk of death, although due to the small numbers, this was not staTsTcally significant). On the other hand, the lowest death rate was seen in people with haemoglobin levels between 120 and 139 g/L (64% reduced mortality) (10) .

The effect of cumulaTve ESA dose was also reported in another retrospecTve study (11) . In this study, which looked at data from Medicare's end-stage kidney disease program between 1999 and 2007, different US dialysis centres annual anaemia management pracTce were characterised by esTmaTng their typical use of ESAs and intravenous iron in people receiving haemodialysis within 4 hematocrit categories. Monthly mortality rates were assessed using Cox proporTonal hazards regression to correlate centre-level paXerns of ESA and iron use with 1-year mortality risk in 269,717 people on incident haemodialysis. Monthly mortality rates were highest in people with haematocrit less than 30% (mortality, 2.1%) and lowest for those with haematocrit of 36% or higher (mortality, 0.7%). AVer adjustment for baseline case-mix differences, dialysis centres that used larger ESA doses in people with haematocrit less than 30% had lower mortality rates than centres that used smaller doses (highest vs. lowest dose group: HR, 0.94; 95% CI: 0.90-0.97). Centres that administered iron more frequently to people with haematocrit less than 33% also had lower mortality rates (highest vs. lowest quinTle, HR, 0.95; 95% CI: 0.91-0.98). However, centres that used larger ESA doses in people with haematocrit between 33% and 35.9% had higher mortality rates (highest vs. lowest quinTle, HR,

<!-- image -->

1.07; 95% CI: 1.03-1.12). More intensive use of both ESAs and iron was associated with increased mortality risk in people with haematocrit of 36% or higher (11) .

Post Hoc analysis of the PIVOTAL Trial of 2141 people receiving dialysis, examining the baseline data, and potenTal relaTonship to the primary outcome (all-cause mortality, myocardial infarcTon, stroke, and heart failure hospitalisaTon), and associaTons with key baseline characterisTcs and QoL was measured using EQ5D index, Visual Analogue Scale (VAS), and the KD-QoL (Physical Component Score (PCS) and Mental Component Score (MCS)). The mean baseline EQ5D index and VAS scores were 0.68 and 60.7; 33.7 (PCS) and 46.0 (MCS). Female sex, higher BMI, diabetes mellitus, history of myocardial infarcTon, stroke or heart failure were associated with significantly worse EQ5D index and VAS. CRP and transferrin saturaTon (TSAT) were associated with QoL. Hemoglobin was not an independent predictor of QoL. TSAT was an independent predictor of PCS. CRP was associated with most aspects of QoL. Impaired funcTonal status was associated with mortality. Quality of life was impaired in people starTng hemodialysis. CRP was a consistent independent predictor of the majority of QoL scores. TSAT ≤20% was associated with worse physical component scores of QoL (12) .

Several systemaTc reviews have looked at ESA or ESA and iron use, comparing various Hb target ranges and their effect on quality of life. One review by Spinowitz et al. looked at people receiving dialysis being treated with ESAs and without ESAs and its impact on their health-related quality of life (HRQoL). This showed that there may be some increase in HRQoL in those people treated with ESAs (with or without iron) vs those without treatment. However, there was no evidence for higher Hb targets leading to improved reporTng of HRQoL (13) .

Guedes et al. found that, similarly, higher Hb targets (&gt;115 g/L) may be associated with a small decrease in faTgue levels when compared to those people with CKD aiming for the recommended target of 100-115 g/L. However, there was no significant effect seen on physical funcTon or HRQoL (14) .

The findings of all the above studies have made it difficult to define a safe target Hb in CKD paTents treated with ESA. As a result, target Hb in this paTent group has been the subject of extensive debate in the literature:

- KDIGO suggests that for adults receiving dialysis, ESA therapy could be used to avoid having the Hb concentraTon fall below 90 g/L by starTng ESA therapy when the haemoglobin is between 90- 100 g/L (15) .
- The Anaemia Working Group of ERBP expressed its view that Hb values of 110-120 g/L should be generally sought in the CKD populaTon without intenTonally exceeding 130 g/L In low-risk paTents (i.e., in younger paTents with very few comorbidiTes). In those with ischaemic heart disease with worsening ischaemic symptoms associated with anaemia, or in those in whom a clear benefit on quality of life can be foreseen, the start of ESA therapy could be considered at higher Hb values but not exceeding 120 g/L. In high-risk paTents, including those with asymptomaTc ischaemic heart disease, treatment iniTaTon with ESA should be started at Hb values between 90 and 100 g/L to maintain a Hb value ∼ 100 g/L during maintenance therapy (16) .
- NICE guidelines on managing anaemia in people with CKD suggest maintaining the 'aspiraTonal' Hb range between 100 and 120 g/L for adults (17) . The raTonale behind choosing a wide target Hb

<!-- image -->

range (100-120 g/L) for this guideline is that when the target Hb level is narrow (i.e. 10 g/L), variability in achieved Hb levels around the target is high, the fracTon of prevalent paTents with achieved Hb levels within the target range is low and ESA dose TtraTon is required frequently during maintenance therapy.

It is also suggested to consider accepTng Hb levels below the lower limit of the target range, if the target range cannot be achieved despite high, escalaTng doses of ESAs. High ESA doses are defined as: over 175 IU/kg per week for people receiving haemodialysis, over 125 IU/kg per week for peritoneal dialysis paTents and over 100IU/kg per week for pre-dialysis paTents (17) .

- The health economics of anaemia therapy using ESAs has been subject to a NICE systemaTc review (17) which concludes that treaTng to a target Hb 100-120 g/L is cost effecTve in HD paTents (17) . Table 1 summarises the mean Hb data for prevalent UK dialysis paTents from the 24 th (2020) UK Renal Registry Reports (18) .
- The Medicines and Healthcare products Regulatory Agency (MHRA) guidance (2007) notes that using ESAs to achieve Hb levels greater than 120 g/L is associated with an increased risk of death and serious cardiovascular events in people with CKD. The MHRA advises that Hb levels greater than this should be avoided, and that paTents should be monitored closely to ensure that the lowest approved dose of ESA is used to provide adequate control of the symptoms of anaemia. Use of ESAs to achieve Hb levels greater than 120 g/L is not consistent with UK markeTng authorisaTons for ESAs. Informed consent should be obtained and documented (19) .
