## Guideline 3.6 - Treatment of Anaemia - Choice of ESA:

We recommend that the choice of ESA is based on local availability and cost of ESAs. (1B)

## Rationale

Many studies have been published comparing different ESA products against each other when used at different dosing intervals, by different routes of administraTon and in different paTent groups. (1, 2) All the available products are efficacious when administered according to the manufacturers' recommendaTons. The choice of ESA will be dependent upon the clinician and paTent agreeing a management plan and local supply arrangements (2, 3) .

## Guideline 3.7 - Treatment of Anaemia - Ini9al ESA Dose:

We suggest that the iniTal ESA dose should be determined by the individual's Hb level, the desired target Hb range, the observed rate of increase in Hb level and clinical circumstances. (2B)

## Rationale

For iniTaTon of ESA therapy, several points are to be considered:

- Type(s) of licensed ESAs available
- IniTal ESA dose
- ESA dose adjustment: dose required for Hb correcTon vs. maintenance.
- Route of ESA administraTon
- Frequency of ESA administraTon that best fit paTent requirements and achieve maximal convenience
- Monitoring for the anTcipated response in terms of Hb rise, rate of Hb rise, possible adverse effect (e.g. hypertension).

In general, the aim of iniTal ESA therapy is to achieve a rate of increase in Hb levels of 10 to 20 g/L per month. This rate of rise is considered safe as evidenced from intervenTonal trials on people who are ESA naïve (1-3) . In CKD paTents with iniTal Hb levels less than target range, these trials have shown the mean iniTal rate of Hb level increase to be in the range of 7 to 25 g/L in the first 4 weeks. This rate of Hb increase is affected by the populaTon, iron status, iniTal ESA dose, and the frequency and route of ESA administraTon.

2. Eschbach JW, Kelly MR, Haley NR et al. Treatment of the anaemia of progressive renal failure with recombinant human erythropoieTn. N Engl J Med.1989 Jul 20; 321(3):158-63.

## Guideline 3.8 - Treatment of Anaemia with ESA Therapy - Route of Administra9on:

We suggest that the route of ESA administraTon should be determined by the CKD grade, paTent preference, treatment seLng, efficacy, safety, and class of ESA used. Subcutaneous (SC) use is preferable in people who are not receiving HD to avoid puncture of peripheral veins. Subcutaneous administraTon of short-acTng ESA preparaTons may be preferred in people receiving haemodialysis due to improved ESA efficacy at the EPO receptor, allowing for smaller doses to be used. However, convenience and choice may favour intravenous (IV) administraTon in people on HD. (2B)

## Rationale

In the outpaTent seLng, SC administraTon is the only rouTnely feasible route of administraTon for non-HD CKD paTents. For HD paTents, either SC or IV administraTon is feasible. Among short-acTng ESAs, subcutaneous administraTon is associated with approximately 30% reducTon in dose requirements compared to that of IV administraTon for the same target Hb outcome. This has been proven in a large mulT-centre RCT on people on long term HD who had their haematocrit maintained within target range while on epoeTn α either via SC or IV route. ParTcipants were then randomised to IV or SC route. Upon randomisaTon, ESA doses were first decreased to allow haematocrit levels to decrease to less than target range. Doses were Ttrated upward to again achieve target haematocrit levels, and then were adjusted to maintain haematocrit in the target range during a 26-week maintenance phase. Among 107 people who completed the trial, those assigned to SC route showed 27% lower ESA doses than those assigned to IV administraTon (1) . However, not all parTcipants showed a dose decrease aVer conversion from IV to SC, and some paTents showed a dose increase. Among long-acTng agents, efficacy of SC administraTon appears to be equivalent to that of IV route at the examined dosing frequencies (2-5) .

2. Locatelli F, Canaud B, Giacardy F, et al. Treatment of anaemia in dialysis paTents with unit dosing of darbepoeTn α at a reduced dose frequency relaTve to recombinant human erythropoieTn (rHuEpo). Nephrol Dial Transplant 2003; 18:362-369.

## Guideline 3.9 - Treatment of Anaemia with ESA Therapy - Frequency of Administra9on:

We suggest that the frequency of administraTon should be determined by the CKD grade, preferences of treated populaTon, treatment seLng and class of ESA. Less frequent administraTon using long-acTng ESAs may be the treatment of choice in people with CKD not on haemodialysis. (2B)

## Rationale

The frequency of ESA administraTon should be determined by the CKD treatment seLng and the class of ESA. Maximum efficacy is achieved by using the dosing intervals that are ESA class specific. In people receiving HD prescribed SC short-acTng ESA therapy, ESA efficacy is maximal when the drug is given thrice weekly. Short-acTng ESA efficacy decreases and dose requirement increases, when the dosing frequency is extended from thrice-weekly to once-weekly administraTon (1) . Increasing the Tme interval between dosages of long-acTng ESAs could also result in an increase in dose requirements (2) . Doses and dosing intervals should be tailored to each individual person's response to therapy. Use of algorithms may assist in opTmising therapy (3) .

## References

1. Tolman C, Richardson D, BartleX C et al. Structured conversion from thrice weekly to weekly erythropoieTc regimens using a computerized decision-support system: A randomized clinical study. J Am Soc Nephrol 2005; 16:1463-1470.
2. Jadoul M, Vanrenterghem Y, Foret M et al. DarbepoeTn α administered once monthly maintains haemoglobin levels in stable dialysis paTents. Nephrol Dial Transplant 2004;19:898-903.
3. Lines SW, Lindley EJ, TaXersall JE and Wright MJ. A predicTve algorithm for the management of anaemia in haemodialysis paTents based on ESA pharmacodynamics: beXer results for less work. Nephrol Dial Transplant 2012; 27: 2425-2429.

## Guideline 3.10 - Treatment of Anaemia with ESA Therapy - ESA Dose Adjustments:

We recommend that adjustments to ESA doses should be considered when Hb is &lt;105 or &gt;115 g/L in adults, young people and children aged 2 years and older. (1A)

## Guideline 3.10.1

We suggest these thresholds for intervenTon should achieve a populaTon distribuTon centered on a mean of 110 g/L with a range of 100-120 g/L. (2B)

## Guideline 3.10.2

We suggest in children younger than 2 years to keep the Hb level within the aspiraTonal range, do not wait unTl Hb levels are outside the aspiraTonal range before adjusTng treatment (for example, take acTon when Hb levels are within 5 g/L of the range's limits). (not graded)

## Guideline 3.10.3

We suggest that ESA doses should ideally be decreased rather than withheld when a downward adjustment of Hb level is desirable. (2B)

## Guideline 3.11 - Treatment of Anaemia with ESA Therapy - Specific Situa9ons:

We suggest that ESA administraTon in ESA-dependent people should conTnue during acute illness, surgical procedures or any other cause of hospitalisaTon, unless there is a clear contra-indicaTon (for example, acute stroke or vascular access thrombosis). (2B)

## Rationale for Guidelines 3.10 and 3.11

The NICE Guidelines for anaemia management in chronic kidney disease recommend an 'aspiraTonal' Hb of 100-120 g/L. It is anTcipated that if a populaTon Hb distribuTon is centred on this outcome with a mean of 110 g/L, then 85% of the populaTon will have Hb &gt; 100 g/L (1) .

In people receiving HD, withholding ESA doses for Hb levels greater than the target range is associated with subsequent downward Hb excursions oVen to levels less than target Range (2) . The Tme between withholding ESA doses and return of Hb to target range is variable and unpredictable. In HD paTents with Hb values greater than 140 g/L, the median Tme for Hb to return to 120 g/L or less aVer withholding of a SC-administered ESA is 7-9 weeks. The difference between withholding long and short acTng ESAs on the rate of Hb reducTon is not significant (3) .

ESA dose adjustment may be higher during iniTaTon (or TtraTon aVer switching between different ESAs) than maintenance phases of ESA therapy. In a randomized double-blind trial comparing a short-acTng ESA with a long-acTng ESA in people on haemodialysis previously receiving epoeTn α, dose adjustments were made in 25% increments or decrements of the baseline dose, aiming to maintain individual Hb concentraTons within a range of 90 to 130 g/L (4) . Approximately 70% of paTents required dose adjustment in the 20-week TtraTon period, and 50% required dose adjustment during the 8-week maintenance period.

1. NaTonal CollaboraTng Centre for Chronic CondiTons, Royal College of Physicians. Chronic kidney disease: assessment and management. 2021. NaTonal InsTtute for Clinical Excellence.

## Guideline 3.12 - Cau9on in Prescribing ESA in Certain People with CKD:

We suggest exerTng cauTon while prescribing ESA therapy in people with CKD with a history of stroke, or malignancy, parTcularly in those with acTve malignancy when cure is the anTcipated outcome. (2C)

## Rationale

In the TREAT trial, there was an increased risk of stroke in the high ESA group (HR 1.92; 95% CI 1.382.68): 5.0% of the high Hb group had a stroke compared to 2.6% in the placebo group (P&lt;0.001).

<!-- image -->

<!-- image -->

Venous thrombo-embolic events occurred significantly more frequently in the high Hb arm (2.0%) compared to the placebo arm (1.1%, P=0.02) (1) .

A post-hoc analysis of TREAT trial showed that: 7.4% of those with a history of malignancy at baseline died from cancer in the ESA arm compared to 0.6% in the placebo arm (P=0.002) (2) .

In a meta-analysis comparing possible adverse events related to ESA therapy, The higher Hb concentraTons in ESA treated people with NDD-CKD increased risk for stroke (RR 1.51, 95% CI: 1.032.21), hypertension (RR 1.67, 95% CI: 1.31-2.12), and vascular access thrombosis (RR 1.33; 95% CI: 1.16-1.53), and possibly the risk of death (RR 1.09; 95% CI: 0.99-1.20), serious cardiovascular events (RR 1.15, 95% CI: 0.98-1.33) or ESRD (RR 1.08; 95% CI: 0.97-1.20). (3) However, the risk of stroke was independent of Hb level or dose of ESA suggesTng other factors such as iron deficiency (4) .

PaTents with neoplasia who received ESA in randomised clinical trials had an increased risk of tumor progression and reduced overall survival compared with study controls (5) .

The MHRA advised that r-HuEPOs should not be given to people with cancer who do not fulfil the criteria in the authorised cancer indicaTons, and that paTents should be monitored closely to ensure that the lowest approved dose of r-HuEPO is used to adequately control of symptoms of anaemia (5) .

The joint guideline from the American Society of Clinical Oncology and the American Society of Haematology (6) recommends using ESA therapy with great cauTon in people with acTve malignancy, parTcularly when cure is the anTcipated outcome.

NICE evaluated the efficacy and safety of ESA in treaTng anaemia in people with cancer receiving chemotherapy (7) . Although NICE researchers idenTfied 23 randomised controlled trials evaluaTng the effecTveness and safety of erythropoiesis-sTmulaTng agents (ESAs) for treaTng cancer treatment-related anaemia, NICE assessment focused only on trials that evaluated ESAs at a starTng dose reflecTng the current license (Hb &lt;100 g/L). In total 16 studies were included in the analysis of the outcome related to anaemia and 7 trials in the outcome related to overall survival. NICE analysis of available trials concluded that erythropoiesis-sTmulaTng agents are recommended, within their markeTng authorisaTons, as opTons for treaTng anaemia in people with cancer who are having chemotherapy. ESAs were effecTve in increasing haemoglobin concentraTons, improving haematological responses, reducing the need for blood transfusions and improving health-related quality of life, but that it could not assume that ESA treatment either prolonged or shortened survival compared with treatment without an ESA (7) .

## Guideline 3.13 - Monitoring of ESA Treatment - Haemoglobin during ESA Therapy:

We suggest that Hb concentraTon should be monitored every 2-4 weeks in the correcTon phase or aVer a dose adjustment and every 1-3 months for stable individuals in the maintenance phase of ESA treatment. More frequent monitoring will depend on clinical circumstances. (2B)

## Rationale

It is important to closely monitor Hb response to treatment to monitor for possible adverse events and plan ESA dose modificaTon. More frequent Hb monitoring may be needed for people with unstable Hb, out of target Hb level, anTcipated Hb drop due to blood loss/haemolysis, infecTon or subopTmal dialysis. The response to ESA therapy varies widely between different paTent groups and individuals within those groups. In addiTon, an individual's response can vary greatly dependent on other clinical variables. During ESA iniTaTon therapy, aVer drug dose adjustments or changes in an individual's clinical condiTon, more frequent monitoring is advised in order that under-treatment (ongoing anaemia) and overtreatment (rapidly rising Hb/hypertension or polycythaemia) may be avoided (1-3) .

## References

2. Locatelli F, Aljama P , Barany P et al. Revised European best pracTce Guidelines for the management of anaemia in paTents with chronic renal failure. Nephrol Dial Transplant 2004; 19 Suppl 2: ii1 47.

## Guideline 3.14 - Monitoring of ESA Treatment - Resistance to ESA Therapy:

We recommend that inadequate response ('resistance') to ESA therapy is defined as failure to reach the target Hb range despite SC epoeTn dose &gt;300 IU/kg/week (450 IU/kg/week IV epoeTn), or darbepoeTn dose &gt;1.5 mcg/kg/week, or equivalent dose of methoxy ethylene glycol epoeTn beta following invesTgaTon and treatment of other causes. (1A)

## Guideline 3.14.1

We suggest clinicians consider accepTng lower aspiraTonal haemoglobin target range in those on high, escalaTng ESA doses with inadequate response or consider alternaTve therapy such as a trial use of HIF-PHI for non-dialysis dependent CKD (NDD-CKD). (2C)

## Rationale

Extensive publicaTons are available on the topic of resistance to ESA therapy including the Revised European Best PracTce Guidelines (1) which defines ESA resistance as above. Failure to respond at an earlier stage in therapy should however raise suspicion of ESA resistance.

Comparison of the individual Hb outcome achieved and the dose of ESA used can provide a useful way of highlighTng individuals that are ESA resistant during local unit audit (2, 3) . ESA therapy is efficacious in most people. However, many condiTons and treatment variables can cause or explain apparent resistance to ESA therapy. Adequate invesTgaTon and management of these underlying condiTons is crucial in achieving saTsfactory outcome haemoglobin values as well as requiring therapy in their own right.

It is suggested to consider accepTng lower Hb target levels in individuals who require high escalaTng doses of ESA, where the aspiraTonal range cannot be achieved. High doses are defined as:

- &gt;175 IU/kg per week for people on HD
- &gt;125 IU/kg per week for people on PD
- &gt;100 IU/kg per week for people who have CKD and aren't dialysis dependent (NDD-CKD)

Individuals on these doses, should start to receive invesTgaTons into potenTal other reversible causes of low haemoglobin levels (4) .

Suggested approaches to invesTgate ESA hypo-responsiveness:

- Check adherence If poor, aXempt to improve (if self-injecTon)
- Check reTculocyte count If &gt;100,000/microL (&gt; 3 %), look for blood loss or hemolysis: endoscopy, colonoscopy, hemolysis screen
- Check serum vitamin B12 and folate, if low, replenish
- Check iron status, if low, replenish iron
- Check serum PTH, if elevated, manage hyperparathyroidism
- Check Serum CRP, if elevated, check for and treat infecTon or inflammaTon
- Check efficiency of dialysis. If underdialysed, improve dialysis efficiency
- Check if on ACEi/ARB use If yes, consider reducing dose or disconTnuing drug

<!-- image -->

- Perform bone marrow biopsy and manage any condiTon diagnosed e.g., dyscrasia, infiltraTon, fibrosis (5) .

## Guideline 3.15 - Monitoring of ESA Treatment - Evalua9on for ESA Induced Pure Red Cell Aplasia:

We recommend that a diagnosis of ESA induced pure red cell aplasia (PRCA) should be considered whenever a person receiving long term ESA therapy (&gt;8 weeks) develops all the following (1A):

- A sudden decrease in Hb concentraTon at the rate of 5 to 10 g/L per week or requirement of transfusions at the rate of approximately 1 to 2 per week
- Normal platelet and white cell counts
- Absolute reTculocyte count less than 10,000/µl
- High serum ferriTn

## Guideline 3.15.1

We recommend that all ESA therapy should be stopped in people who develop ESA induced PRCA. (1A)

## Guideline 3.15.2

We recommend that individuals who remain transfusion dependent aVer withdrawing ESA therapy should be treated with immunosuppressant medicaTons guided by the level of anT EPO anTbodies. (1B)

## Guideline 3.15.3

We do not recommend rouTne screening for anT-erythropoieTn anTbodies among people with CKD regularly treated with erythropoiesis sTmulaTng agents. (1B)

## Rationale

AnT-erythropoieTn anTbody associated pure red cell aplasia (PRCA) is a very rare cause of resistance characterised by

- transfusion dependency
- low reTculocyte count (&lt;1%)
- lack of proerythroid progenitor cells in the bone marrow
- neutralising anT-erythropoieTn anTbodies (1) .

ESA induced PRCA is a very rare condiTon, with the overall incidence of reported cases between 1989 and June 2004 was 1.6 per 10,000 paTent-years of subcutaneous exposure (2) , and 0.02 per

<!-- image -->

10,000 paTent-years of intravenous exposure (3) . Nevertheless, most reported cases of anTerythropoieTn anTbody-associated PRCA have occurred in people with CKD who have received the drug subcutaneously (4,5,6) .

Pure red cell aplasia (PRCA) due to anT-erythropoieTn (EPO) anTbodies should be suspected in an individual who has previously responded to EPO if the haemoglobin (Hb) level declines by &gt;20 g/L per month or the reTculocyte count is &lt;20,000 /uL (5) .

PRCA is specifically characterized by the following clinical features (6) :

- A drop in Hb level of &gt;7 to 10 g/L per week without transfusions or transfusion requirement of at least one unit per week to maintain adequate Hb, despite conTnued use of ESA at high doses.
- Markedly reduced reTculocyte count (&lt;10,000 /uL).
- Normal platelet and white blood cell count.
- Elevated serum transferrin saturaTon and serum ferriTn.
- Rarely, allergic urTcarial skin reacTons at sites of earlier subcutaneous EPO injecTons have been described (7) .

## The diagnosis of PRCA is established by:

- Bone marrow examinaTon: which confirms severe hypoplasia of erythroid precursors (&lt;5%).
- The presence of anT-erythropoieTn anTbodies:
- o There are several available tests to detect anTbodies to erythropoieTn, with varying sensiTviTes and specificiTes (8) .
- o People with suspected ESA induced PRCA who test posiTve using binding anTbodies should have the diagnosis confirmed with the definiTve tesTng for neutralising anTbodies (9) .

## There are several tests that can be used. These include:

- radioimmunoprecipitation assay
- electrochemiluminescence bridging enzyme-linked immunosorbent assay
- surface plasmon resonance-based immunoassay.

The most validated test is the radioimmunoprecipitation assay. This test is very sensitive and can detect the presence of high-affinity anti-EPO IgG at concentrations as low as 10 ng/ml. This test is not currently available within NHS but can be organised with the industry as a part of post-marketing pharmacovigilance arrangement with the PRCA Rare Disease Group.

ESA induced PRCA is an immune mediated process. While spontaneous remissions aVer cessaTon of EPO therapy have been reported, immunosuppressive therapy is usually needed in most cases (10) . One study evaluated 170 CKD paTents who developed epoeTn-associated PRCA (11) . Of the 34 people who received epoeTn aVer the onset of PRCA, 56% recovered epoeTn responsiveness; the highest rate of epoeTn responsiveness was observed among those who had no detectable anTerythropoieTn anTbodies at the Tme of epoeTn administraTon (89%). The study also reported that the highest recovery rates were among those treated with immunosuppressive therapy, parTcularly a combinaTon of cyclophosphamide and prednisone (11) . Other opTons such as rituximab, danazol or even plasma exchange may be considered.

<!-- image -->

Verhelst et al (12) compared various immunosuppressive agents in 37 people with anTbody mediated PRCA compared to 10 with no treatment and found benefit with cyclophosphamide, plasma exchange and ciclosporin and also transplantaTon.

Given these data, it is advisable that retreatment with ESA may be considered in people with a history of PRCA only if anT-EPO anTbody level is no longer detectable. In addiTon, if epoeTn therapy is to be reconsidered for these people, only the intravenous rather than the subcutaneous route should be considered for drug administraTon.

ESA induced PRCA is now part of RaDaR, the UK rare disease registry (13) .

## Guideline 3.16 - Monitoring of ESA treatment - Hypertension during ESA therapy:

We recommend that blood pressure should be monitored in all people receiving ESAs and, if present, hypertension be treated by volume removal and/or hypotensive drugs. (1A)

## Rationale

Hypertension is the most common complicaTon in CKD and can be aggravated by ESA treatment (1) . Earlier studies demonstrated higher incidence rates of hypertension though ESA doses used were higher and Hb responses faster in these trials. It is now more common to start at low doses and increase gradually according to response. The commonest cause of hypertension in CKD is not ESA therapy.

ExacerbaTon of hypertension in paTent on ESA therapy may be associated with polycythaemia or rapidly rising haemoglobin levels. These complicaTons should be looked for in paTents with hypertension but in the absence of these complicaTng factors and in the absence of severe hypertension, ESA therapy can usually conTnue. Hypertension should be adequately controlled prior to iniTaTng ESA therapy. ESA therapy should be disconTnued in malignant hypertension.

## References

1. Horl WH, Jacobs C, Macdougall IC et al. European Best PracTce Guidelines 14-16: inadequate response to epoeTn. Nephrol Dial Transplant 2000; 15 Suppl 4: 43-50.

## 4. Treatment of Anaemia with HIF-PHI Agents (Guidelines 4.1-4.9)

## Guideline 4.1 - Treatment of Anaemia - HIF-PHI Agents

We recommend that treatment with HIF-PHI agents should be offered aVer iron repleTon, to people with symptomaTc anaemia (Hb &lt;105 g/L) of CKD (stages 3-5 (eGFR &lt;60 ml/min/1.73m 2 )) who are not receiving dialysis at the start of therapy and who are likely to benefit in terms of quality of life and physical funcTon and to avoid blood transfusion; especially in people considered suitable for transplantaTon. (1B)

## Guideline 4.1.1 - Treatment of Anaemia - DD-CKD and HIF-PHI Agents

We suggest that that treatment with HIF-PHI agents should be considered aVer iron repleTon, to people with DD-CKD and symptomaTc anaemia (Hb &lt;105 g/L) who are likely to benefit in terms of quality of life and physical funcTon and to avoid blood transfusion; especially in people considered suitable for transplantaTon. (2B)

## Guideline 4.1.2 - Treatment of Anaemia - Pa9ents intolerant to ESA

We suggest that that treatment with HIF-PHI agents should be considered as an opTon, aVer iron repleTon, to people who are intolerant to ESA therapy. (2C)

## Guideline 4.1.3 - Choosing between ESA and HIF-PHI therapy for people with non-dialysis dependent CKD and DD-CKD

We suggest, when deciding between ESA and HIF-PHI therapy for people with non-dialysis dependent CKD or DD-CKD, considering the preference of the person with anaemia of CKD, or, where appropriate, their family or carers, the cost of local drug supply, nursing and administraTon costs and previous treatment with ESA or HIF-PHI. (2B)

## Guideline 4.2 - Treatment of Anaemia

We suggest that HIF-PHI administraTon in HIF-PHI-dependent people should conTnue during acute illness, surgical procedures or any other cause of hospitalisaTon, unless there is a clear contraindicaTon such as accelerated hypertension or thrombosis. (2C)

<!-- image -->

## Rationale

Hypoxia-inducible factor-prolyl hydroxylase inhibitors (HIF-PHI therapy) also known as HIF stabilisers have emerged as a groundbreaking class of compounds that play a pivotal role in regulaTng various cellular processes. These oxygen-responsive heterodimeric transcripTon factors, known as HIFs (hypoxia-inducible factors), are integral to the cellular response to hypoxia, orchestraTng a cascade of events that influence vital physiological funcTons (1) . HIFs are parTcularly noteworthy for their role in managing oxygen homeostasis through their involvement in the producTon of endogenous erythropoieTn (EPO) and iron regulaTon within the body (1-3) .

Under hypoxic condiTons, such as those encountered at high alTtudes or in cases of anaemia, HIFs acTvate the transcripTon of EPO genes within the kidneys and liver, promoTng erythropoiesis to enhance oxygen delivery to Tssues (1-2) . AddiTonally, HIFs mediate the expression of genes involved in iron transport, uptake, and absorpTon. These genes contribute to the efficient uTlisaTon of iron, a vital element for mulTple physiological processes including oxygen transport, extracTon, energy producTon, and DNA synthesis (1-3) .

Erythroferrone (ERFE) is the main erythroid regulator of hepcidin. When erythropoieTn is released from the kidney it sTmulates the producTon of new red blood cells and also increases the synthesis of ERFE in bone marrow erythroblasts. Increased ERFE then suppresses hepcidin synthesis, thereby mobilizing cellular iron stores for use in heme and haemoglobin synthesis. The mechanism by which hepcidin is decreased by HIF-PHI therapy has been aXributed primarily to increased producTon of erythroferrone due to increased haematopoiesis (4) . Hepcidin is a pepTde hormone that modulates the levels of ferroporTn - an iron-export channel found on the surface of cells in the duodenum, liver, and macrophages. Hepcidin's inhibitory acTon on ferroporTn suppresses both intesTnal iron absorpTon and iron release from macrophages, thereby influencing the overall iron balance in the body (5) . The connecTon between HIFs and hepcidin is significant; systemic acTvaTon of HIFs prompts an increase in endogenous EPO producTon and iron uTlisaTon by erythroblasts. This intricate interacTon subsequently downregulates hepcidin producTon in the liver, promoTng enhanced intesTnal iron absorpTon and mobilisaTon from iron stores (6-9) . This orchestrated interplay forms a sophisTcated regulatory network that maintains iron homeostasis, ensuring opTmal funcToning of various biological processes.

In the presence of oxygen, prolyl hydroxylase enzymes hydroxylate the oxygen-regulated HIF-α subunit, thereby targeTng it for proteasomal degradaTon (10) . HIF-PHI therapy sTmulates erythropoiesis primarily by upregulaTng endogenous EPO producTon, thereby promoTng red blood cell producTon and amelioraTng anaemia. Clinical studies have consistently demonstrated the efficacy and safety of HIF-PHI therapy in addressing the anaemia associated with CKD (11-14) .

When there is a reducTon of blood oxygen levels, prolyl hydroxylaTon and degradaTon of HIF-α subunits are inhibited, resulTng in its cellular accumulaTon and dimerisaTon with the HIF-β subunits and this heterodimer acts as the transcripTon factor for the EPO gene (15-16) . Prolyl hydroxylaTon can be pharmacologically inhibited by oral HIF-PHI therapy (17-18) , which sTmulates erythropoiesis, largely by increasing endogenous EPO producTon from increased transcripTon of EPO genes.

<!-- image -->

<!-- image -->

Phase three trials have confirmed the efficacy and safety of this class of drug. At the Tme of wriTng, only Roxadustat is available and approved in the UK and should be considered in people with CKD not on dialysis but can be conTnued when the person progresses to dialysis therapy based on NICE guidance. The drug can be used in people receiving dialysis according to the SmPC but this has not been reviewed by NICE for this purpose (from increased transcripTon of EPO genes).

Health related quality of life in the trials to date have given variable results. Some trials have suggested improvement in Health-related quality of life in comparison to ESA therapy but these are confounded by changes in haemoglobin concentraTons (19, 20) . Others suggest no significant change in health-related quality of life measures or funcTonal health scores (10-13) .

'PotenTal' clinical and pracTcal benefits of HIF-PHI therapy include:

- Reduced exposure to high peak serum EPO concentraTons
- Increased endogenous EPO/erythropoieTn producTon
- Enhanced enteric iron absorpTon, mobilisaTon and iron uTlisaTon based on secondary outcome data.
- Possible reducTon in intravenous iron frequency and requirements
- Oral route of administraTon

## Rationale for Treatment with HIF-PHI Agents (Guidelines 4.1-4.9)

Published trials confirm that the six HIF-PHI agents with data are superior to placebo and noninferior to ESAs in increasing and maintaining haemoglobin (Hb) concentraTon among people with NDD-CKD and DD-CKD (1-33) . Large, randomised trials have demonstrated that Roxadustat (8-19) , Vadadustat (20-23) , and Daprodustat (1-7) are superior to placebo and/or non-inferior to ESAs in correcTng and/or maintaining Hb at target levels in NDD-CKD and incident and prevalent people with DD-CKD. These findings have also been seen with Molidustat, Enarodustat, and Desidustat (24-33) . The Hb response when using HIF-PHI therapy is dose-dependent and there are variaTons depending on the molecule used and trial protocol. Some molecules depending on the starTng doses used, increased the Hb more rapidly than others. Rates of blood transfusion are similar among paTents receiving HIF-PHI therapy versus ESA therapy and lower than among those receiving placebo in randomised trials.

<!-- image -->

Several meta-analyses have confirmed the clinical findings from these RCTs (34-40) . A recent metaanalysis of the phase 3 RCTS comparing HIF-PHI therapy to acTve comparator has confirmed the efficacy of this class of drug in both people on dialysis (both peritoneal and haemodialysis) and not on dialysis (41) . A total of 26 trials were included in this recent analysis with over 24, 000 parTcipants. The average length of follow-up was 16.5 months (range 6-42 months) and the mean baseline eGFR ranged from 15.9-22.1 ml/min/1,73m 2 . The mean age was 67.1 years (range 48-72 years). The authors found a staTsTcally higher change in Hb levels from baseline between HIF-PHI therapy at the dose selected compared to ESA of 1.0 g/L (95% CI 0.2-1.7) favouring HIF-PHI use but perhaps not clinically significant. There was no difference in those reaching the pre-specified Hb target (OR 1.04; 95% CI 0.88-1.22) (41) .

Roxadustat, the first in class HIF-PHI to receive NICE guidance and SmPC recommendaTon has a halflife of around 15 hours in people with CKD, is orally bioavailable, leads to an increase in EPO levels aVer oral administraTon and lasts for up to 48 hours post-dose administraTon. Its efficacy and safety have been examined in eight randomised controlled trials involving 9600 paTents (8-19) .

The results of trials that included people treated with haemodialysis (HD) and peritoneal dialysis (PD) and single-arm trials among paTents treated with PD (19) have shown that HIF-PHI therapy is at least as effecTve among those receiving PD versus HD.

## Guideline 4.3 - Treatment of Anaemia with HIF- PHI therapy - Target Haemoglobin:

We suggest that people over the age of 18 years with NDD-CKD on HIF-PHI therapy should achieve a Hb between:

- 100 and 120 g/L in adults and young people similar to ESA therapy (2B)
- In children and those younger than 2 years of age no data is currently available (not graded)

## Guideline 4.3.1

We suggest that people over the age of 18 years with DD-CKD on HIF-PHI therapy should achieve a Hb between:

<!-- image -->

- 100 and 120 g/L in adults and young people similar to ESA therapy but this may depend on the choice of HIF-PHI where a Hb of 100-110 g/|L is recommended. (2B)
- In children and those younger than 2 years of age no data is currently available

## Rationale

Current targets which aim for parTal correcTon of Hb are based on clinical trials conducted more than a decade ago (1-3) . These landmark trials compared higher versus lower Hb targets achieved using ESAs, in which major adverse cardiovascular events (MACE), mortality, and thromboTc events were more common among people assigned to the higher of the Hb targets (1-3) . In addiTon, one trial comparing a high Hb target with placebo (and a conservaTve rescue strategy) in people with NDDCKD and diabetes found an increased rate of strokes (4) . The current HIF-PHI trials compared with acTve comparator used standard Hb targets based on ESA therapy. These varied depending on the country with lower targets in the USA compared to Europe and the UK. No HIF-PHI trials to date involving people in the UK or Europe have compared Hb normalisaTon or near-normalisaTon with the currently recommended lower Hb targets for people with CKD.

For people with symptomaTc anaemia associated with non-dialysis dependent chronic kidney disease at the Tme of treatment iniTaTon, HIF-PHI therapy is recommended by NICE (5) as a treatment opTon and is comparable to ESA therapy. NICE recommends that the choice between a HIF-PHI and ESA should be made in discussion with the individual.

In determining target Hb guidelines it is important to assess potenTal benefits (such as possible improved survival, improvement in health-related quality of life (HRQoL) and avoidance of transfusion requirement and hospitalisaTon) vs. potenTal harms (increased mortality and vascular events) and paTent choice (6, 7) .

The randomised open-label phase 3 ESA-controlled DOLOMITES trial in NDD-CKD, included 28 countries and the UK reported that the safety of the HIF-PHI was comparable to the ESA's in the majority of safety variables in people with NDD-CKD (8) . They also reported no evidence of increased cardiovascular safety or mortality risk when compared with ESA's (8) The study included NDD-CKD people with symptomaTc anaemia and stage 3, 4 or 5 CKD with Hb levels &lt;105 g/L at the start of treatment. NICE commiXee recommends treatment at a Hb of less than 100 g/L. (5) .

The NICE CommiXee reported that HIF-PHI therapy was non inferior to ESA therapy in all measures of quality of Life and that the DOLOMITES trial demonstrated that people with NDD-CKD required less IV iron than those treated with an ESA.

In the case of non-responsiveness, treatment with HIF-PHI should not be conTnued beyond 24 weeks aVer the start of treatment and other causes of anaemia be invesTgated (9) .

Meta analysis of RCTS comparing HIF-PHI with ESA therapy have also demonstrated that there was no significant difference in adjudicated MACE events (10 trials) (1.00, 95% CI 0.94-1.07). For MACE+ again no difference was seen between HIF-PHI and ESA therapy (1.01, 95% CI 0.95-1.06) even aVer sensiTvity analysis (10) . There was also no difference in the mortality rate, but one must be cauToned as the duraTon of follow up was relaTvely short.

Vadadustat, recently approved by the EMA has a recommended Hb range of 100- 110 g/L. The two RCTs, INNO 2 VATE 1 (n 369) and 2 (n=3554), on pooled analysis showed non inferiority to darbepoeTn

<!-- image -->

alpha during 52 weeks follow up (11, 12) . The populaTon consisted of 60% US and 40% non-US where the trail Hb ranges were 80 to 110 g/L and 90 to 120 g/L in non-US parTcipants. Overall MACE HR; was 0.96 with 95% CI: 0.83-1.11 and for the US parTcipants only it was HR 1.0 with 95% CI; 0.841.18. The current SmPC recommends a Hb target range of 100-110 g/L (13) . The comparaTve 100 paTent year data for vadadustat versus ESA therapy respecTvely were myocardial infarcTon 2.9 vs 2.9; Vascaulr access thrombosis 4.8 vs 3.9; stroke 1.1 vs 1.4; DVT 0.5 vs 0.6; PE 0.2 vs 0.3.

We recommend that the iniTal HIF-PHI dose should be based on the person's weight and Hb level and the observed rate of increase in Hb level and clinical circumstances. The appropriate dose should follow label recommendaTons unTl further data is available. (1B)

## Guideline 4.4.1

We suggest that the starTng HIF PHI dose should be lower for those who are ESA-naïve versus those who are not. (2B)

## Guideline 4.5 - Treatment of Anaemia with HIF-PHI therapy - Frequency of Administra9on:

We suggest that the frequency of administraTon should be determined by the response to therapy to maintain the desired Hb target range of 100-120g/L . (2B)

## Guideline 4.6 - Treatment of Anaemia with HIF-PHI Therapy - Dose Adjustments:

We suggest that adjustments to HIF-PHI doses should be considered when Hb is &lt;105 or &gt;115 g/L in adults to balance the benefit and safety to people given the current evidence base. (2B)

## Rationale for Guidelines 4.4-4.6

Currently only 1 HIF-PHI has been recommended in the UK by NICE for people who have stage 3 to 5 CKD with no iron deficiency and are not on dialysis at the start of HIF-PHI treatment. Compared with ESA these drugs work as well for anaemia management (1, 2) . NICE has therefore recommended that HIF-PHI can be used as an alternaTve to ESA for people with NDD-CKD stages 3 to 5, especially if they find self-injecTng difficult or painful or rely on others to give them their injecTons. StarTng doses are based on weight, and dose changes are based on response to treatment and changes in Hb levels. The correcTon phase lasts up to approximately 3 months from starTng treatment or unTl a stable Hb level of 100-120 g/L is achieved and stabilised. The maintenance phase starts immediately aVer the correcTon phase. Clinicians with experTse in this field have indicated that the decision whether to stop treatment depends on how well anaemia is managed. They stated that they would Ttrate the dose down if Hb levels reached approximately 125 g/L so that Hb levels stay within a 'presumed safe range' (that is, between 100 g/L and 120 g/L) rather than stopping treatment completely.

Adjustments to HIF-PHI doses should be considered when Hb is &lt;105 or &gt;115 g/L in adults to balance the benefit and safety to people given the current evidence base. These thresholds for intervenTon should achieve a populaTon distribuTon centred on a mean of 110 g/L with a range of 100-120 g/L, similar to ESA based therapy.

The intermiXent dosing strategy with HIF-PHI for the treatment of anaemia in people with chronic kidney disease was developed to maintain its effecTveness. This is the case for Roxadustat with a half life of 15 hours administered three Tmes per week, enabling HIF transcripTonal acTvity to return to baseline between doses, which results in the intermiXent inducTon of hypoxia-inducible target genes involved in erythropoiesis. However, for vadadustat which has a half-life of approximately 4.5 - 9.2 hours it is given daily.

The appropriate dose should follow label recommendaTons. We would suggest that starTng HIF PHI dose should be lower for those who are ESA-naïve versus those who are not. Based on the current Hb and the achieved change in Hb (typically over a 4-week period), the dosing in phase 3 trials was maintained or changed in stepwise fashion. Treatment was temporarily disconTnued when Hb exceeded 120 or 130 g/L in most studies (3-20) . It should be noted that Roxadustat is more than

<!-- image -->

90% protein bound so cauTon should be used when the drug is used in paTents with nephroTc syndrome due to the potenTally higher levels of free drug which may lead to adverse events and more rapid rise in haemoglobin.

## Guideline 4.7 - Prescribing HIF-PHI in Sub-Groups of People with CKD:

We recommend that HIF-PHI should be avoided or used cauTously in people with acTve malignancy. (1B)

## Guideline 4.7.1

We recommend that HIF-PHI should be avoided or used cauTously in people with autosomal dominant polycysTc kidney disease unTl further data is available. (1D)

## Guideline 4.7.2

We recommend that HIF-PHI should be avoided or used cauTously in people with a history of seizures. (1B)

## Guideline 4.7.3

We recommend cauTon in using HIF-PHI in people with uncontrolled hypertension . (1B)

## Guideline 4.7.4

We recommend cauTon in using HIF-PHI therapy in people with reTnopathy. (1D)

## Guideline 4.7.5

We recommend cauTon in people with a history of thromboTc events following the SmPC for contraindicaTons. (1A)

## Guideline 4.7.6

We suggest consideraTon of use of HIF-PHI in people with hyporesponsiveness to ESA therapy or underlying inflammaTon, but further high-quality randomised trials are needed to confirm its effecTveness. (2C)

## Rationale for certain specific groups (Guidelines 4.7)

## Malignancy

Hypoxia inducible factor-1 alpha (HIF-1α) is a key transcripTon factor in tumour progression (1) . HIF1α inhibitors are currently used in cancer treatment (1) .  GeneTc HIF acTvaTon is a central mechanism of tumorigenesis in people with the von Hippel-Lindau (VHL) disease and clear cell renal carcinomas (2) . These data suggest that HIF-PHI may potenTally increase the risk of iniTaTon/ progression of malignancy in people with CKD.  Data from the most recent metanalysis of over 24,000 people indicate the risk esTmate for malignancy was 7% lower in the HIF-PHI arm compared to ESA (nonsignificant RR 0.93; 95% CI 0.76 to 1.13) (3) . However, once again follow-up was relaTvely short- and longer-term data is needed to assess for the development of new cancers and impact on previous cancers. Those with established cancer or a history of cancer were excluded from all trials.

<!-- image -->

In the ASCEND-ND trial, cancer-related death or tumour progression or recurrence was numerically more common in those randomised to daprodustat (72 of 1937, 3.7%) versus darbepoeTn alfa (49 of 1933, 2.5%), with a relaTve risk of 1.47 (95% CI: 1.03- 2.10) (4) . Post hoc analyses that accounted for differenTal dosing frequency aXenuated this observed risk (4) . Molidustat in clinical trials also reported neoplasms in 9.8% of trial parTcipants in the molidustat group compared with 5.3% in the darbepoeTn group (5) .

There has been no consistent signal across the published HIF-PHI trials of an excess risk of malignancy-related adverse events, but since paTents with a history of recent or acTve malignancy were excluded from trials, it is difficult to conclude with confidence of the absence of a clinically relevant risk of the use of these drugs compared with ESAs. It is therefore advisable that the use of this product should be avoided or used cauTously in people with known acTve malignancy.

## Polycys9c Kidney Disease

People with CKD who have underlying PolycysTc Kidney Disease were excluded from trials using HIF PHI's because of the potenTal risk of malignant transformaTon of cysts. HIF acTvaTon occurs in polycysTc kidneys in humans and rodents and acTvaTon of the HIF-pathway has been shown to enhance cyst expansion in preclinical models. However, whether the use of HIF-PHI therapy to treat anaemia may enhance cyst growth remains to be further clarified. It has been postulated that cyst formaTon is related to chloride-dependent fluid secreTon into the cyst lumen (6) .

## Hypertension

Pre-clinical studies in healthy rats and rats with CKD demonstrated that HIF-PHI therapy generate significant dose-dependent blood-pressure lowering effects (7, 8) . However, so far, no significant blood pressure safety concerns have been reported in any HIF-PHI phase 3 programs.

## Re9nopathy

DiabeTc reTnopathy may be exacerbated potenTally by the effect of HIF-PHI therapy on neovascularisaTon (9) . There were few parTcipants with significant reTnopathy recruited to the current published phase 3 clinical trials. Data from a pooled Japanese analysis of trials in NDD-CKD and DDCKD using daprodustat did not report any increased risk for reTnal events or deterioraTon in disease (10) . One relaTvely large study, the SYMPHONY-ND study which compared enarodustat ESA therapy did show an increase in the levels of VEGF levels and an increase in reported reTnal adverse events (3.7 vs 0.9%) (11) . Other NDD-CKD trials have not shown increased risk of eye disease with HIF-PHI therapy (12, 13, 14, 15) .

In dialysis paTents the SYMPHONY-HD trial which examined vadadustat versus darbepoeTn did find an increased risk of reTnal adverse events (6.9 vs 3.5%) (16) . Again, in the ASCEND-ID trial of daprodustat compared to ESA therapy there was a reported increased incidence of eye disease (3.4 vs 0.79/100 paTent-years) (17) .

## Thromboembolic events

AdministraTon of HIF-PHI therapy has been associated with a higher risk of thromboTc events, including dialysis vascular access thrombosis compared with ESAs or placebo (8, 18) . The underlying mechanisms are complex and may be related to the rapid rate of rise in Hb for roxadustat. (19) In

<!-- image -->

addiTon, the impact of HIF-PHI on iron metabolism (upregulaTon of transferrin, (20) or interference of the coagulaTon system (increased expression of plasminogen acTvator inhibitor) may contribute to thromboTc risk (21) . Roxadustat showed an excess risk of thrombosis in both NDD-CKD (versus ESA) and DD-CKD (versus placebo) trials (22) . Vascular access thrombosis occurred in 1.5 versus 0.9/100 paTents years and DVT was 0.7 versus 0.2/100 paTents years (23) . A pooled analysis of roxadustat trials demonstrated higher risks were associated with the rate of Hb rise (24) . Lower doses of roxadustat, may lead to a slower rate of Hb rise, and thus ameliorate thrombosis risk while maintaining efficacy. ThromboTc risk with vadadustat were not noted in published data (25) . Daprodustat trials have not reported a significant excess risk of thrombosis compared with acTve comparator although there was a numerical increased risk of vascular access thrombosis (2.1% versus 1.5%) (4, 25, 26) .

Data from the meta-analysis from 17 of the trials reported on DVT and PE events and found no difference. Again, there was no difference in AVF thrombosis incidence (14 trials) (3) .

## Pa9ents hyporesponsive to ESAs

By lowering hepcidin levels, HIF-PHI therapy may theoreTcally be more effecTve in treaTng people who are hyporesponsive to ESAs because of chronic inflammaTon or funcTonal iron deficiency. Preliminary data from the randomised trials suggest that whereas higher doses of ESAs are needed for people with high C-reacTve protein (CRP) levels, this may not be true for HIF-PHI therapy. (27, 28) However, CRP concentraTons that were considered high in trial parTcipants were only slightly elevated, and sicker and more inflamed paTents may have been less likely to have been enrolled in trials of HIF-PHI therapy. Therefore, more evidence from randomised controlled trials are needed before recommendaTons for rouTne use in the populaTon can be made.

Although the use of HIF-PHI therapy in combinaTon with ESAs might theoreTcally be advantageous for people who are ESA hyporesponsive, there are no data available to support this strategy in clinical pracTce at present (29) . As with all drugs, there is a risk for drug-drug interacTons with the use of HIFPHI therapy, parTcularly in combinaTon with other oral agents.

## Children

There are insufficient data supporTng the use of HIF-PHI therapy in paediatric paTents with anaemia of CKD because people under the age of 18 years were excluded from all Phase 3 trials.

## Cardiovascular Disease

For MACE outcomes, all trials with a HIF-PHI met the non-inferiority margin set by the regulator except Vadadustat in people with NDD-CKD. However, there is bias in these trials due to the withdrawal of large number of people in both arms (over 20%). A recent meta-analysis has shown no difference as described previously (3) . A cochrane meta-analysis of 51 trials with almost 31,000 parTcipants with NDD-CKD or DD-CKD found no difference in CV death (RR 1.05: 95% CI 0.88 to 1.26); non-fatal MI (RR 0.91: 95% CI 0.76 to 1.00 or non-fatal stroke (RR 1.06: 95% CI 0.71 to 1.56) (30).  The secondary outcome studied in the DOLOMITES trial was the impact of roxadustat compared to the acTve comparator (darbepoeTn alfa) on CV safety, based on the number of major adverse cardiac events (26, 27) . The hazard raTo (HR) For MACE was 0.89; 95%; CI 0.60 to 1.33 and for MACE+ (a composite of MACE plus unstable angina or congesTve heart failure requiring

<!-- image -->

hospitalisaTon) was 0.93; 95% CI 0.65 to 1.32, indicaTng no increased risk with roxadustat compared to darbepoeTn alfa. In addiTon, there was no extension of the risk beyond the non-inferiority margin set by the regulator. It must be noted that in the dialysis paTents, there was a potenTal increase in risk in the pooled analysis of those paTents who switched therapy from ESA to Roxadustat (18, 19) .

Roxadustat was previous reviewed by the FDA for its potenTal use in CKD (29, 31) . Data submiXed included 3 separate trials comparing Roxadustat with placebo that were pooled for meta-analyses in the NDD-CKD (n = 4270) populaTon (32) . A fourth study comparing roxadustat to darbepoeTn alfa was analysed separately (DOLOMITES) (33) . The pooled analyses for roxadustat did not have prespecified agreed (FDA) noninferiority margins (32, 33) . Further this risk was accentuated during on-treatment sensiTvity analyses (as opposed to intenTon-to-treat analysis). This analysis was suggested by the FDA to minimise the effect of including unexposed person-Tmes or events that may not be affected by the intervenTon (18) . When assessing events occurring while people were on treatment and for one week aVer disconTnuaTon (on-treatment + 7 days analyses), 277 (7.2%) events were recorded in the roxadustat arm compared with 131 (5.6%) events in the placebo arm (HR 1.38; 95% CI: 1.111.70). However, there was higher dropout rates in the placebo compared to roxadustat arms, with potenTal for bias that may have disadvantaged roxadustat (33) .

Three studies of roxadustat compared to ESA involving people receiving dialysis (N=3880) were metaanalysed (33) . The analyses of the effect of roxadustat for MACE were discordant based on the analyTcal approach: in the primary, on-treatment + 7-day analyses, the risk of MACE was similar in the roxadustat and ESA groups: HR 1.02; 95% CI: 0.88-1.20. In the sensiTvity, on-treatment analysis, the HR for the risk of MACE in paTents treated with roxadustat versus ESA was 1.14; 95% CI: 1.001.30 (not staTsTcal significance for non-inferiority). A fourth trial conducted in Europe and not included in the pooled meta-analysis, permiXed the use of two different ESAs (epoeTn alfa or darbepoeTn alfa) as comparators, demonstrated a higher risk of death in roxadustat vs. ESA-treated paTents (8.9 per 100 paTent years (PY) vs. 6.3 per 100 PY; HR 1.54, 95% CI 1.04-2.28) (29) .

The differences observed in the stable DD populaTon maybe somewhat challenging to accurately interpret due to the different treatment strategies for the Roxadustat and the comparator arm. While in the comparator arm in the stable dialysis dependent CKD (SDD) studies the treatment was not changed (paTents conTnued ESA therapy) in the Roxadustat arm were switched from ESA therapy with inherent risks related to this change which created an imbalance between the two arms (35) . Several contribuTng factors may impact this risk, including treatment non-responsiveness, and converTng stable ESA treated people receiving dialysis.

In the case of non-responsiveness, treatment with roxadustat should not be conTnued beyond 24 weeks aVer the start of treatment. Conversion of people receiving dialysis otherwise stable on ESA treatment is only to be considered when there is a valid clinical reason and aVer a shared decision with the paTent on the risks and benefits. For stable ESA treated people with anaemia associated with CKD and not on dialysis, this risk could not be esTmated as these paTents have not been studied. A decision to treat these paTents with roxadustat should be based on a shared decision with the paTent of the benefits and risks for that parTcular individual paTent.'

<!-- image -->

In a published analysis of the four roxadustat trials in the DD-CKD populaTon (8) , MACE and MACE+  in the on-treatment plus 7 day analyses showed different results in incident vs. prevalent dialysis paTents, noted similar risk of MACE (HR 0.83; 95% CI: 0.61-1.13) and nominally lower risk of MACE+ (HR 0.76; 95% CI: 0.57-1.00) among incident dialysis paTents treated with roxadustat, whereas roxadustat was less favourable for MACE (HR 1.18; 95% CI: 1.00-1.38) and all-cause mortality (HR 1.23; 95% CI: 1.02-1.49) in prevalent dialysis paTents (33) .

Pooled results from two randomized trials involving NDD-CKD persons in which vadadustat was compared with darbepoeTn alfa were analysed (19) .  One trial involved ESA naïve paTents (n=1751) and the other involved paTents who were receiving acTve ESA therapy (n =1725). Pooled analysis showed that while vadadustat met its margin with respect to hematologic efficacy in each trial, it did not meet its pre-specified non inferiority margin (HR 1.3) with respect to MACE, defined as death from any cause, non-fatal myocardial infarcTon, or non-fatal stroke, with a higher risk of MACE in the vadadustat arm (25) . The excess risk was accounted for by non-fatal MI and death from noncardiovascular causes. Subgroup analyses found a regional difference in the study results, with the increased MACE risk observed in non-U.S. study sites (HR 1.30; 95% CI 1.05-1.62) but no difference in risk in the U.S. study sites (HR 1.06; 95% CI 0.87-1.29).

Two randomised trials involving DD-CKD paTents compared vadadustat with darbepoeTn alfa.  One trial involved prevalent (n = 3554) and the other incident (n = 369) parTcipants (34) . Pooled analysis of results from both trials showed similar MACE rates in the two arms and met non-inferiority (HR 0.96; 95% CI: 0.83-1.11) (34) .

A phase 3 trial assessed the safety and efficacy of daprodustat against an acTve comparator (darbepoeTn alfa) for the treatment in people with NDD-CKD. Daprodustat was non-inferior to the acTve comparator as it met the pre-specified non-inferiority margins of a HR of 1.25 in primary analyses (HR: 1.03; 95% CI: 0.89-1.19) (24) . However, in the sensiTvity on-treatment MACE analysis, which censored paTents at 28 days aVer the last dose, parTcipants randomised to daprodustat had a higher incidence of MACE than those randomised to ESA (14.1% vs. 10.5%, HR 1.40; 95% CI: 1.171.68).  The trial authors suggested that differences in the dosing frequency of daprodustat versus ESAs in this trial and differences in definiTons of treatment periods may have led to potenTal bias that disadvantaged daprodustat (24) .

Daprodustat was evaluated in randomised, open-label, phase 3 trial, in paTents with DD-CKD subjects using the acTve comparator of epoeTn alfa if they were receiving haemodialysis or darbepoeTn alfa if they were receiving peritoneal dialysis (5) . A total of 2964 paTents underwent randomizaTon. Daprodustat, compared to the acTve comparators, met the pre-specified noninferiority margin of 1.25 in primary analyses of the DD-CKD populaTons (DD: 0.93; 95% CI: 0.811.07). Similar conclusions were obtained from sensiTvity analysis.

Overall meta-analyses have reassured the short-term safety of these drugs in comparison to ESA therapy. Natale et al (30) found not difference in MACE and MACE+; similar to those from Takkavatakarn et al (37) . A meta-analysis limited to only non-dialysis and acTve comparator (9 studies with 9470 people) there was no difference in CV outcomes (3) .

<!-- image -->

More long-term data will be needed to reassure on the impact of HIF-PHI therapy and their potenTal off-target effects including the systemic response to their acTon via inhibiTon of alpha ketoglutarate which may have effects of cardiovascular and heart failure complicaTons (38) . Several studies are ongoning including comparing roxadustat combined with sacubitril/valsartan versus recombinant human erythropoieTn combined with ACEI or ARB in Chinese paTents with cardiorenal syndrome and anaemia (NCT05053893) and examining the safety and efficacy of roxadustat in the treatment of HF in paTents with CKD and anaemia (NCT05691257).

## Guideline 4.8 - Safety of HIF-PHI:

We suggest that cauTous use of HIF-PHI in people with CKD and either known CVD or thromboTc events and consideraTon of lower dose regimes to reduce rapid rises in Hb. (2C)

## Guideline 4.9 - Monitoring Response to HIF-PHI:

We recommend that Hb levels should be monitored every two weeks unTl the desired Hb target range of 100 to 120 g/L is achieved and stabilised, and every 4 weeks thereaVer, or as clinically indicated. (1B)

## Rationale

The recommendaTons are based on current SMPC guidance and the protocols from RCTs.

## 5. Treatment of Anaemia of CKD with Blood Transfusions (Guidelines 5.1 - 5.1.3)

## Guideline 5.1 Blood Transfusion in People with Anaemia of CKD

We recommend that in people with anaemia of CKD, especially those in whom kidney transplantaTon is an opTon, red blood cell transfusion should be avoided, if possible, to minimise the risk of allosensiTsaTon. (1A)

## Guideline 5.1.1

We recommend if red blood cell transfusion becomes essenTal (usually in the seLng of acute blood loss, acute haemolysis or severe sepsis) transfusion should be based on policies set by local transfusion guidelines rather than Hb target range for ESA therapy in chronic anaemia of CKD. (1B)

## Guideline 5.1.2

We suggest using single unit transfusion, where possible for stable non-bleeding people with CKD who clinically require transfusion. (2C)

## Guideline 5.1.3

We suggest that kidney transplant recipients, those on the transplant waiTng list or people on immunosuppressive therapy should receive only hepaTTs E negaTve blood components (all UK blood components are tested), but neither CMV negaTve nor irradiated blood is required. (2B)

## Rationale for Guideline 5.1

CKD results in chronic anaemia, the degree of which usually reflects the severity of CKD. As with any chronic anaemia, paTents tend to deal with this by various compensatory mechanisms. Blood transfusion is rarely an acute requirement except in emergencies such as acute blood loss, acute haemolysis or severe sepsis/inflammaTon. Hence the risk benefit raTo of the intervenTon needs to be analysed before prescribing a red blood cell transfusion to treat anaemia in people with chronic kidney disease.

The potenTal risks associated with blood transfusion include transfusion reacTons, iron overload and transfusion acquired infecTons. In the presence of severe chronic anaemia, transfusion may lead to congesTve cardiac failure, parTcularly in the elderly. A review of transfusion outcome in people with acute coronary artery syndromes revealed a greater mortality rate in transfusion recipients (1) . Another review suggested that treatment of mild to moderate anaemia resulted in increased mortality (2) . Also transplant recipient sensiTsaTon may occur following transfusion resulTng in longer transplant register waiTng Tmes and difficulty in finding a cross match negaTve donor. A study from Ireland looking at causes of sensiTsaTon of potenTal allograV recipients showed that the level of sensiTsaTon increased with the number of units of blood transfused and also demonstrated a direct relaTonship between degree of sensiTsaTon and waiTng Tme for transplantaTon (3) . Blood transfusions can induce anTbodies to histocompaTbility leukocyte anTgens that can reduce the success of kidney transplantaTon; thus transfusions generally should be avoided in paTents awaiTng a kidney transplant (2) .

The use of ESAs can greatly reduce the need for red blood cell transfusions in people with anaemia of CKD when target Hb concentraTons are achieved and maintained (4-5) . Since the introducTon of ESAs and reducTon in rouTne blood transfusion in people with anaemia and CKD, sensiTsaTon has markedly reduced (6) . With the advent of new immunosuppressant regimens aVer 1995, the use of pre-transplantaTon transfusion have been rendered largely obsolete (7) . The K-DOQI anaemia guideline recommends that no single Hb concentraTon jusTfies or requires transfusion and the target Hb recommended for chronic anaemia management should not serve as a transfusion trigger (8) . NICE agrees that there are clinical reasons to minimise blood transfusion in anaemia of CKD and if blood transfusion is essenTal the relevant haematology guidelines should be followed (e.g. the BriTsh Society for Haematology (BSH) guidelines hXps://b-s-h.org.uk/guidelines/guidelines) (8) . In hospitalised paTents who are haemodynamically stable, the need for transfusion is directed by symptoms and the Hb values. Whilst one study suggested that a value in CKD paTents of &lt;70 g/L or &lt;80 g/L in post-operaTve surgical paTents or pre-exisTng cardiac disease should prompt transfusion , transfusion should be based on paTent assessment and policies set by local transfusion guidelines (9) .

<!-- image -->

<!-- image -->

HepaTTs E virus (HEV) is a RNA virus and has 4 genotypes: the one commonly found in the UK is genotype 3. The most common route of infecTon in the UK is from eaTng raw or undercooked meat (parTcularly pork products) and shellfish; however, HEV can be transmiXed via blood transfusion and solid organ transplantaTon. Incidence of HEV in the UK has been increasing considerably since 2011. It was esTmated that as many as 100,000 persons may suffer acute infecTons each year and that less than 1 in 100 will have any illness at all. The majority of people who become infected with HEV have no symptoms and the infecTon clears completely within a couple of months. HEV may pose a risk of harm to immunocompromised paTents who may be unable to clear the infecTon, which may then become persistent, potenTally leading to chronic inflammaTon of the liver and cirrhosis. The UK Advisory CommiXee on the Safety of Blood, Tissues and Organs (SaBTO) recommends that immunocompromised /immunosuppressed paTents should receive HEV negaTve blood components (10) . All blood components in the UK are HEV tested. However, neither irradiated components nor CMV negaTve components (unless the paTent is a neonate or pregnant), are required.

## 6. Management of Peri-Transplant and Post-Transplant Anaemia (Guidelines 6.16.3.3)

## Guideline 6.1 - Peri-Transplant Anaemia:

We suggest that anaemia management should be opTmal pre-transplant in all people with CKD on the transplant wait-list, to minimise the risk of a post-transplant transfusion. (2B)

## Guideline 6.1.1

<!-- image -->

We suggest for people with CKD undergoing kidney transplantaTon, that recombinant EPO maybe conTnued, aVer a shared decision of the risks and benefits with the paTent, unTl endogenous EPO producTon is sufficient to maintain Hb concentraTons. For HIF-PHI therapy we make no recommendaTon unTl further data is available. (2C)

## Guideline 6.2 - Blood Transfusion:

We suggest for stable non-bleeding individuals who clinically require a red cell transfusion, using single unit transfusion, where possible. (2B)

## Rationale (Guideline 6.1-6.2)

Anaemia post-transplant is extremely common; the aeTology of which is mulT-factorial and influenced by the Tming post-transplant (Table 1) (1-3) .

Apart from the usual causes of anaemia due to CKD, kidney transplant recipients have various unique factors predisposing to anaemia.

Factors causing post-transplant anaemia (PTA):

1. GFR: anaemia in people with a kidney transplant reflects the degree of GFR similar to other people with CKD (3) .
2. Immunosuppressive medicaTons: Mycophenolate and azathioprine are myelosuppressive agents. Calcineurin inhibitors may cause anaemia by microangiopathic haemolysis (4-8) . OKT3 may also cause haemolyTc uraemic syndrome (HUS) (9-10) . Tacrolimus has also been associated with anaemia (11-13) . It may interfere with post erythropoieTn receptor binding intracellular signalling and may occasionally cause HUS (13-14) .
3. Angiotensin converTng enzyme (ACE) inhibitor and angiotensin receptor blocker (ARB) use: ACE inhibiTon has been linked with anaemia (3, 15) . Its pathogenesis is mulTfactorial and may include inhibiTon of endogenous EPO producTon, producTon of an erythropoiesis-inhibiTng protein (16) and inhibiTon of angiotensin II mediated sTmulaTon of erythrocyte precursors (17)

.

4. AnTbioTc use: various common anTbioTcs may cause anaemia including trimethoprimsulfamethoxazole.
5. InfecTons: viral infecTons such as cytomegalovirus and parvovirus B19 and anTviral agents such as ganciclovir may cause anaemia in people with a kidney transplant (18-19) .
6. Malignancy: malignancies including post-transplant lymphoproliferaTve disorder may result in anaemia.
7. HaemolyTc anaemia: haemolyTc anaemia may result from HUS or minor blood group incompaTbility in people with a kidney transplant (20-22) .
8. RejecTon episodes: Acute rejecTon may cause reduced endogenous EPO producTon (23) . Severe vascular rejecTon may cause microangiopathy.
9. Chronic inflammaTon: Failing kidney transplant causes a chronic inflammatory state resulTng in EPO hypo-responsiveness.

Early post-transplant anaemia (PTA) can be seen in the first 6 months following transplantaTon due to iron deficiency and the slow producTon of EPO from the new transplant graV (24) .

The prevalence of red cell transfusions early post-transplant is high, averaging 40% in reported series (25) .  Risk factors for peri-transplant transfusion include female gender, non-white ethnicity, Tme on dialysis pre-transplant and receiving a deceased donor transplant. The requirement of a peritransplant transfusion has been shown to be associated with poor allograV outcomes, through

<!-- image -->

mechanisms which are yet to be elucidated (25) . PaTent blood management, that is opTmal management of anaemia and preserving a paTent's own blood, are therefore important in the early post-transplant period (26-28) .  OpTmal anaemia management of the person with CKD prior to transplantaTon, as has been set out in this guideline, is important to minimise complicaTons of anaemia.

Although blood loss occurring via the transplant surgery itself will contribute to early post-transplant anaemia, transfusion usually occurs in the first two weeks post-transplant (25) .  Endogenous EPO is sustained from 28 days post-transplant in the seLng of primary graV funcTon (29) .  Studies have shown that in the early post-transplant period ESA (erythropoiesis sTmulaTng agent) is safe and effecTve, although the dose required may be higher than in the pre-transplant period (30, 31) .  Given the increasing use of marginal donor kidneys, associated with delayed graV funcTon and subopTmal funcTon, there may be a longer requirement for EPO.

When a red cell transfusion is required clinically post-transplant in the stable paTent, single unit transfusions should be uTlised where possible (3) .  Although red cell transfusions are recognised to be associated with HLA sensiTsaTon in the pre-transplant seLng, less data are available on the risk of de novo alloimmune responses post-transplant whilst receiving immunosuppression (25, 32) . There are data that the development of de novo transfusion specific anTbodies post-transplant can occur posttransplant; plus, addiTonal evidence of a potenTal link between the receipt of a red cell transfusion and de novo donor specific anTbodies (32) . LimiTng exposure to mulTple red cell donors is therefore advisable, if possible.

Late transplant anaemia occurs more than 6 months aVer transplantaTon and can be seen in up to 36% of paTents (33) . This is influenced by the funcTon of the transplant graV, but also a combinaTon of the factors listed above as well as iron deficiency.

AVer the early post-transplant period, the frequency of transfusions is markedly reduced. Anaemia management is more in line with that of CKD, however, there remain specific risk factors for anaemia which will be present and need consideraTon in terms of reversibility and management.

The JACK-II study looked at the associaTon between PTA and graV funcTon in 1307 kidney transplant recipients. This study found that 46.3% of the cohort had PTA at 7 years post transplantaTon, with low Hb levels associated to an increase in transplant graV failure (hazard raTo = 1.83, 95% CI: 1.662.02, P &lt; 0.001) (34) .

This associaTon was previously reported in the TransQol-HU Study, looking at a cohort of 938 kidney transplant recipients with a 4-year follow-up period. This study showed that mortality and graV failure were significantly higher in people who were anaemic vs those who were not anaemic. Mortality rates were reported at 18% vs 10% (p&lt;0.001) and graV failure rates were 17% vs 6% (p&lt;0.001) (35) .

GaVer-Gvili et al. assessed 266 people and found that 51.3% experienced early PTA and 36.6% experiences late PTA. The study found that there was higher mortality aVer 4 years in those who experienced early PTA, which processed to late PTA vs those who did not have anaemia and lower Hb was associated with increased mortality (hazard raTo [HR] 0.716, 95% confidence intervals [CI] 0.541-0.948, for every increment of 1 g/dL) and increase graV failure (HR 0.775, 95% CI: 0.619-0.969, for every increment of 1 g/dL) (36) .

<!-- image -->

Scechter et al. (33) also found similar results in their cohort of 1139 kidney transplant recipients. 36.2% of the cohort had PTA and 11.7% had severe anaemia (Hb &lt;11g/dL). Early PTA was associated with graV loss or mortality (hazard raTo (HR) 3.64, 95% CI: 2.34-5.66, p &lt; 0.001) but a weaker associaTon as seen with late PTA (HR 1.44, 95% CI: 0.97-2.13, 0.07). Severe anaemia was associated with an increase in all-cause mortality. It was also found that PTA secondary to AKI, acute rejecTon, infecTon is associated with a higher risk of death or graV loss (HR 9.32, 95% CI: 5.3-26.41, p &lt; 0.001 and HR 3.99, 95% CI: 2.01-7.95, p &lt; 0.001, respecTvely) (33) .

## Safety of ESA in transplant pa9ents

A few early retrospecTve studies suggested increased incidence of delayed graV funcTon in people on ESA prior to transplantaTon (37, 38) . However, Registry data has since shown reduced incidence of delayed graV funcTon despite increasing use of ESA. It has also been shown that ESA use prior to kidney transplantaTon does not reduce producTon of or response to endogenous EPO (39, 40) . Studies in the early post-transplant period did not show significant adverse events including delayed graV funcTon or hypertension (41, 42) . Studies in the late transplant period have shown increased incidence of hypertension (43, 44) . ESAs, most probably, do not accelerate rate of graV funcTon decline and one study suggested that correcTon of anaemia slowed the decline in allograV funcTon (45) .

In another prospecTve study that assessed the effect of correcTon of anaemia on progression of kidney Insufficiency in people with a kidney transplant; 128 people from 17 centres in France treated with ESA were randomised to full correcTon of anaemia (haemoglobin values130-150 g/l, n =63) versus parTal correcTon of anaemia (Hb value 105-115 g/l, n =62).This study found that in the group of paTents with a haemoglobin level close to normal (~130 g/L), the rate of decline of kidney funcTon was lower compared with the group of control parTcipants, and the number of people reaching end-stage kidney disease and the number of graV failures was lower in this treatment group compared with the control group, suggesTng that correcTng anaemia in people with a kidney transplant reduces the rate of decrease of kidney funcTon and reduces the number of graVs lost (46) .

A similar study from Japan compared the effect of maintaining higher Hb levels (125-135 g/L, n=64) using ESAs with maintaining Hb at 105-115 g/L (n=63). This study found that those people in the lower Hb target group (mean levels 115 ± 12 g/L) had significantly higher rate of eGFR decline at 12, 18, 24 and 36 (p=0.02) months, compared to those in the higher Hb target group. Similar levels of adverse events were seen in both groups (47) .

A further single centre study showed no change in the rate of decline in allograV funcTon over 2 years with epoeTn beta therapy (target Hb 115-135 g/L). However, the study did show significant increase in the vitality and mental health domains of the medical outcomes short form health survey (48) .

## Efficacy of ESA in people with a kidney transplant

Studies in the early post-transplant period have shown that ESA is effecTve in these people, although the dose required may be higher than in pre-transplant period (41, 42) . Similarly, studies in late posttransplant period have shown efficacy of ESA in these people (43, 44, 49, 50) .

In the Neo-PDGF study, high dose epoeTn beta (30,000 IU) was administered during the first 2 weeks around kidney transplantaTon. Doses were given just before surgery, 12-24 h aVer transplantaTon, at

<!-- image -->

7 days and at 14 days post transplantaTon. When compared to the group not receiving any ESA, there was found to be significantly increased haemoglobin levels at 1 month aVer transplantaTon (mean Hb 111 g/L vs 105 g/L p=0.038. However, there was no significant difference in the incidence of delayed graV funcTon (32% vs 38.5%), the incidence of slow graV funcTon (26 vs 25%) or eGFR (42,7 vs 44.3 ml/min/1.73m 2 ) of epoeTn beta versus control (51) .

## Guideline 6.3 - Post-Transplant Anaemia:

We suggest that consideraTon is given to idenTfying (and correcTng) reversible transplant specific causes of anaemia. (2B)

## Guideline 6.3.1

We recommend that the treatment guidelines for anaemia in kidney transplant recipients should be similar to those for people with CKD not receiving dialysis (NDD-CKD). (1B)

## Guideline 6.3.2

We suggest for stable non-bleeding individuals who clinically require a red cell transfusion, using single unit transfusion, where possible. (2B)

## Guideline 6.3.3

We suggest the use of HIF-PHI therapy in transplant recipients should be as in people with NDD-CKD. (2B)

## Rationale

Data on the use of HIF-PHI therapy in kidney transplant recipients are limited to small case series and non-randomised real-world evidence.  In the randomised controlled trials transplant paTents were excluded in the majority and hence data is limited to draw any conclusions.  However, in resports thus far, the use of HIF-PHI therapy appears to be effecTve in raising Hb levels to the current recommended target range in the short term with limited documented adverse effects (1-2) . The numbers of people treated are too small to currently make any specific recommendaTons of use of HIF-PHI in people with a kidney transaplant (1-2) . In the recent published studies from Asia, there were approximately 73 paTents in the 4 studies from Asia (3-6) . A growing experience exists in Europe and the UK (personal communicaTon). A specific consideraTon in the transplant populaTon, would be the addiTve theoreTcal risk of malignancy in the long term.

## Special Populations (Guidelines 7.1-7.3)

## Guideline 7.1 - Malignancy:

We suggest treaTng symptomaTc anaemia in people with CKD and cancer in those with a Hb &lt;100 g/L. (2B)

## Rationale

- Iron replacement may be used to improve haemoglobin response, and reduce RBC transfusions, for  paTents  receiving  ESA  with  or  without  iron  deficiency.  If  paTents  are  receiving  cardiotoxic chemotherapy, iron must NOT be administered on the same day.
- ESAs may be offered to paTents, on acTve treatment for cancer (chemotherapy), whose cancer treatment is not curaTve.
- If a person has an acTve cancer, NOT having chemotherapy treatment, DO NOT give ESA's.
- If a person had a previous cancer which is deemed cured, then there are no concerns for giving ESA's.

## Guideline 7.2 - Haematological Disease:

We suggest ESA therapy be considered in people with myelodysplasTc syndrome (MDS), myeloma and others with CKD and haematological disease. We make no recommendaTon on HIF-PHI therapy. (not graded)

<!-- image -->

## Rationale

ESA should be considered in people with MDS with low risk MDS and symptomaTc anaemia and Hb &lt;100g/L.  If  a  person  with  MDS  does  not  respond  to  ESA  therapy  within  8-12  weeks  then Haematologists should consider commencing G-CSF.

## Guideline 7.3 - Pregnancy:

We recommend that pregnant and post-partum women with CKD are given parenteral iron, if indicated, from the second trimester onwards and if the benefit is judged to outweigh the potenTal risk for both the mother and the foetus according to SmPC guidance. (1C)

## Guideline 7.3.1

We recommend that ESA therapy during pregnancy may be conTnued unless there is a major contraindicaTon (e.g., hypertension or thrombosis risks). (2C)

## Rationale

During pregnancy, plasma volumes become higher than the increase in red blood cell mass, which leads to lower observed haemoglobin levels. The lower reference limit for haemoglobin concentraTons in pregnancy is 110 g/L in the first trimester, 105 g/L in the second trimester and 100 g/L in the post-partum period (1, 2), with values &lt;85 g/L associated with an esTmated 62% increase in the risk of low birth weight (&lt;2,500 g) and a 72% increase in the risk of preterm delivery before 37 weeks, across ethnic groups (3) . There are no data to guide the opTmum target haemoglobin for women with CKD in pregnancy (4) .

The most common cause of anaemia in pregnancy is iron deficiency, which is esTmated to affect greater than 40% of pregnancies (5) . Iron deficiency in pregnancy can contribute to maternal mortality, increased maternal morbidity, impaired infant development and potenTal negaTve effects on pregnancy outcomes (low birth weight, placental abrupTon, peripartum blood loss) (2) .

Pregnant women are normally offered oral iron in the first instance if Hb is below target value, however IV iron is offered if they cannot tolerate oral iron or the Hb has not responded to oral therapy (2, 6) . Parenteral iron is considered safe in pregnancy and breasGLYPH&lt;145&gt;eeding (7-10) , although there is a paucity of safety data on exposure in the first trimester. It is important to review the SmPC for each pregnant person as foetal bradycardia may occur following administraTon of parenteral irons. It is usually transient and a consequence of a hypersensiTvity reacTon in the mother. The unborn baby should be carefully monitored during intravenous administraTon of parenteral irons to pregnant women.

Recent reviews have commented that ESA therapy are considered safe in pregnancy, as they are large molecules which is unlikely to cross the placenta (11, 12) . They are also considered safe during lactaTon. OVen twice to three Tmes the use dose is required during pregnancy to achieve the desired haemoglobin target.

These recommendaTons are consistent with Guideline 4.6.1 of the UKKA pregnancy in CKD guidelines which states that 'We recommend pregnant women with CKD are given parenteral iron if indicated'.
