## New Therapies for Consideration in the future

Several new areas of drug development are ongoing including inhibitors of hepcidin producTon or acTon, anT-interleukin-6 specific anTbodies (ZilTvekimab), anT-inflammatory biologicals, and acTvin receptor ligand traps (1) .

A phase 1/2, placebo-controlled trial examined the effects of intravenous zilTvekimab every 2 weeks for 12 weeks vs placebo, in 61 people on haemodialy sis with a single nucleoTde polymorphism of the TMPRSS6 gene (putaTvely increases suscepTbility to IL-6-mediated inflammatory effects). ZilTvekimab led to a significantly greater reducTon of high-sensiTvity C-reacTve protein and ESA usage and ESA resistance index. There was also an increased serum iron, total iron binding capacity, transferrin saturaTon, and serum albumin (2) . Future trials may define its benefit.

PRS080#022, a PEGylated (polyethylene glycol bound) anTcalin protein, antagonizes hepcidin with picomolar affinity (3) . Two first-in-human randomised, double-blind phase 1 trials of PRS-080#22; a single intravenous infusion of placebo or PRS-080#22 was administered to 48 healthy volunteers (phase 1a) and 24 paTents on hemodialysis (phase 1b) at different doses (0.08-16 mg/kg for the phase 1a study and 2-8mg/kg for the phase 1b study) in successive dosing cohorts. This led to a dose dependent reducTon in serum hepcidin levels, mobilisaTon of serum iron with increased serum iron concentraTon and transferrin saturaTon. There was impact on ferriTn or haemoglobin. This drug was safe and effecTve but further clinical studies are needed.

Neutralising hepcidin with a monoclonal anTbody (mAb) may prevent ferroporTn internalizaTon, restore iron efflux from cells, and allow transferrin-mediated iron transport to the bone marrow. A mulT-centre, phase 1 study evaluated the safety, pharmacokineTcs (PK), pharmacodynamics (PD), and efficacy of a fully humanized mAb (LY2787106) with high affinity for hepcidin in cancer paTents with anaemia (4) . Thirty-three paTents with hepcidin levels ≥5 ng/mL received LY2787106 either every 3 weeks (19 paTents, dose range 0.3-10 mg/kg) (part A) or weekly (14 paTents, dose 10 mg/kg) (part B). Consistent dose-dependent increases in serum iron, and transferrin saturaTon were seen at the 3 and 10 mg/kg dose levels, typically peaking within 24 h aVer LY2787106 administraTon and returning to baseline by day 8. LY2787106 resulted in transient iron mobilizaTon, thus supporTng the role of hepcidin in iron regulaTon (4) .

Haemojuvelin (HJV), encoded by the HEF2 gene, a glycosylphosphaTdylinositol-linked membraneassociated protein (expressed in liver, skeletal muscle and heart), binds to bone morphogenic proteins (BMPs) and enhances their effecTveness to acTvate the BMP-SMAD (small mothers against decapentaplegic) signalling pathway to sTmulate hepcidin transcripTon in hepatocytes (5, 6) .

Currently animal data is available suggesTng that BMP6 is a key endogenous regulator of hepcidin (7, 8) . DeleTons in genes encoding the ligand BMP6, the BMP coreceptor HJV, the BMP Type I receptors ALK2 and ALK3, or the intracellular signalling molecule SMAD4 (9-11) , all independently resulted in inappropriately suppressed hepcidin expression and Tssue iron overload. Recent studies examining te use of anT-haemojuvelin anTbodies have demonstrated improvements in anaemia (12) .  Preclinical models have clearly shown that anT-HJV anTbodies have the potenTal to modulate hepcidin and improve Hb in inflammatory and non-inflammatory condiTons (13) . A recent phase 1b/2a multicenter, open-label clinical trial of DISC-0974, a monoclonal anTbody (mAB) targeTng hemojuvelin, in

<!-- image -->

<!-- image -->

people with myelofibrosis or chronic kidney disease is ongoing (NCT05320198). Initial findings have shown a dose-dependent decreases in hepcidin across all treated patients. These reductions in hepcidin corresponded to dose-dependent increases in serum iron.

Aptamers are syntheTc single-stranded oligonucleoTdes that bind ligands with high affinity, represenTng a novel class of oligonucleoTde structures suitable for blocking purposes (14) . Spiegelmers are mirror image aptamers. Spiegelmers are aXracTve therapeuTc agents and NOX-H94 is a polyethylene glycol conjugated (PEGylated) spiegelmer that targets and binds to human hepcidin (15) . The first human trial is currently underway (15, 16) .

A study characterizing the molecular mechanism of ferroporTn disease with parenchymal iron overload and resistance to hepcidin, the thiol form of Cys326 in ferroporTn was found to be essenTal in hepcidin-ferroporTn interacTon. An anTferroporTn mAb which binds to the extracellular loop of ferroporTn, can block the hepcidin-ferroporTn interacTon, while maintaining ferroporTn funcTon (17) . This strategy would promote ferroporTn acTvity and allow conTnuous iron influx, potenTally prevenTng iron-restricTve erythropoiesis due to hepcidin excess. Human studies are awaited.

SGLT2 inhibitors have been shown to increase Hb in people with chronic kidney disease and/ or heart failure (18-23) . The increased Hb in people treated with SGLT2 inhibitors seems to be independent of diureTc use or as a result of intravascular volume depleTon causing haemoconcentraTon (24, 25) . Data suggest that SGLT2 inhibitor administraTon is associated with transient increases in serum EPO concentraTons (30%-40%), an increase in reTculocyte counts or haematocrit levels (26) and a decrease in ferriTn and hepcidin, indicaTng erythropoieTc sTmulaTon (2730) . However, more informaTon is needed to beXer understand the mechanisms of acTon underlying these effects and their clinical relevance.

## Lay Summary

Anaemia is a commonly diagnosed complicaTon among people suffering with chronic kidney disease. If leV untreated, it may affect quality of life. There are several causes for anaemia in this populaTon. As the kidney funcTon deteriorates, together with medicaTons and dietary restricTons, people with chronic kidney disease may develop iron deficiency, resulTng in reducTon of iron supply to the bone marrow (which is the body organ responsible for the producTon of different blood elements). People with chronic kidney disease may not be able to use their own body's iron stores well and hence, many people, parTcularly those receiving haemodialysis, may need addiTonal iron treatment, usually provided by injecTon.

With further weakening of kidney funcTon, people with chronic kidney disease may need addiTonal treatment with a substance called erythropoieTn which drives the bone marrow to produce its own blood. This substance, which is naturally produced by the kidneys, becomes relaTvely deficient in people with chronic kidney disease. These people will eventually need treatment with erythropoieTn or similar products that are given by injecTon.

Over the last few years, several iron and erythropoieTn products (syntheTc products and those sTmulaTng the body's own erythropoieTn) have been licensed for treaTng anaemia in people with chronic kidney disease. In addiTon, several publicaTons discussed the benefits of each treatment and possible risks associated with long term treatment. The current guidelines provide advice to health care professionals on how to screen people with chronic kidney disease for anaemia, which people to invesTgate for other causes of anaemia, when and how to treat people with different medicaTons, how to ensure safe prescribing and monitoring of treatment and how to diagnose and manage complicaTons associated with anaemia and the drugs used for its treatment.

## Patient Information leaflet for Anaemia of Chronic Kidney Disease (CKD)

## Pa9ent Informa9on Sheet:

One of the kidney's main jobs is to help make red blood cells. The kidneys make a hormone called erythropoieTn that is a signal for the body to make red blood cells.

If your kidneys don't work as well as they should, they make less erythropoieTn, so fewer red blood cells are made. Iron levels in the body are usually low in people with advanced kidney disease.

The medical term for this condiTon is Anaemia of Chronic Kidney Disease. It means a low amount of red blood cells in the body caused by long-term kidney disease.

People with anaemia oVen feel Tred and short of breath, but there can be other symptoms.

We treat anaemia with medicines including iron tablets and iron injecTons. We also use ESA (also called EPO) injecTons and other medicaTon (HIF-PHI) that help make erythropoieTn and help make iron work beXer in the body.

## Guideline 1 - Evalua9on of anaemia - Baseline inves9ga9ons

Subjects with chronic kidney (CKD) disease who develop anaemia need iniTal invesTgaTons in the form of blood tests to:

1. Assess the severity of anaemia
2. Assess the cause of anaemia: some subjects with CKD could develop anaemia due to causes other than kidney disease: such as bleeding, iron &amp; vitamin deficiency or many other causes. It is important to perform all the required tests before concluding that the anaemia is due to CKD.

## Guideline 2.1 - Treatment of Anaemia with Iron therapy:

Iron is one of the building blocks of red cell formaTon by the body. Therefore correcTon of iron deficiency is an important iniTal step in the treatment of anaemia of CKD.

## Guideline 2.2 - Treatment of Anaemia with Iron Therapy - Ini9a9on of ESA/HIF-PHI and Iron Status:

- There are several specific blood tests arranged regularly through the kidney clinic to ensure the adequacy of iron stores in the body of subjects with CKD, and to assess how easily these iron stores are uTlized by the body to correct anaemia.
- Many subjects could have their anaemia corrected by receiving iron therapy only.
- Therefore, it is important to ensure that people with CKD have adequate iron stores prior to considering the iniTaTon of other drugs like ESA or HIF-PHI.

## Guideline 2.3 - Treatment of Anaemia with Iron therapy - Route of Administra9on

Oral iron tablets are effecTve in correcTng iron deficiency in the majority of CKD subjects with anaemia.

## Intravenous iron:

- Intravenous iron (Iron injecTon given by a drip into the vein, IV iron), is usually needed when iron tablets are not effecTve or not tolerated by subjects with CKD.
- IV iron is widely used to correct iron deficiency among people receiving haemodialysis.
- IV iron is also given rouTnely to people before starTng on ESA of HIF-PHI therapy to make this treatment more effecTve.

<!-- image -->

- There are different preparaTons of IV iron. Some preparaTons are given in a small dose at frequent intervals, others are given as large dose infused over few hours.
- People receiving large dose iron infusions will not require frequent treatment unlike those receiving small dose preparaTons.
- Small dose, more frequent IV iron is suitable for people receiving In Centre Haemodialysis, while large dose, less frequent preparaTons are suitable for people with CKD not receiving dialysis or those receiving peritoneal dialysis.

## Guideline 2.4 - Treatment of Anaemia with Iron therapy - safety and Upper limit for iron therapy.

- Iron treatment, parTcularly IV iron, is not without risks, they are administered guided by the value of iron level in the blood.
- There are several tests available to assess iron level in the blood and body.
- The commonest test is 'serum FerriTn'.
- Usually the aim of treatment with iron is to achieve serum ferriTn level between 500-700 mcg/L.
- IV iron treatment should be withheld in the presence of any acTve infecTon. It should also be avoided in people with acTve HepaTTs C virus infecTon, in some people with chronic liver disease and those with hereditary liver condiTons.
- To ensure safe treatment with IV iron, people will need regular blood tests (every 1-3 months) to monitor iron level and the response to treatment.

## Guideline 3. Treatment with Erythropoiesis S9mula9ng Agents

- The aim of treatment of CKD with ESA preparaTons is to improve quality of life and reduce/avoid the need for blood transfusion.
- Like intravenous iron, ESA therapy is not without risks. The doctor will discuss the risk benefit raTo with the receipient/their carer before iniTaTng this treatment.
- There are different ESA preparaTons; ome are short acTng preparaTons, needing to be administered more than once weekly, other are longer acTng preparaTons given less frequently (weekly or monthly).
- ESA preparaTons are given by injecTon: either into the vein (IV) or under the skin (subcutaneous, SC). Although SC injecTon allows lower dose of the drug to produce the desired effect, the choice and route of ESA administraTon is determined by several factors, such as the availability of the preparaTon in the local hospital, the availability of access for IV administraTon and choice of the recipient.
- It will be helpful if the recipient/carer discuss the need for the drug (a shared decision), available opTons and risk benefit raTo of treatment with the health care professional prior to starTng therapy.
- OvercorrecTon of anaemia using ESA therapy could be harmful with adverse effects on the heart and blood pressure. Therefore, treatment with ESA aims to achieve and maintain haemoglobin level between 100 and 120 g/L in adults, and slightly lower in children younger than 2 years old.
- To achieve and maintain this target Hb range, people receiving ESA therapy will need regular blood tesTng to assess the response to treatment and allow adjustment of ESA dose according to Hb level.
- ESA therapy is not without risks. Therefore, ESA therapy is a shared decision (balancing benefits and risks) and should be used cauTously/ avoided in people with history of stroke, or in people with certain malignancies.
- Blood pressure needs to be monitored regularly in people receiving ESA, and if needed, blood pressure should be controlled with medicaTons according to local guidelines.
- Very rarely, people may become immune against ESA therapy, as a result they become severely anaemic and do not respond to any ESA drugs. People who develop this condiTon should stop

<!-- image -->

receiving ESA therapy. They may require regular blood transfusions unTl they are treated to regain their ability to respond to ESA therapy.

## Guideline 4 - Treatment of Anaemia - HIF-PHI Agents

HIF-PHI are newly introduced medicaTons that are now licensed to treat anaemia in CKD subjects. They are administered in the form of oral tablets. They act on a system in the body that is acTvated when the level of oxygen in the blood is low. When acTvated, the body produces more red blood cells. This could be partly through the producTon of more erythropoieTn and partly through beXer use of available iron for blood cell producTon.

HIF-PHI tablets acTvate this system, regardless of the oxygen level in the blood. As a result, more red blood cells are produced and anaemia is corrected.

- Like ESA, HIF-PHI treatment is dependent on the amount of iron in the body. Therefore, it is important to treat iron deficiency in any person before prescribing HIF-PHI therapy.
- Treatment with HIF-PHI should conTnue if and when the person is unwell for any other reason. During any illness, like surgery or chest infecTon, anaemia in subjects with CKD could get worse, so treatment of anaemia should conTnue during acute illness unless there is a reason to stop the drug.
- OvercorrecTon of anaemia using HIF-PHI therapy could be harmful with adverse effects on the heart and blood pressure. Therefore, treatment with HIF-PHI therapy aims to achieve and maintain haemoglobin level between 100 and 120 g/L in adults.
- To achieve and maintain this target Hb range, people receiving HIF-PHI therapy will need regular blood tesTng to assess the response to treatment and allow adjustment of HIF-PHI dose according to Haemoglobin level.
- HIF-PHII therapy is not without risks. Therefore, this therapy should be used cauTously/ avoided in people with history of stroke or in people with certain malignancies.
- Blood pressure needs to be monitored regularly in people receiving HIF-PHI, and if needed, blood pressure should be controlled with medicaTons according to local guidelines.
- HIF-PHI therapy should be avoided in people with acTve malignancy, those with history of epilepsy, history of blood clots and subjects with autosomal dominant polycysTc kidney disease.

## Guideline 5: Blood Transfusion in people with Anaemia of CKD

- Whenever possible, people on the transplant waiTng list should avoid receiving blood transfusions to avoid/ reduce the risks of rejecTon when they receive their transplant.
- Blood transfusion, if needed, should be decided based on each person's requirements, rather than guided by the blood test results only.
- People with suppressed immune system, due to kidney transplant or due to any other medical condiTon, and those on the transplant waiTng list should receive blood that is negaTve for hepaTTs E virus.

## Guideline 6.0 Peri-transplant and Post Transplant Anaemia

- Kidney transplant recipients may develop anaemia due to a variety of causes.
- It is important to invesTgate and idenTfy the cause of anaemia prior to considering treatment with iron, ESA or HIF-PHI.
- If indicated, transplant recipients can be treated with iron, ESA or HIF-PHI as per the guidelines for people with CKD not on dialysis.

## Guideline 7.0 Special Popula9ons

- SymptomaTc people with CKD and cancer may be treated with Iron &amp; ESA therapy as per the guidelines for people with CKD not on dialysis.
- Pregnant subjects with CKD who develop anaemia may be treated with ESA therapy. They may be treated with IV iron from the second trimester.
- Pregnant subjects may conTnue ESA therapy during pregnancy.
